Worldly Consequences of False Creed
Lists worldly calamities for lacking tawhid and punishments for false creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the worldly calamities and punishments that result from a false or missing creed, as described by the author.

Core Idea
A corrupt creed leads to failure in every endeavor and societal punishment.

This passage vividly describes the tangible, real-world consequences of lacking true tawhid or holding a false creed. It uses striking examples to show that creed is not merely an abstract belief but has immediate effects in this life. Read carefully to see how the author connects creed to practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on calamities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the vivid examples: ships sinking, planes crashing, kingdoms falling.
The author uses repetition for emphasis.
2
Identify the two types of calamity
The passage distinguishes between calamity from lack of tawhid (failure in all deeds) and calamity from false creed (social and legal punishments).
Both are worldly consequences.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why the author puts such emphasis on worldly consequences. This shows that creed is not only about the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوبال الذي يلحق عديم التوحيد في أعماله كلها فسادها وعدم نجاحها ؛ فإن كانت باخرة غرقت أو سيارة تكسرت، أو طائرة سقطت وتحطمت، أو مملكة تمزقت أو مدرسة أو معملاً أو بلدة أو بيتاً خرب واضمحل، وأي وبال أكبر وأوضح من هذا ؟ هذا في مطلق عقيدة ومطلق توحيد. وأما وبال العقيدة الباطلة : فهو مقت العقلاء له وسقوطه من فضائل الأخلاق الإنسانية، وحرمانه من الحقوق المدنية، وتعرضه للعقوبات الدنيوية من إهانة وإفلاس وضرب وسجن وإعدام، وأي وبال أكبر من هذا؟ فالعقيدة الصحيحة هي ما كانت مطابقة للواقع ومؤيَّدة بالنقل والعقل، والعقيدة الباطلة الفاسدة هي ما لم تطابق الواقع ولم تؤيَّد بنقل ولا بعقل. وبالجملة : ففائدة العقيدة الفاسدة إن حصلت فهي الفوز ببعض لذاته العاجلة النفسانية الشهوانية الحيوانية الوقتية ويعقبها ندامة وعقوبة في الدنيا والآخرة. وفائدة العقيدة الصحيحة السليمة هي الفوز في الدنيا بصحة الأعمال في الدنيا والثناء الجميل والأمن والثقة بصاحبها والغنى والراحة والطمأنينة، والنصر على الأعداء، وفي الآخرة الفوز برضا الرحمن ودخول الجنان(۱) فيعتبر علم العقيدة من أجل العلوم وأعلاها، وأوجبها وأولاها، ومن أهمها تحصيلاً وتبجيلاً ، فلذلك أعطى علماء السلف والخلف اهتماماً بالغاً بهذا العلم، وتعليمه لكافة شرائح المجتمع، فألفوا فيه المتون والشروح، والحواشي والمطولات والمختصرات ليناسب كافة المستويات.
And as for the calamity that befalls the one devoid of tawhid in all his deeds: their corruption and lack of success. If it is a ship, it sinks; or a car, it breaks down; or an airplane, it falls and crashes; or a kingdom, it is torn apart; or a school, a factory, a town, or a house, it is ruined and destroyed. And what calamity is greater and clearer than this? This applies to absolute creed and absolute tawhid. As for the calamity of false creed: it is the hatred of the intelligent for him, his fall from the virtues of human morals, his deprivation of civil rights, and his exposure to worldly punishments: humiliation, bankruptcy, beating, imprisonment, and execution. And what calamity is greater than this? So the correct creed is that which conforms to reality and is supported by transmitted evidence and reason. The false, corrupt creed is that which does not conform to reality and is not supported by transmitted evidence or reason. In summary: the benefit of false creed, if any, is attaining some of its immediate, psychic, lustful, animal, temporary pleasures, followed by regret and punishment in this world and the Hereafter. And the benefit of the correct, sound creed is success in this world through righteous deeds, good reputation, security and trust in its possessor, wealth, comfort, tranquility, and victory over enemies; and in the Hereafter, success through the pleasure of the Merciful and entry into Paradise. Thus the science of creed is considered one of the noblest, highest, most obligatory, and most deserving sciences, and among the most important to acquire and honor. Therefore, the early and later scholars gave great attention to this science and to teaching it to all segments of society. They authored texts, commentaries, glosses, lengthy works, and abridgments to suit all levels.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage contrasts the consequences of false creed with those of true creed to demonstrate the supreme importance of correct creed. It begins by describing the worldly failure that afflicts all deeds of someone lacking tawhid: ships sink, cars break, planes crash, kingdoms disintegrate, and buildings crumble—showing that no endeavor succeeds without proper foundation. Then it specifies punishments for false creed: social contempt, loss of moral standing, deprivation of civil rights, and penalties like humiliation, bankruptcy, beatings, imprisonment, and execution. After this, it defines correct creed as conformity to reality supported by transmitted and rational evidence, and false creed as its opposite. The passage then sums up: false creed yields only fleeting animal pleasures followed by regret and punishment in both worlds, while true creed brings worldly success (righteous deeds, good reputation, security, wealth, comfort, victory) and eternal reward (God's pleasure and Paradise). Consequently, the science of creed (‘ilm al-‘aqida) is declared among the noblest and most obligatory sciences, which is why scholars past and present devoted great effort to teaching it and authoring works at all levels.

Key Terms
الوبال
العقيدة الفاسدة
عديم التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creed only matters for the afterlife and has no worldly impact.
Correction: The passage explicitly shows that false creed leads to worldly failure and punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one worldly punishment for someone with a false creed?
A) Loss of wealth only
B) Hatred of the intelligent and loss of civil rights
C) Increase in worldly pleasures
D) Victory over enemies

Key Takeaway
False creed or lack of tawhid brings tangible worldly calamities and severe societal punishments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a major failure or setback in your life, do you ever pause to examine whether your relationship with tawhid is sound? What would it mean for you to treat correct creed as the foundation of every endeavor from now on?
?
The author says that false creed leads to being hated by the intelligent. Is there any belief you hold that, after honest reflection, you realize conflicts with pure tawhid and might cause respected people to lose trust in you? Are you willing to abandon that belief today?
?
This lesson lists worldly punishments for false creed: humiliation, bankruptcy, beating, imprisonment, execution. If you were ever threatened with such punishments for your faith, would your creed stand firm? What steps will you take now to strengthen it so that it can endure any trial?

Definition and Benefits of True Creed
Defines correct and false creed, then compares their benefits, concluding with the importance of creedal science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of correct creed, its comprehensive benefits in this world and the Hereafter, and why the science of creed is supreme.

Core Idea
True creed conforms to reality, is supported by revelation and reason, and brings success in both worlds.

After detailing the disasters of false creed, the author now defines what makes a creed correct or false, and then summarizes the profound benefits of holding true creed—benefits that span from worldly tranquility to eternal paradise. This passage also explains why scholars have devoted immense effort to teaching this science.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of correct creed
Read the Arabic and translation for the definition. Note the two criteria: conformity to reality and support from transmitted evidence (naql) and reason (aql).
This definition emphasizes that true creed is rational and authentic.
2
Contrast the benefits
Read the summary of benefits: false creed yields fleeting pleasures followed by regret and punishment; true creed brings worldly success and eternal reward.
Notice the comprehensive list for true creed.
3
Understand the importance
Read the final part where the author declares creedal science as the noblest and notes the scholars' efforts.
This explains why we study this subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوبال الذي يلحق عديم التوحيد في أعماله كلها فسادها وعدم نجاحها ؛ فإن كانت باخرة غرقت أو سيارة تكسرت، أو طائرة سقطت وتحطمت، أو مملكة تمزقت أو مدرسة أو معملاً أو بلدة أو بيتاً خرب واضمحل، وأي وبال أكبر وأوضح من هذا ؟ هذا في مطلق عقيدة ومطلق توحيد. وأما وبال العقيدة الباطلة : فهو مقت العقلاء له وسقوطه من فضائل الأخلاق الإنسانية، وحرمانه من الحقوق المدنية، وتعرضه للعقوبات الدنيوية من إهانة وإفلاس وضرب وسجن وإعدام، وأي وبال أكبر من هذا؟ فالعقيدة الصحيحة هي ما كانت مطابقة للواقع ومؤيَّدة بالنقل والعقل، والعقيدة الباطلة الفاسدة هي ما لم تطابق الواقع ولم تؤيَّد بنقل ولا بعقل. وبالجملة : ففائدة العقيدة الفاسدة إن حصلت فهي الفوز ببعض لذاته العاجلة النفسانية الشهوانية الحيوانية الوقتية ويعقبها ندامة وعقوبة في الدنيا والآخرة. وفائدة العقيدة الصحيحة السليمة هي الفوز في الدنيا بصحة الأعمال في الدنيا والثناء الجميل والأمن والثقة بصاحبها والغنى والراحة والطمأنينة، والنصر على الأعداء، وفي الآخرة الفوز برضا الرحمن ودخول الجنان(۱) فيعتبر علم العقيدة من أجل العلوم وأعلاها، وأوجبها وأولاها، ومن أهمها تحصيلاً وتبجيلاً ، فلذلك أعطى علماء السلف والخلف اهتماماً بالغاً بهذا العلم، وتعليمه لكافة شرائح المجتمع، فألفوا فيه المتون والشروح، والحواشي والمطولات والمختصرات ليناسب كافة المستويات.
And as for the calamity that befalls the one devoid of tawhid in all his deeds: their corruption and lack of success. If it is a ship, it sinks; or a car, it breaks down; or an airplane, it falls and crashes; or a kingdom, it is torn apart; or a school, a factory, a town, or a house, it is ruined and destroyed. And what calamity is greater and clearer than this? This applies to absolute creed and absolute tawhid. As for the calamity of false creed: it is the hatred of the intelligent for him, his fall from the virtues of human morals, his deprivation of civil rights, and his exposure to worldly punishments: humiliation, bankruptcy, beating, imprisonment, and execution. And what calamity is greater than this? So the correct creed is that which conforms to reality and is supported by transmitted evidence and reason. The false, corrupt creed is that which does not conform to reality and is not supported by transmitted evidence or reason. In summary: the benefit of false creed, if any, is attaining some of its immediate, psychic, lustful, animal, temporary pleasures, followed by regret and punishment in this world and the Hereafter. And the benefit of the correct, sound creed is success in this world through righteous deeds, good reputation, security and trust in its possessor, wealth, comfort, tranquility, and victory over enemies; and in the Hereafter, success through the pleasure of the Merciful and entry into Paradise. Thus the science of creed is considered one of the noblest, highest, most obligatory, and most deserving sciences, and among the most important to acquire and honor. Commentary
This passage contrasts the consequences of false creed with those of true creed to demonstrate the supreme importance of correct creed. It begins by describing the worldly failure that afflicts all deeds of someone lacking tawhid: ships sink, cars break, planes crash, kingdoms disintegrate, and buildings crumble—showing that no endeavor succeeds without proper foundation. Then it specifies punishments for false creed: social contempt, loss of moral standing, deprivation of civil rights, and penalties like humiliation, bankruptcy, beatings, imprisonment, and execution. After this, it defines correct creed as conformity to reality supported by transmitted and rational evidence, and false creed as its opposite. The passage then sums up: false creed yields only fleeting animal pleasures followed by regret and punishment in both worlds, while true creed brings worldly success (righteous deeds, good reputation, security, wealth, comfort, victory) and eternal reward (God's pleasure and Paradise). Consequently, the science of creed (‘ilm al-‘aqida) is declared among the noblest and most obligatory sciences, which is why scholars past and present devoted great effort to teaching it and authoring works at all levels.

Misconception & Correction

?

Faith and Disbelief

Taqlid in Creed: Definition and Ruling
Al-Sawi defines taqlid and explains why it does not suffice for creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taqlid in creed, why it does not suffice, and the distinction between blind imitation and following an infallible guide.

Core Idea
Taqlid is blind imitation without proof; true knowledge comes from evidence, not following others.

This lesson examines the concept of taqlid in Islamic creed. Al-Sawi defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without proof, and clarifies that such imitation does not constitute valid belief. He further distinguishes it from following an infallible (ma'sum), which yields necessary knowledge. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the proper foundation of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of taqlid
Read the first line carefully: 'taqlid is taking the statement of another without proof.' Note the two parts: 'statement of another' and 'without proof.'
The commentary clarifies that 'another' means non-infallible.
2
Understand the implied question
The passage is answering whether imitation suffices in creed. The answer is no, because taqlid lacks proof.
The implied question connects this section to the previous discussion of knowledge.
3
Distinguish between taqlid and following an infallible
Notice that hearing an infallible (like a prophet) during his lifetime is not taqlid; it is knowledge and yields necessary certainty.
This is a key exception: the source of knowledge matters.
4
Reflect on the nature of firm belief in taqlid
The final line suggests that if the imitator reverses his position, his belief would not be firm. This shows that taqlid lacks genuine stability.
Even though taqlid involves 'firm belief', it is not grounded in evidence, so it is unstable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقليد في العقائد وكلام العلماء فيه
وأمَّا التّقليد، وهو الأخذ بقول الغير من غير حجّة؛ أي: الاعتقاد الجازم المتمسك فيه بمجرد قول الغير،
قوله : (وأمَّا التّقليد): جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر، حاصله قد ذكرت المعرفة، وما يتعلَّق بها فهل يكتفي بالتّقليد أو لا؟ فأجاب بما ذكر .
قوله : (بقول الغير ) أي : وهو غير معصوم ، وأمَّا سماع المعصوم في حال حياته؛ فلا يسمى تقليداً، بل هو معرفة وتحقيق، فيفيد العلم الضَّروري.
قوله : (أي: الاعتقاد الجازم) : أي : بحيث لو رجع مقلّده لا مقلّده لا يرجع
Imitation in the creeds and the speech of the scholars concerning it.
As for imitation (taqlid), it is taking the statement of another without proof; i.e., the firm belief that adheres merely to the statement of another.
His statement: (As for imitation): a response to an implied question, whose summary is: I have mentioned knowledge and what pertains to it, so does imitation suffice or not? So he answered as mentioned.
His statement: (statement of another) i.e., and he is not infallible. As for hearing an infallible during his lifetime, it is not called imitation, but rather it is knowledge and verification, and it yields necessary knowledge.
His statement: (i.e., the firm belief): i.e., such that if his follower returned... his follower does not return [؟].
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of taqlid (imitation) in matters of creed. The author begins by defining taqlid: it is accepting the statement of another (al-ghayr) without evidence (ḥujja). This definition emphasizes that taqlid involves firm belief (iʿtiqād jāzim) but the basis is merely another person's statement, not proof. The author then notes that this section is an answer to an implied question: since knowledge has been discussed, does imitation suffice? The answer is implied in the definition—it does not. Next, the author clarifies that 'statement of another' refers to someone who is not infallible (ghayr maʿṣūm). Hearing an infallible person (maʿṣūm) like a prophet during his lifetime is not called taqlid; it is knowledge and verification (maʿrifa wa taḥqīq) that yields necessary knowledge. The final line is garbled, but it attempts to explain that pure imitation is unstable—if the imitator reverses his position, his belief would not be firm. This underscores that taqlid lacks genuine certainty.

Key Terms
تقليد
اعتقاد جازم
حجة
معصوم
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that following any authority in creed is taqlid, including following a living prophet.
Correction: Following a living infallible (like a prophet) is based on direct evidence and yields necessary knowledge; it is not blind imitation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does following an infallible (maʿṣūm) become knowledge rather than imitation?
A) When he is a scholar
B) When he is heard during his lifetime
C) When one writes down his words
D) Never, because all following is imitation

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in creed is unacceptable because it lacks proof; only following a living infallible (prophet) provides necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any belief about Allah simply because you were raised with it, without ever personally seeking evidence? If so, which one are you most afraid to question? What would it take for you to examine it honestly today?
?
If you were asked to defend your belief in the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) without quoting any scholar or book, could you offer a clear proof from reason or direct experience? If not, does that mean your belief is actually taqlid? What step will you take this week to turn it into real certainty?
?
When a trusted teacher makes a claim about faith, do you accept it automatically or do you seek the underlying proof? Would you be willing to delay acceptance until you find evidence in the Quran or sound reason? What would that cost you?

The Sufficiency of Taqlid in Faith
Al-Sawi presents the two main opinions on taqlid in beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on whether taqlid suffices for the beliefs of faith, and which view is considered correct.

Core Idea
Taqlid in basic faith is valid, but reflection may still be required.

This passage introduces the core debate: does blind following (taqlid) suffice for the foundational beliefs of faith? The author presents two starkly opposing views: one that considers taqlid sufficient and another that brands the muqallid a disbeliever. The correct position is that taqlid is sufficient, though a further question arises about the obligation of reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the two opinions. Notice the immediate distinction between the two positions.
2
Identify the two opinions
Look for the two key 'قيل' statements: one saying taqlid suffices, the other saying it does not. Also note that the first opinion includes a sub-question about reflection.
The sub-question is part of the first opinion.
3
Note the correct view
The author explicitly states that the view that taqlid suffices is the correct one (وهو الصحيح). Recognize that this is the foundational position from which further discussion proceeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقليد في العقائد فقد اختلف فيه : فقيل : إنَّه يكفي في عقائد الإيمان وهو الصحيح، فإيمان المقلّد صحيح، وعليه فهل يجب النظر فيكون مع صحة إيمانه عاصياً بترك النَّظر الموصل للمعرفة – وهو الصَّحيح كما يُفهم من قولنا : (معرفة الله) - أو لا ، بل هو شرط كمال؟ وقيل: لا يكفي، فالمقلّد كافر . قوله : (فقد اختلف فيه أي : على ستّة أقوال ذكر الشَّرح منها خمسة، وترك سادساً، وهو عصيانه بترك النظر إن كان فيه أهليته، وإلا فلا يعصى، وهو المعتمد. قوله : (فإيمان المقلّد (صحيح) : أي خلافاً لأبي هاشم الجبائي القائل : بأنه كافر، وكل هذا بالنظر لما عند الله في الآخرة، وأما في الدُّنيا : فمن نطق بالشَّهادتين؛ فهو مسلم اتفاقاً تجري عليه أحكام المسلمين. ، ، وقولهم في تعريف الإيمان : هو حديث النَّفس التابع للمعرفة، محمول على الإيمان الكامل، وأما تعريف أصل الإيمان؛ فهو حديث النَّفس التّابع للاعتقاد الجازم فيشمل التّقليد . قوله : ( وعليه) أي: على القول بكفاية التقليد في عقائد الإيمان. قوله : (فهل يجب النظر ) : أي : وجوب الفروع، سواء كان فيه أهلية النظر أم لا، بناء على أنَّ كلَّ مكلَّف فيه أهلية الدليل الجملي. قوله : ( أو لا ) : أي : أو لا يجب النظر . قوله : (فالمقلّد كافر أي بناء على أنَّ المعرفة واجبة وجوب
Taqlid in beliefs: There is disagreement concerning it. It is said: It suffices for the beliefs of faith, and this is the correct view. So the faith of the muqallid is valid. Upon this view, is reflection (naẓar) obligatory such that despite the validity of his faith, he is sinful for abandoning reflection that leads to knowledge – and this is the correct view as understood from our statement 'knowledge of God' – or is it not obligatory, but rather a condition of perfection? And it is said: It does not suffice, so the muqallid is a disbeliever. His saying: 'There is disagreement concerning it' means: there are six opinions; the commentary (sharḥ) mentioned five of them and left out a sixth, which is that he is sinful for abandoning reflection if he has the capability for it; otherwise he is not sinful, and this is the relied-upon view. His saying: 'The faith of the muqallid is valid' means: contrary to Abū Hāshim al-Jubbā'ī who said he is a disbeliever. All of this is with regard to what is with Allah in the Hereafter. As for in this world, whoever pronounces the two testimonies is a Muslim by consensus, and the rulings of Muslims apply to him. Their statement in the definition of faith (īmān): it is an inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs) that follows knowledge is applied to perfect faith. As for the definition of the essence of faith, it is inner speech that follows firm conviction, which includes taqlid. His saying: 'and upon it' means: upon the view that taqlid suffices in beliefs. His saying: 'so is reflection obligatory' means: as a subsidiary obligation (wujūb al-furū‘), whether he has the capacity for reflection or not, based on the fact that every legally responsible person has the capacity for summary evidence. His saying: 'or not' means: or is reflection not obligatory. His saying: 'so the muqallid is a disbeliever' means: based on the view that knowledge is obligatory as an obligation...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether taqlid is sufficient for the beliefs of faith. The author states that the correct view is that taqlid suffices, so the faith of the muqallid is valid. However, a further question arises: is reflection (naẓar) obligatory upon him such that he is sinful for abandoning it, or is it merely a condition of perfection? The correct answer is that he is sinful if he has the capacity, because knowledge (ma'rifa) is required. Another opinion is that taqlid does not suffice and the muqallid is a disbeliever. The commentator notes that there are six opinions total, five mentioned in the sharh, and the sixth (the relied-upon one) is that he is sinful if capable of reflection, otherwise not. Then it distinguishes between the Hereafter and this world: in this world, pronouncing the shahada makes him a Muslim. The definition of iman is then clarified: perfect iman is inner speech following knowledge, while the essence of iman is inner speech following firm conviction, which includes taqlid. Finally, details on the obligation of reflection are given.

Key Terms
تقليد
إيمان
نظر
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If taqlid suffices, then reflection is not important or required at all.
Correction: The passage shows that even though taqlid suffices for the validity of faith, reflection may still be obligatory, and abandoning it can be sinful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding the sufficiency of taqlid in beliefs?
A) Taqlid suffices, and this is the correct view.
B) Taqlid does not suffice, and the muqallid is a disbeliever.
C) Taqlid suffices, but only if followed by reflection.
D) Taqlid is rejected altogether.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that taqlid suffices for the beliefs of faith, but the obligation of reflection remains a matter of discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content with merely following the beliefs you were raised with, or have you personally engaged in reflection (nazar) to attain certain knowledge of God? If reflection is obligatory, what does your current state say about your faith?
?
If you discovered that you have been sinful for not seeking deeper conviction, would you change your approach? What concrete step will you take this week to move from taqlid to ma'rifa?
?
Consider the weight of the opposite view: that a muqallid could be considered a disbeliever. Do you take your faith seriously enough to investigate whether you are merely imitating or truly convinced? What would it take for you to be certain?

The Obligation of Reflection and the Definition of Faith
Al-Sawi expands on the opinions regarding reflection and defines faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on whether reflection is obligatory upon the muqallid, the relied-upon view, and how faith is defined in relation to taqlid.

Core Idea
Reflection is obligatory only for those capable, and the essence of faith includes taqlid.

This passage builds on the previous discussion by detailing the various opinions on the obligation of reflection. The commentator reveals that there are six opinions, with the most reliable one stating that a muqallid is sinful only if he has the capacity for reflection. Additionally, the passage clarifies the distinction between worldly and Hereafter judgments and provides a precise definition of faith that accommodates taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Examine the six opinions
The passage mentions that there are six opinions on the obligation of reflection. The sharh mentioned five, but the sixth is given here. Read carefully to understand the sixth opinion.
2
Understand the relied-upon view
The sixth opinion is the relied-upon view: the muqallid is sinful for abandoning reflection only if he has the capacity (ahliyya) for it. Otherwise, he is not sinful.
3
Note the Hereafter vs world distinction
The passage clarifies that the rulings about faith and kufr (including the validity of taqlid) pertain to the Hereafter. In this world, anyone who pronounces the shahada is treated as a Muslim.
4
Grasp the definition of faith
The passage distinguishes between perfect faith (inner speech following knowledge) and the essence of faith (inner speech following firm conviction, which includes taqlid). This shows that taqlid can be part of the essence of faith.

The Six Opinions on Taqlid in Creed
Commentator enumerates six opinions on taqlid and gives the relied-upon view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six scholarly opinions on taqlid in creed and the relied-upon view that takes into account the individual's capacity for rational investigation.

Core Idea
Taqlid in creed is debated; the correct view depends on your ability to reason.

The commentator on al-Kharida presents a classification of opinions regarding whether blind acceptance (taqlid) suffices for true faith. The passage lays out six views, including the crucial distinction that a muqallid's sinfulness hinges on their intellectual capacity. This lesson unpacks these views and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the different opinions mentioned.
Notice the structure: first the dispute, then the two main families of opinion.
2
Distinguish the two main families
Identify that the first family holds taqlid suffices, while the second says it does not suffice.
The second family considers the muqallid a disbeliever.
3
Explore the sub-opinions under 'suffices'
Under the view that taqlid suffices, there is a further question whether nazar (rational investigation) is obligatory or just a condition of perfection.
The correct view according to the commentator is that nazar is obligatory.
4
Discover the sixth opinion
The commentator adds that the Sharh mentioned five opinions, leaving a sixth: sinfulness depends on capacity (ahliyya).
This is the relied-upon view.
5
Synthesize the key takeaway
Understand that the nuance of capacity resolves the apparent contradiction between the opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقليد في العقائد فقد اختلف فيه : فقيل : إنَّه يكفي في عقائد الإيمان وهو الصحيح، فإيمان المقلّد صحيح، وعليه فهل يجب النظر فيكون مع صحة إيمانه عاصياً بترك النَّظر الموصل للمعرفة – وهو الصَّحيح كما يُفهم من قولنا : (معرفة الله) - أو لا ، بل هو شرط كمال؟ وقيل: لا يكفي، فالمقلّد كافر . قوله : (فقد اختلف فيه أي : على ستّة أقوال ذكر الشَّرح منها خمسة، وترك سادساً، وهو عصيانه بترك النظر إن كان فيه أهليته، وإلا فلا يعصى، وهو المعتمد.
Taqlid in matters of creed – indeed it is disagreed upon. It is said: It suffices for the pillars of faith, and that is the correct view; so the faith of the muqallid is valid. Upon this, is nazar obligatory such that despite the validity of his faith he is disobedient by abandoning nazar which leads to knowledge – and this is the correct [view] as understood from our saying 'knowledge of Allah' – or not, rather it is a condition of perfection? And it is said: It does not suffice, so the muqallid is a disbeliever. His saying: (Indeed it is disagreed upon) meaning: upon six opinions. The Sharh mentioned five of them and left a sixth, which is that he is disobedient by abandoning nazar if he has capacity for it, and if not, he is not disobedient, and it is the relied-upon [view].
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the opinions on taqlid in creed. It begins by stating that taqlid in creed is disputed. Then it reports two initial opinions: the first says taqlid suffices for faith, but then asks whether nazar (rational investigation) is obligatory for the muqallid, noting that if it is, he would be sinful for abandoning it despite valid faith; the second opinion says taqlid does not suffice, so the muqallid is a disbeliever. The commentator then explains that there are actually six opinions, of which the Sharh mentioned five. He supplies the sixth, which is the relied-upon view: that the muqallid's sinfulness depends on his capacity for nazar – if he has the ability to reason, he is sinful for not doing so; otherwise he is not.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
نظر (nazar)
معرفة (ma'rifa)
مقلد (muqallid)
كافر (kafir)
عصيان (disobedience)
أهليّة (capacity)
معتمد (relied-upon)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There are only two opinions on taqlid: it either suffices or it does not.
Correction: Actually, there are six opinions, with nuanced sub-opinions and a final view that considers capacity.

Checkpoint
How many opinions on taqlid does the commentator mention?
A) Two
B) Five
C) Six
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view on taqlid is that if a person has the capacity for rational investigation, they are sinful for not using it; if not, they are excused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Danger of Taqlid of Apparent Texts
Critique of taqlid limited to apparent Quran and Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why merely imitating the apparent meanings of Quran and Sunnah without proper interpretation can be a foundation of unbelief.

Core Idea
Blind taqlid of apparent texts can lead to unbelief.

Earlier we learned about taqlid in general. Now the commentator addresses a specific opinion that claims taqlid is sufficient as long as one imitates the Quran and definitive Sunnah. At first this sounds safe, but the commentator points out a serious problem: taking certain apparent texts at face value, without proper theological interpretation, can actually lead to unbelief. Let's examine his examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and the comment
First, read the opinion that taqlid suffices if one imitates the Quran and definitive Sunnah. Then read the commentator's response: 'fihi nazar'. What does that indicate?
2
Examine the examples
The commentator lists examples of apparent texts that, if taken literally, lead to anthropomorphism (tashbih). Identify the verses and hadith he cites.
These are examples of mutashabihat that require ta'wil.
3
Understand the principle
The core issue is that mere taqlid of the apparent (zahir) without engaging with theological interpretation (ta'wil) can place one outside the bounds of correct belief. This shows the importance of proper theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : يكفي إن قلد القرآنَ والسُّنَّة القطعية، وفيه نظر . وذهب بعضهم إلى تحريم النظر؛ لأنَّه مَظنَّةُ الوقوع في الشُّبَه والضَّلال، وليس بشيء . قوله : ( وفيه نظر) : أي : لأنَّ مجرَّد تقليد ظاهر الكتاب والسُّنَّة من أصول الكفر؛ كتقليد : يَدُ اللهِ فَوْقَ أَيْدِيهِمْ، (وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهُ ) ، عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ، وكتقليد : ينزل ربُّنَا كل ليلةٍ إلى سماء الدُّنيا . قوله : (وليس بشيء) : أي : لأنَّ بالنَّظر ينتقل الشَّخص من التقليد إلى المعرفة، فهو يزيل الشَّبه فكيف يوقع فيها : ولورود الأمر به. قال الغزالي: أسرفت طائفة بتكفير عوام المسلمين، وزعموا أنَّ من لم يعرف العقائد الشَّرعيَّة بالأدلة التي حرَّروها؛ فهو كافر، فضيقوا رحمة الله الواسعة، وجعلوا الجنَّة مختصة بجماعة يسيرة من المتكلمين) انتهى سحيمي
And it is said: It suffices if he imitates the Quran and the definitive Sunnah, but there is consideration in it. And some went to prohibiting nazar because it is a source of falling into ambiguities and misguidance, and it is nothing. His saying: (there is consideration in it) meaning: because mere imitation of the apparent [texts] of the Book and Sunnah is from the foundations of unbelief, such as imitating: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', 'And He is the One in heaven is God and on earth is God', 'He is established over the Throne', and imitating: 'Our Lord descends every night to the heaven of this world'. His saying: (and it is nothing) meaning: because by nazar a person transfers from taqlid to knowledge, so it removes ambiguities, so how could it cause them? And because of the command to it. Al-Ghazali said: A sect has gone to extremes in declaring the common folk of Muslims to be disbelievers, claiming that whoever does not know the religious creeds by the proofs they have formulated is a disbeliever. They constricted the vast mercy of Allah and made Paradise exclusive to a small group of theologians. End of [quote from] al-Sahimi.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses two further opinions and provides commentary and a quote. First, an opinion that taqlid suffices only if one imitates the Quran and definitive Sunnah—the commentator says 'fihi nazar' (there is consideration), meaning it is problematic. He explains that merely imitating the apparent (zahir) texts without proper interpretation can lead to unbelief, giving examples like the hand of God, God in heaven, istiwa', and the descent hadith, because these appearances require interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid anthropomorphism. Second, the opinion that nazar is prohibited because it leads to doubts and misguidance—the commentator refutes it, saying nazar actually transfers a person from taqlid to knowledge, removing doubts, and is commanded by God. He then quotes al-Ghazali, who condemns those who declare lay Muslims disbelievers for not knowing the technical proofs of the theologians, saying they constrict God's mercy and limit Paradise to a small group of dialectical theologians.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
ظاهر (apparent)
كفر (unbelief)
تشبيه (anthropomorphism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some scholars think that taqlid of Quran and definitive Sunnah is always sufficient without interpretation.
Correction: The commentator shows that apparent meanings of mutashabihat can lead to unbelief if not properly interpreted.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the opinion that taqlid suffices with Quran and definitive Sunnah?
A) He agrees it is sufficient
B) He says it is fine for laypeople
C) He says there is consideration (fihi nazar)
D) He says it is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Mere imitation of the apparent meanings of scripture, without proper theological interpretation, can be a root of unbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refuting the Prohibition of Nazar and Warning Against Takfir
Refutation of prohibition of nazar and condemnation of takfir of laity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nazar is not prohibited but rather removes doubts, and why declaring laypeople disbelievers is a grave error.

Core Idea
Nazar removes doubts and is commanded; takfir of common Muslims constricts God's mercy.

After discussing taqlid, the commentator turns to another extreme opinion: that nazar (theological reasoning) is prohibited because it leads to doubt and misguidance. He refutes this strongly, arguing that nazar is the means to certainty. He then quotes al-Ghazali, who condemns those who declare lay Muslims disbelievers for not knowing the technical proofs of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the commentator's response to the prohibition of nazar. Why does he say 'it is nothing'?
The refutation is brief but strong.
2
Understand the argument

3
Read al-Ghazali's quote
Al-Ghazali warns against extremism in takfir. What is the danger he identifies?
Relates to the common folk (al-awamm).

Al-Ghazali said: A sect has gone to extremes in declaring the common folk of Muslims to be disbelievers, claiming that whoever does not know the religious creeds by the proofs they have formulated is a disbeliever. They constricted the vast mercy of Allah and made Paradise exclusive to a small group of theologians. End of [quote from] al-Sahimi.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses two further opinions and provides commentary and a quote. First, an opinion that taqlid suffices only if one imitates the Quran and definitive Sunnah—the commentator says 'fihi nazar' (there is consideration), meaning it is problematic. He explains that merely imitating the apparent (zahir) texts without proper interpretation can lead to unbelief, giving examples like the hand of God, God in heaven, istiwa', and the descent hadith, because these appearances require interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid anthropomorphism. Second, the opinion that nazar is prohibited because it leads to doubts and misguidance—the commentator refutes it, saying nazar actually transfers a person from taqlid to knowledge, removing doubts, and is commanded by God. He then quotes al-Ghazali, who condemns those who declare lay Muslims disbelievers for not knowing the technical proofs of the theologians, saying they constrict God's mercy and limit Paradise to a small group of dialectical theologians.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
تحريم (prohibition)
شبه (ambiguities)
جنة (Paradise)
غزالي (Ghazali)
تكفير (declaring disbeliever)
عوام (common folk)
متكلمون (theologians)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar (theological reasoning) is prohibited because it inevitably leads to doubt and error.
Correction: Nazar is actually commanded and leads to certainty by removing doubts; the prohibition is baseless.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is nazar not prohibited?
A) Because it is easy
B) Because it transfers from taqlid to knowledge and is commanded
C) Because the majority practice it
D) Because it is only for scholars

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the means to true knowledge, not a source of error, and declaring laypeople disbelievers for not knowing technical proofs is a grave extremism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological doubt, do you tend to avoid thinking deeply (fearing it will weaken your faith) or do you engage in nazar to remove the doubt? Which approach does this lesson command, and what will you change starting today?
?
Have you ever looked down on someone whose faith seemed less 'intellectual' or less informed by formal proofs? How does al-Ghazali's warning—that such narrowness constricts God's mercy—challenge your attitude? Name one person you have judged and commit to seeing them as a fellow Muslim whose faith may be simple but sincere.
?
If you are honest, do you rely on taqlid (blind imitation) in many of your beliefs? What is one belief you have held without personal investigation? Will you now examine it through nazar, even if it feels uncomfortable? Set a specific time this week to do so.

Al-Ghazali's Warning Against Takfir of Lay Muslims
Al-Ghazali criticizes those who declare lay Muslims disbelievers for not knowing proofs.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali condemned declaring lay Muslims disbelievers merely for not mastering theological proofs.

Takfir of lay Muslims constricts Allah's vast mercy.

Al-Ghazali, the great theologian, addresses a dangerous tendency among some Muslim groups to exclude ordinary believers from the fold of Islam. He warns that making detailed theological knowledge a condition for faith contradicts the expansiveness of divine mercy. This passage sets forth a balanced understanding of faith and disbelief.

Ibn Arabi's Five Levels of Faith
Ibn Arabi classifies iman into five levels from taqlid to haqiqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Arabi's classification of iman into five levels, which ones are obligatory, and the nature of the higher spiritual stations.

Core Idea
Faith has five levels — but only the first two are required of every Muslim.

Ibn Arabi, the famous Andalusian mystic, offers a classification of faith (iman) that moves from basic acceptance to complete absorption in God. This passage from al-Kharidat presents five ascending levels, emphasizing that while the first two are duties, the higher three are divine gifts. Understanding this hierarchy helps clarify the relationship between outer practice and inner realization in Islamic spirituality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ibn Arabi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the five divisions
The passage presents five divisions of iman. Find each one and notice how Ibn Arabi defines it before stating whether it is obligatory or a divine gift.
3
Distinguish obligatory from gift
Ibn Arabi explicitly states that only the first two divisions (taqlid and 'ilm) are obligatory. The other three ('iyan, haqq, haqiqa) are divine knowledges bestowed upon whom Allah wills. Note this important boundary.
This corrects the misconception that all levels are required.
4
Reflect on the progression
Consider how each level builds on the previous one, from imitation to knowledge to direct vision to truth to reality. What does this say about the journey of faith?
The higher levels involve veiling removal and direct experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن العربي : أقسام الإيمان :خمسة : إيمان تقليد: وهو من العقائد عن شيخ، وجزم بها من غير معرفة دليل، وإيمان علم: وهو معرفة العقائد بأدلتها، وهذا من أهل علم اليقين، وكلا القسمين صاحبهما محجوب، وإيمان عيان وهو معرفة الله بمراقبة القلب، فلا يغيب ربُّه عن خاطره طرفة عين، بل هيبته في قلبه كأنه يراه، وهو مقام المراقبة وعين اليقين وإيمان حقٌّ وهو رؤية الله بقلبه، وهو معنى قولهم : العارف يرى الله في كل شيء، وهو مقام المشاهدة وحق اليقين، وصاحب هذا المقام والذي قبله يستدل بالحقِّ على الخلق، وإيمان حقيقة: وهو بالله عما سواه، والسُّكر بحبه فلا يشهد إلا إياه، كمن غرق في بحر ولم ير له ساحلاً، وهذا ليس له دليل ولا مدلول، فالواجب على الشخص أحد القسمين الأوَّلين، وأمَّا الثَّلاثة الآخر؛ فعلوم ربَّانيَّة يخصُّ بها من يشاء .
And Ibn Arabi said: The divisions of faith (iman) are five: faith of imitation (iman taqlid): it is the beliefs from a shaykh, and he is certain of them without knowledge of evidence; and faith of knowledge (iman 'ilm): it is knowing the beliefs with their evidences, and this is from the people of the knowledge of certainty ('ilm al-yaqin), and both of these divisions their possessor is veiled; and faith of direct vision (iman 'iyan): it is knowing Allah through the watchfulness of the heart, so his Lord does not absent from his mind for a blink of an eye, but His awe is in his heart as if he sees Him, and it is the station of watchfulness (muraqaba) and the eye of certainty ('ayn al-yaqin); and faith of truth (iman haqq): it is seeing Allah with his heart, and it is the meaning of their saying: the gnostic sees Allah in everything, and it is the station of witnessing (mushahada) and the truth of certainty (haqq al-yaqin), and the possessor of this station and the one before it use the Truth to derive evidence for creation; and faith of reality (iman haqiqa): it is being with Allah to the exclusion of others, and intoxication with His love so that he witnesses nothing but Him, like one who drowned in the sea and saw no shore, and this has no evidence and no object of evidence. So what is obligatory upon a person is one of the first two divisions; as for the other three, they are divine knowledges that He bestows upon whom He wills.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Arabi's classification of iman into five ascending levels. The first two (taqlid and 'ilm) are obligatory for every Muslim, though their possessors are 'veiled' compared to higher stations. The next three ('iyan, haqq, haqiqa) are spiritual gifts, not obligations, culminating in the state of complete annihilation in God (fana'). The passage underscores that higher mystical states are divine favors, not duties.

Key Terms
إيمان
تقليد
علم
عيان
حق
حقيقة
يقين
مراقبة
مشاهدة
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All five levels of faith are mandatory for every Muslim.
Correction: Ibn Arabi explicitly states that only the first two divisions (taqlid and 'ilm) are obligatory; the remaining three are divine bestowals.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Arabi, which levels of iman are obligatory for every Muslim?
A) All five levels
B) The first two: iman of taqlid and iman of 'ilm
C) The middle three
D) Only iman of haqiqa

Key Takeaway
Ibn Arabi teaches that while the first two levels of faith are duties, the higher degrees of witnessing and annihilation are gifts from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the first two levels of faith are obligatory and the higher ones are divine gifts. Which level do you honestly feel you are at right now? Does knowing that the higher levels are not obligatory make you complacent, or does it make you yearn more earnestly for Allah's grace?
?
Ibn Arabi says that even the level of 'ilm (knowledge with evidence) is still 'veiled' from direct witnessing. In your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge, have you allowed your studies to become a veil that keeps you from the immediacy of Allah's presence? What practical step can you take today to move from knowing about Allah to actually being aware of Him in your heart?
?
The highest level (haqiqa) is described as being so overwhelmed by love that one sees nothing but Allah. This is a gift, not an achievement. Does that humble you or frustrate you? Are you willing to surrender your own striving and beg Allah to grant you even a glimpse of that station, or do you secretly prefer to rely on your own efforts?

The Five Divisions of Faith According to Ibn Arabi
Ibn Arabi enumerates five categories of faith and describes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Arabi's five-fold classification of faith and the distinction between the obligatory levels (taqlid and 'ilm) and the higher spiritual stations that are divine gifts.

Core Idea
Faith progresses from imitation to knowledge to direct vision to union.

This lesson presents a profound classification by Ibn Arabi, mapping the journey of faith from the basic level of acceptance through authority to the highest station of annihilation in Allah. Understanding these categories helps grasp the spiritual path in Islamic theology and the relationship between outward belief and inner realization.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the text once without over-analyzing, absorbing the rhythm of Ibn Arabi's enumeration. Then compare the translation to get a first sense of the terms.
2
Identify the five categories and their descriptions
List the five divisions as they appear: taqlid, 'ilm, 'iyan, haqq, haqiqa. Note the defining characteristic of each and the associated terms (like 'ilm al-yaqin, 'ayn al-yaqin, haqq al-yaqin).
The three yaqin terms mark increasing levels of certainty.
3
Distinguish the obligatory from the gifted
The passage states that only the first two divisions are duties (wajib). The rest are divine sciences (ʿulūm rabbāniyya) that Allah grants to whom He wills. Understand the theological significance of this.
This guards against the misconception that all five are required of every Muslim.
4
Reflect on the progression and metaphor
Notice how the levels move from reliance on external authority (taqlid) to knowledge with proof, then to immediate awareness, then to seeing Allah in all things, and finally to complete absorption. The metaphor of drowning in the sea illustrates haqiqa.
Each higher level subsumes and transcends the lower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن العربي : أقسام الإيمان :خمسة : إيمان تقليد: وهو من العقائد عن شيخ، وجزم بها من غير معرفة دليل، وإيمان علم: وهو معرفة العقائد بأدلتها، وهذا من أهل علم اليقين، وكلا القسمين صاحبهما محجوب، وإيمان عيان وهو معرفة الله بمراقبة القلب، فلا يغيب ربُّه عن خاطره طرفة عين، بل هيبته في قلبه كأنه يراه، وهو مقام المراقبة وعين اليقين وإيمان حقٌّ وهو رؤية الله بقلبه، وهو معنى قولهم : العارف يرى الله في كل شيء، وهو مقام المشاهدة وحق اليقين، وصاحب هذا المقام والذي قبله يستدل بالحقِّ على الخلق، وإيمان حقيقة: وهو بالله عما سواه، والسُّكر بحبه فلا يشهد إلا إياه، كمن غرق في بحر ولم ير له ساحلاً) (۱) ، وهذا ليس له دليل ولا مدلول، فالواجب على الشخص أحد القسمين الأوَّلين، وأمَّا الثَّلاثة الآخر؛ فعلوم ربَّانيَّة يخصُّ بها من يشاء.
And Ibn Arabi said: The divisions of faith are five: Faith of taqlid (imitation): it is beliefs from a shaykh, and affirming them without knowing a proof. Faith of knowledge: it is knowing beliefs with their proofs, and this is from the people of 'ilm al-yaqin (knowledge of certainty), and both of these categories, their possessor is veiled. Faith of 'iyan (direct vision): it is knowing Allah by guarding the heart, so his Lord does not disappear from his mind for a blink of an eye, rather His awe is in his heart as if he sees Him, and it is the station of muraqaba (vigilance) and 'ayn al-yaqin (eye of certainty). Faith of haqq (truth): it is seeing Allah with his heart, and it is the meaning of their saying: the gnostic sees Allah in everything, and it is the station of mushahada (witnessing) and haqq al-yaqin (truth of certainty), and the possessor of this station and the one before it deduces from the Truth about creation. Faith of haqiqa (reality): it is being with Allah away from other than Him, and intoxication with His love so that he witnesses none but Him, like one who drowns in a sea and sees no shore for it. And this has no proof nor object of proof. So the duty upon a person is one of the first two divisions, and as for the other three, they are divine sciences that He specializes to whomever He wills.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Arabi's classification of faith into five levels. The first is taqlid (imitation), where one accepts beliefs from a teacher without proof. The second is 'ilm (knowledge), where one knows beliefs with their proofs; this is the level of 'ilm al-yaqin. Both are veiled from higher stations. The third is 'iyan (direct vision), achieved through heart vigil, where one is constantly aware of Allah's presence; this is 'ayn al-yaqin. The fourth is haqq (truth), seeing Allah with the heart, mushahada, and haqq al-yaqin. The fifth is haqiqa (reality), complete absorption in Allah, like drowning. The author notes that only the first two are obligatory; the rest are divine gifts.

إيمان علم
إيمان عيان
إيمان حق
إيمان حقيقة
علم اليقين
عين اليقين
حق اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that all five levels of faith mentioned by Ibn Arabi are required of every Muslim to be a true believer.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that only the first two divisions (taqlid and 'ilm) are obligatory (wajib). The other three are divine sciences that Allah grants as gifts to whom He wills, not duties upon everyone.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Only the first two levels of faith (imitation and knowledge) are obligatory for every believer, while the higher three (direct vision, truth, and reality) are divine gifts granted to whom Allah wills.

?

Purpose of Creation and the Path to Love and Gratitude
Al-Sawi explains the purpose of creation and the progression to love and gratitude through seclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the purpose of creation is worship, that this leads to abundant love of God, and that seclusion and reflection are practical means to achieve this progression.

Core Idea
Our purpose is worship, which deepens into love and gratitude through seclusion and reflection.

Al-Sawi unpacks three key phrases from the base text to show the believer's journey: from knowing the purpose of creation (worship) to experiencing God's abundant love, and finally to the practical step of seclusion for reflection. This lesson explains the connection between these elements and how they form a coherent path toward spiritual fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the text's flow inform your first impression.
Notice that the passage explains three separate phrases sequentially.
2
Identify the three elements
The passage covers: (1) Purpose of creation is worship, (2) Abundance of God's love, (3) Seclusion as an aid. Identify each in the text.
3
Understand the Quranic proof
The first element is supported by the verse from Surah al-Dhariyat. Reflect on why worship is the ultimate purpose.
4
Reflect on seclusion and reflection
The third element quotes Ibn Ata Allah: 'Nothing benefits the heart like seclusion by which it enters the field of reflection.' Consider how solitude aids spiritual growth.
5
Connect to personal practice
Think about how you can incorporate moments of seclusion and reflection to deepen your worship and love for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلى ما خلقت لأجله :أي وهو العبادة، قال تعالى: (وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) (۱)
قوله : (إلى وفور حبّه من إضافة الرّفة للموصوف؛ أي: حبه الوافر؛ أي: الزائد.
قوله : (فيترتب على ذلك ... إلخ ) : أي : ويعين على ذلك العزلة عن النَّاس، قال ابن عطاء الله السكندري في حكمه»: (ما نفع القلب مثل عزلة يدخل بها ميدان فكرة)(۲)
His saying: (to that for which you were created) meaning it is worship, the Exalted said: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' His saying: (to abundance of His love) from the possessive construct of abundance for the described; meaning His abundant love, meaning the extra. His saying: (so results from that... etc.) meaning: and that is aided by seclusion from people. Ibn Ata Allah al-Sakandari said in his aphorisms: 'Nothing benefits the heart like seclusion by which it enters the field of reflection.'
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of three phrases from the base text. First, Al-Sawi clarifies that the purpose of creation is worship ('ibada), quoting the Quranic verse from Surah al-Dhariyat. Second, he explains 'to abundance of His love' as the superabundant love of God for His servant. Third, he comments on the resulting effect: it is aided by seclusion ('uzla) from people, supporting this with a saying of Ibn Ata Allah that seclusion helps the heart enter the field of reflection (fikra). Thus, the path to fulfilling one's purpose involves seclusion for contemplation.

Key Terms
العبادة
وفور الحب
العزلة
الفكرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the purpose of creation is merely ritual acts of worship without deeper love or gratitude.
Correction: Al-Sawi emphasizes that the purpose leads to 'abundant love' and that seclusion and reflection cultivate the heart, showing that worship is a path to deeper connection.

Checkpoint

C) Engaging in debate
D) Performing charity

Key Takeaway
The purpose of creation is worship, which leads to abundant love of God, and seclusion with reflection is the practical key to achieving this.

The Three Early Generations in Virtue
Al-Sawi ranks the three early generations and cites hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ranking of the three early generations (Companions, Followers, Followers of the Followers) in virtue and the basis for this ranking in hadith.

Core Idea
The best generations are the first three, each better than the next.

Al-Sawi begins by affirming the creedal ranking of the early Muslim generations. This teaching is based on prophetic hadith and distinguishes Sunni belief from those who deny such ranking. The passage explains the duration of each century and settles a scholarly debate about later generations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about generations
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully, noting the names of the three generations and the specified durations of their centuries.
2
Identify the proof texts
Notice that al-Sawi cites two hadiths to support the ranking: one explicitly stating the best centuries, and another affirming progressive decline.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why this ranking is a matter of creed rather than mere history. Understanding this heightens respect for the early community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالتّابعون، فتابع التابعين.
ويجب الإمساك عما وقع بين الصحابة من النزاع.
قوله : (فالتّابعون) :أي فرتبتهم تلي رتبة الصَّحابة، وقرن التابعين الذين انفردوا فيه عن الصَّحابة سبعون سنة .
قوله : ( فتابع التّابعين :أي فرتبتهم تلي رتبة التابعين في الفضل وقرنهم ثلاثون سنة، والأصل في ذلك التفضيل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «خيركم قرني، ثمَّ الَّذين يلونهم، ثمَّ الَّذين يلونهم» ، ومن بعد هذه القرون، قيل: سواء في الفضل، وقيل : متفاوتون، فكلُّ قرن أفضل من الذي بعده، وهو الحقُّ لحديث : «ما من يوم إلَّا والذي بعده شرٌّ منه»
قوله : ( ويجب الإمساك عما وقع بين الصَّحابة من النزاع) : أي : لأنَّ التفتيش عما جرى بينهم ليس من العقائد الدِّينيَّة، ولا مما ينتفع به في الدين، بل ربَّما ضرَّ في اليقين، فلا يباح الخوض فيه إلَّا للتعليم، أو الرَّدِّ على المتعصبين، ومع ذلك فيجب تأويله وصرفه إلى محمل حسن؛ فإنّهم مجتهدون والمجتهد مأجور أخطأ أو أصاب
The Followers, then the Followers of the Followers. And it is obligatory to refrain from what occurred of dispute among the Companions. His saying: (The Followers): meaning their rank follows the rank of the Companions, and the century of the Followers, who were unique in it from the Companions, is seventy years. His saying: (The Followers of the Followers): meaning their rank follows the rank of the Followers in virtue, and their century is thirty years. The basis for this preference is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The best of you is my century, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' And after these centuries, it is said they are equal in virtue, and it is said they differ, so each century is better than the one after it, and that is the truth due to the hadith: 'There is no day except that the one after it is worse than it.' His saying: (And it is obligatory to refrain from what occurred of dispute among the Companions): meaning because investigating what happened between them is not from religious beliefs, nor from what benefits in religion; rather it may harm certainty. So it is not permitted to delve into it except for teaching or refuting the bigoted. And even then, it is obligatory to interpret it and turn it to a good interpretation, for they are mujtahids, and the mujtahid is rewarded whether he errs or is correct.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a creedal statement and its commentary by al-Sawi. It first states two articles of faith: the ranking of the three early generations (the Companions, the Followers, and the Followers of the Followers) and the obligation to refrain from discussing disputes among the Companions. The commentary then expands on each. For the first, it explains that the rank of the Followers follows that of the Companions, and their century (the first century after the Hijra) lasted seventy years. The Followers of the Followers follow in virtue, and their century lasted thirty years. The basis for this preference is the hadith 'The best of you is my century...' After these three centuries, opinions differ: some say all later generations are equal in virtue, others say they are ranked, with each century being better than the next. Al-Sawi affirms the latter as correct, citing another hadith that each day is worse than the previous. For the second article, the ruling on Sahaba disputes, the commentary explains that delving into those events is not part of religious belief, nor does it benefit the religion; it may even harm one's certainty. Therefore it is forbidden except for two purposes: teaching (explaining what is necessary to know) or refuting those who are fanatical. Even then, one must interpret the actions of the Companions in a positive light, because they were mujtahids, and a mujtahid is rewarded whether correct or mistaken.

Key Terms
الصحابة
التابعون
تابع التابعين
القرون
الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All generations after the Prophet are equal in virtue.
Correction: The hadith explicitly ranks the first three centuries as the best, and the correct view is that each subsequent century is progressively lower in virtue.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how long was the century of the Followers (Tabieen)?
A) 30 years
B) 70 years
C) 100 years
D) The passage does not specify.

Key Takeaway
The first three generations are the best, with the Followers' century lasting 70 years and the Followers of the Followers' century lasting 30 years, based on prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that every day is worse than the previous one. Do you live your life as if that matters, or do you simply go along with the times? What specific step will you take today to align yourself with the way of the early generations?
?
If you truly believe the first three generations are the best, how does that affect your choices in whom you take knowledge from and whom you emulate? Are you actively seeking to learn from their example, or are you content with modern influences?

Refraining from Sahaba Disputes
Al-Sawi states the obligation to refrain from Sahaba disputes and explains the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on discussing disputes among the Companions and the proper approach to such discussions.

Core Idea
Do not delve into Sahaba disputes except for teaching or refuting bigots, and always interpret favorably.

The second part of the passage addresses a sensitive topic: the conflicts that occurred among the Companions. Sunni scholars have consistently advised restraint and good assumptions. Al-Sawi lays out the ruling: it is not permitted to investigate such disputes freely, but only for specific legitimate purposes, and even then with positive interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling and its rationale
Read the Arabic and translation to see why investigating Sahaba disputes is not part of religious belief.
2
Examine the two exceptions
Note the two valid reasons: teaching and refuting bigots. Also note the requirement of favorable interpretation.
3
Understand the principle of ijtihad
The Companions were mujtahids, rewarded whether right or wrong. This is the basis for excusing their mistakes.

Belief in Awliya and the Reality of Karamat
Al-Sawi lays out the obligation of belief in awliya and refutes objections against karamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to believe in awliya, the distinction between denying their existence and denying their miracles, and the refutation of objections against karamat.

Core Idea
Denying the awliya is kufr; denying their karamat is innovation, not kufr.

Al-Sawi begins by establishing the obligation to believe in the awliya, citing Quranic verses. He then addresses those who deny the miracles of the awliya, labeling them as sinful innovators. The passage refutes two major objections: confusion with prophethood and the loss of supernatural nature due to frequency. Finally, a question about the increase of karamat in later times is posed, and its answer begins.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow, then read the full translation. Notice the author's style of presenting rulings and then refuting objections.
The passage is incomplete; the answer to the last question is cut off.
2
Identify the two rulings
Find where the author states the ruling for denying the existence of awliya and the ruling for denying their karamat. Note the difference in severity.
The first is kufr, the second is fisq and bid'a.
3
Examine the refutation of objections
The passage presents two objections from those who deny karamat. Locate each objection and its corresponding refutation. Understand the reasoning.
The key distinction is the claim of prophethood.
4
Reflect on the question about frequency
Read the question and the beginning of the answer. Consider how the answer relates to the purpose of karamat.
The answer is incomplete, but the idea is that karamat become more frequent when faith is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالأولياء (ثم) يجب الإيمان بـ (الأوليا) .... وردَّ بأنَّ الله أخبر عنهم أنَّهم يلحقون بآبائهم في السيادة والخلقة. قوله : (ثم يجب الإيمان بالأوليا :أي وجوب الأصول، فمن أنكر وجودهم ؛ كفر لمصادمة القرآن قال تعالى : (أَلَا إِنَّ أَوْلِيَاءَ اللَّهِ لَا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ (1) ، إِنْ أَوْلِيَاؤُهُ إِلَّا الْمُتَّقُونَ ) (2). وأمَّا من أنكر كراماتهم كالحليمي من أهل السُّنَّة، والمعتزلة؛ فهو فاسق مبتدع، محتجين بأنَّها لو وجدت الكرامات؛ لالتبست بمعجزات الأنبياء، فيلتبس النَّبِيُّ بغيره، ولو وجدت واستمرَّت؛ لكثرت وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة. ورد ذلك بأنَّا لا نسلّم التباس الوليّ بالنَّبيِّ ؛ للفرق بينهما، وهو دعوى النُّبوَّة وعدمها، ولا نسلّم أنَّ كثرتها تصيرها غير خارقة بالعادة، بل تفيد استمرار الخارق، وهو أمر واقع لا شكّ فيه . وسئل بعضهم : لأي شيء كثرت الكرامات في الزمان المتأخّر دون المتقدّم؟ فأجاب بأنَّ ذلك لضعف إيمان المتأخرين، فاحتيج لتأليفهم بالكرامات ليعتقدوا في الصَّالحين، وأمَّا في الزَّمن المتقدّم ؛ فاعتقادهم تابع
Belief in the saints. (Then) belief in the saints is obligatory.... And it was refuted that Allah informed about them that they will join their fathers in leadership and creation. His statement: (Then belief in the saints is obligatory): i.e., obligatory in fundamentals. Whoever denies their existence disbelieves because it contradicts the Quran. He said, exalted: (Verily, the saints of Allah, no fear shall come upon them nor shall they grieve) (1), (His saints are only the righteous) (2). As for whoever denies their miracles (karamat), like al-Halimi from the Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila, he is a sinful innovator (fasiq mubtadi'), arguing that if miracles (karamat) existed, they would be confused with the miracles of the prophets, so the prophet would be confused with others; and if they existed and continued, they would become frequent and cease to be supernatural. That is refuted by: we do not admit the confusion of a saint with a prophet because of the difference between them, which is the claim of prophethood or its absence; and we do not admit that their frequency makes them non-supernatural; rather, it indicates the continuation of the supernatural, which is a reality about which there is no doubt. And someone asked them: Why are karamat more frequent in later times than earlier? He answered: That is because of the weakness of faith of later people, so they needed to be won over by karamat so that they believe in the righteous; as for earlier times, their faith was [more] established...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological status of the awliya (saints) and their miracles (karamat). It begins by stating that belief in the awliya is obligatory as a matter of fundamental doctrine. Whoever denies their existence commits kufr because it contradicts explicit Quranic verses, such as 'Verily, the saints of Allah, no fear shall come upon them nor shall they grieve' and 'His saints are only the righteous.' The passage then addresses those who deny the miracles of the saints, specifically naming al-Halimi from the Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila. Such a person is not a kafir but a fasiq mubtadi' (sinful innovator). The argument of these deniers is that if karamat existed, they would be confused with the miracles of the prophets, leading to confusion between a prophet and a non-prophet. Also, if karamat occurred frequently, they would cease to be supernatural. The refutation is that confusion does not arise because there is a clear distinction: a prophet claims prophethood while a saint does not. Furthermore, frequency does not make a miracle non-supernatural; rather, it confirms the continuation of the supernatural, which is a real and undeniable phenomenon. The passage concludes with a question and the beginning of an answer: why are karamat more common in later times? The answer given is that due to the weakness of faith in later periods, karamat are needed to win people over to believe in righteous individuals, while in earlier times faith was stronger. The text ends mid-sentence, indicating the answer continues but is not present in this window.

فاسق مبتدع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the miracles (karamat) of awliya is an act of disbelief (kufr).
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is a sinful innovation (fisq and bid'a), not disbelief, as long as one does not deny the existence of awliya themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who denies the miracles (karamat) of awliya?
A) He is a kafir.
B) He is a fasiq mubtadi' (sinful innovator).
C) He is a righteous believer.
D) He is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Belief in awliya is obligatory; denying their existence is kufr, but denying their miracles is a sinful innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear stories of saints performing miracles (karamat), do you feel a sense of awe and connection, or do you find yourself doubting or dismissing them? What does your reaction reveal about the strength of your iman in the unseen realities of Allah's creation? Commit to reflecting on one story of a saint's karamat this week and ask yourself: Does it increase my love for the righteous, or does it expose a weakness in my belief?
?
The passage explains that karamat become more frequent in later times because of weak faith, not because they are less real. How would you assess your own faith? Do you rely on extraordinary signs to stay steadfast, or is your yaqeen (certainty) so strong that you would believe even without them? If you find yourself needing signs, what one step can you take today to strengthen your faith without depending on miraculous confirmations?
?
Denying the karamat of awliya is considered a sinful innovation (fisq wa bid'a), even if one believes in the existence of awliya. Have you ever, even silently, looked down on those who venerate saints or their stories as superstitious? Test your heart: Are you guilty of the very attitude this passage warns against? Repent if needed and ask Allah to grant you love for His righteous friends without falling into extremes.

Belief in Awliya and Its Obligation
Al-Sawi states the obligation of belief in awliya and classifies deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic obligation to believe in awliya, the difference between denying their existence (kufr) and denying their karamat (fisq and bid‘a), and the evidence from the Qur’an.

Core Idea
Denying the existence of awliya is kufr; denying their karamat is a sinful innovation.

This lesson begins with the foundational obligation of believing in the awliya of Allah. Al-Sawi explains that rejecting their existence contradicts the Qur’an and constitutes disbelief, while rejecting their extraordinary acts, though a serious error, is not disbelief but innovation and sin. Understanding this distinction is crucial for sound Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before interpreting.
Notice the verse citations embedded in the commentary.
2
Identify the two types of denial
The passage mentions two types of people: those who deny the existence of awliya and those who deny their karamat. Note the different rulings for each.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic evidence
Look at the verse quoted: 'Verily, the awliya of God, no fear shall be upon them nor shall they grieve'. How does this prove the existence of awliya?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب الإيمان بالأوليا. وردَّ بأنَّ الله أخبر عنهم أنَّهم يلحقون بآبائهم في السيادة والخلقة. قوله : (ثم يجب الإيمان بالأوليا :أي وجوب الأصول، فمن أنكر وجودهم ؛ كفر لمصادمة القرآن قال تعالى : (أَلَا إِنَّ أَوْلِيَاءَ اللَّهِ لَا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ) ، إِنْ أَوْلِيَاؤُهُ إِلَّا الْمُتَّقُونَ ). وأمَّا من أنكر كراماتهم كالحليمي من أهل السُّنَّة، والمعتزلة؛ فهو فاسق مبتدع، محتجين بأنَّها لو وجدت الكرامات؛ لالتبست بمعجزات الأنبياء، فيلتبس النَّبِيُّ بغيره، ولو وجدت واستمرَّت؛ لكثرت وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة. ورد ذلك بأنَّا لا نسلّم التباس الوليّ بالنَّبيِّ ؛ للفرق بينهما، وهو دعوى النُّبوَّة وعدمها، ولا نسلّم أنَّ كثرتها تصيرها غير خارقة بالعادة، بل تفيد استمرار الخارق، وهو أمر واقع لا شكّ فيه . وسئل بعضهم : لأي شيء كثرت الكرامات في الزمان المتأخّر دون المتقدّم؟ فأجاب بأنَّ ذلك لضعف إيمان المتأخرين، فاحتيج لتأليفهم بالكرامات ليعتقدوا في الصَّالحين، وأمَّا في الزَّمن المتقدّم ؛ فاعتقادهم تابع
Then belief in the awliya is obligatory. And it was refuted that God informed about them that they will join their fathers in nobility and creation. His statement: (Then belief in the awliya is obligatory) meaning the obligation of the fundamentals, for whoever denies their existence disbelieves due to contradicting the Qur'an, He said: 'Verily, the awliya of God, no fear shall be upon them nor shall they grieve' and 'His awliya are only the pious'. As for one who denies their karamat, like al-Halimi from the Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu‘tazila, he is a sinful innovator, arguing that if karamat existed they would be confused with the miracles of prophets, so the prophet would be confused with others, and if they existed and continued, they would become abundant and cease to be extraordinary. This was refuted by that we do not concede the confusion of the wali with the prophet due to the difference between them, which is the claim of prophethood or its absence, and we do not concede that their abundance makes them non-extraordinary, rather it indicates the continuity of the extraordinary, which is a real matter with no doubt. And someone asked: Why did karamat become abundant in later times and not in earlier times? He answered: That is due to the weakness of faith of later people, so they needed to be won over by karamat to believe in the righteous, while in earlier times their belief was based on
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of obligation, classification of deniers, and refutation of objections. It begins by establishing the obligation of believing in awliya (saints), then explains that denying their very existence is kufr because it contradicts the Qur'anic verses affirming them. The author then distinguishes a second group: those who accept the existence of awliya but deny their karamat (extraordinary acts), such as al-Halimi and the Mu‘tazila. These are declared fasiq (sinful) and mubtadi‘ (innovator). The objection raised by them is that karamat would cause confusion with the miracles (mu‘jizat) of prophets. The author refutes by noting the clear difference: a prophet claims prophethood, a wali does not. Another objection is that if karamat were abundant, they would cease to be extraordinary; the author replies that continuity of the extraordinary does not negate its extraordinary nature. Finally, a historical question is posed about the increase of karamat in later times. The answer given is that later believers had weaker faith, so karamat served to strengthen their belief in righteous individuals, whereas earlier believers had stronger faith and did not need such signs.

أولياء
كرامات
فاسق
مبتدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying karamat is the same as denying the existence of awliya.
Correction: Denying existence is kufr; denying karamat is innovation and sin, not kufr.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the ruling on someone who denies the karamat of awliya?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) He is a sinful innovator.
C) He is excused because there is no evidence.
D) He is considered a righteous Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Belief in awliya is obligatory; denying their existence is disbelief, while denying their karamat is a serious sin and innovation.
