Refuting Objections Against Karamat
Al-Sawi refutes objections against karamat and answers a historical question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections raised against the reality of karamat and how they are refuted, as well as the wisdom behind their prevalence in later times.

Core Idea
Karamat are distinct from miracles by the claim of prophethood; abundance does not negate their extraordinary nature.

This lesson presents the objections that karamat would cause confusion with prophetic miracles and that their abundance would make them ordinary. Al-Sawi refutes both, and then explains a historical observation about the increase of karamat in later times due to weak faith. This solidifies the Sunni belief in the reality of karamat.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two objections raised by the deniers.
2
Analyze the first objection and its refutation
The first objection is that karamat would be confused with prophetic miracles. Note how Al-Sawi refutes it: the wali does not claim prophethood.
3
Analyze the second objection and its refutation
The second objection is that abundance would make karamat non-extraordinary. Al-Sawi argues that continuity does not negate the extraordinary nature.
4
Consider the historical question and answer
The passage ends with a question about why karamat are more abundant in later times. The answer links it to weak faith and the need for reassurance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب الإيمان بالأوليا. وردَّ بأنَّ الله أخبر عنهم أنَّهم يلحقون بآبائهم في السيادة والخلقة. قوله : (ثم يجب الإيمان بالأوليا :أي وجوب الأصول، فمن أنكر وجودهم ؛ كفر لمصادمة القرآن قال تعالى : (أَلَا إِنَّ أَوْلِيَاءَ اللَّهِ لَا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ) ، إِنْ أَوْلِيَاؤُهُ إِلَّا الْمُتَّقُونَ ). وأمَّا من أنكر كراماتهم كالحليمي من أهل السُّنَّة، والمعتزلة؛ فهو فاسق مبتدع، محتجين بأنَّها لو وجدت الكرامات؛ لالتبست بمعجزات الأنبياء، فيلتبس النَّبِيُّ بغيره، ولو وجدت واستمرَّت؛ لكثرت وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة. ورد ذلك بأنَّا لا نسلّم التباس الوليّ بالنَّبيِّ ؛ للفرق بينهما، وهو دعوى النُّبوَّة وعدمها، ولا نسلّم أنَّ كثرتها تصيرها غير خارقة بالعادة، بل تفيد استمرار الخارق، وهو أمر واقع لا شكّ فيه . وسئل بعضهم : لأي شيء كثرت الكرامات في الزمان المتأخّر دون المتقدّم؟ فأجاب بأنَّ ذلك لضعف إيمان المتأخرين، فاحتيج لتأليفهم بالكرامات ليعتقدوا في الصَّالحين، وأمَّا في الزَّمن المتقدّم ؛ فاعتقادهم تابع
Then belief in the awliya is obligatory. And it was refuted that God informed about them that they will join their fathers in nobility and creation. His statement: (Then belief in the awliya is obligatory) meaning the obligation of the fundamentals, for whoever denies their existence disbelieves due to contradicting the Qur'an, He said: 'Verily, the awliya of God, no fear shall be upon them nor shall they grieve' and 'His awliya are only the pious'. As for one who denies their karamat, like al-Halimi from the Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu‘tazila, he is a sinful innovator, arguing that if karamat existed they would be confused with the miracles of prophets, so the prophet would be confused with others, and if they existed and continued, they would become abundant and cease to be extraordinary. This was refuted by that we do not concede the confusion of the wali with the prophet due to the difference between them, which is the claim of prophethood or its absence, and we do not concede that their abundance makes them non-extraordinary, rather it indicates the continuity of the extraordinary, which is a real matter with no doubt. Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of obligation, classification of deniers, and refutation of objections. It begins by establishing the obligation of believing in awliya (saints), then explains that denying their very existence is kufr because it contradicts the Qur'anic verses affirming them. The author then distinguishes a second group: those who accept the existence of awliya but deny their karamat (extraordinary acts), such as al-Halimi and the Mu‘tazila. These are declared fasiq (sinful) and mubtadi‘ (innovator). The objection raised by them is that karamat would cause confusion with the miracles (mu‘jizat) of prophets. The author refutes by noting the clear difference: a prophet claims prophethood, a wali does not. Another objection is that if karamat were abundant, they would cease to be extraordinary; the author replies that continuity of the extraordinary does not negate its extraordinary nature. Finally, a historical question is posed about the increase of karamat in later times. The answer given is that later believers had weaker faith, so karamat served to strengthen their belief in righteous individuals, whereas earlier believers had stronger faith and did not need such signs.

Definition of Wali and Karamah with Proofs
Al-Sawi defines the wali and karamah and provides evidence for their reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a wali (friend of God) and the concept of karamah (miracle of saints), and why belief in karamat is obligatory based on Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Core Idea
The wali is a righteous servant, and karamah is an extraordinary act from God, not prophecy.

Al-Sawi begins by defining who a wali is: one who fulfills the rights of God and people, knowing God and His attributes, obedient, avoiding sins. He then explains the term 'wali' linguistically. Next, he defines karamah as an extraordinary event through a righteous servant without a claim to prophethood. He affirms that such karamat are established by religious law and rationally permissible, and provides proofs from the stories of Mary, Asif ibn Barkhiya, and Umar ibn al-Khattab, supported by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wali
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Sawi defines a wali as one who upholds the rights of God and His servants, knows God and His attributes, perseveres in obedience, avoids sins, and turns away from worldly pleasures.
Notice that the definition combines outward actions and inward knowledge.
2
Explore the linguistic derivation
Al-Sawi explains that the term 'wali' comes from either undertaking God's service or God undertaking his care. Reflect on how this reinforces the meaning.
3
Define karamah and its ruling
Read the definition of karamah as an extraordinary event at the hand of a righteous servant, not accompanied by a claim to prophethood. Note that belief in karamat is obligatory.
Karamah is distinct from a prophetic miracle (mu‘jizah) because it does not involve a claim to prophethood.
4
Examine the evidence
Al-Sawi gives three examples: Mary giving birth to Jesus without a husband, Asif ibn Barkhiya bringing the throne of Bilqis, and Umar extinguishing a fire. Understand that these are from the Quran and Sunnah and have consensus.
The passage concludes that all such karamat are reported in the Book, Sunnah, and consensus, so faith in them is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جمع وليّ، وهو القائم بحقوق الله تعالى وحقوق العباد حسب الإمكان، وهو معنى قول من قال : هو العارف بالله تعالى وصفاته حسب الإمكان المواظب على الطاعات، المُجتَنِب للمخالفات، المُعرِض عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات. قوله: (جمع وليّ ) : سمّي بذلك؛ لأنَّه تولَّى خدمة الله، أو لأنَّ الله تولى أمره، فلم يكله لغيره طرفة عين. ويجب اعتقاد كراماتهم والكرامة أمر خارق للعادة يظهر على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح، غير مقرون بدعوى النُّبوَّة. قوله : (اعتقاد كراماتهم أي ثبوتها، فهي واقعة شرعاً جائزة عقلاً. ودليل ذلك : قصَّة مريم وولادتها عيسى من غير زوج، وآصف ابن برخيا، وعمر بن الخطاب مع نيل مصر ، ومع النَّار التي ظهرت من جهة المدينة في زمنه، فأشار إليها بردائه فأطفأها، وغير ذلك من كرامات الصَّحابة والتابعين إلى وقتنا هذا. كلُّ ذلك ورد به الكتاب والسنة وأجمعت عليه الأمة قبل ظهور المخالفين، وكلّ ما كان كذلك فالإيمان به واجب.
A gathering of a wali: he is the one who upholds the rights of God Almighty and the rights of servants as much as possible. This is the meaning of the saying of whoever said: he is the knower of God Almighty and His attributes as much as possible, the one who perseveres in acts of obedience, avoids acts of disobedience, and turns away from indulgence in pleasures and desires. His statement: (gathering of a wali): he is named that because he undertakes the service of God, or because God undertakes his affair, so He does not entrust him to anyone else for the blink of an eye. It is obligatory to believe in their karamat (miracles). Karamah is an extraordinary matter that appears at the hand of a servant outwardly righteous, not accompanied by a claim to prophethood. His statement: (belief in their karamat) i.e., their occurrence. So they are established by religious law (shar'an) and permissible by reason (ʿaqlan). The proof of that is the story of Mary and her giving birth to Jesus without a husband, and Asif ibn Barkhiya, and Umar ibn al-Khattab with the Nile of Egypt, and with the fire that appeared from the direction of Medina in his time, and he gestured to it with his cloak and extinguished it, and other karamat of the Companions and Successors up to our time. All of that has been reported in the Book and the Sunnah and the community has agreed upon it before the appearance of the opponents, and everything that is so, faith in it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. Al-Sawi begins by defining the 'wali' (friend of God) as one who fulfills the rights of God and people to the best of his ability, and he equates it with a description: the knower of God and His attributes, consistent in obedience, avoiding sins, and turning away from worldly pleasures. Then he comments on the term 'gathering of a wali' explaining it is derived from undertaking God's service or God undertaking his care. Next, he states the obligation to believe in their karamat (extraordinary events) and defines karamah as an extraordinary event that occurs through a righteous servant without a claim to prophethood. He then comments on 'belief in their karamat' clarifying it means affirming their occurrence, and that they are accepted by religious law and rationally possible. The proof offered includes three examples: the story of Mary giving birth to Jesus without a male partner, Asif ibn Barkhiya bringing the throne of Bilqis quickly, and Umar ibn al-Khattab extinguishing a fire with his cloak. These are given as evidence that such miracles happen for saints. He concludes that all these are established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the early community, making belief in them obligatory upon every Muslim.

Belief in Widespread Prophetic Teachings
Al-Sawi explains the ruling on denying widely transmitted prophetic teachings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when denying a prophetic teaching constitutes disbelief according to al-Sawi.

Core Idea
Only widely known, undoubted teachings from the Prophet necessitate belief; denying them is disbelief.

Al-Sawi clarifies a critical distinction in Islamic theology: not every prophetic teaching carries the same weight. Some have become so universally known that denying them implies rejecting the Prophet's message itself. This passage establishes the boundary between teachings that are necessary to believe and those that allow for difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms 'ishtihar' (widespreadness) and 'daruri' (necessary).
The passage is short but dense. Read it twice to grasp the ruling and the exception.
2
Identify the two categories of teachings
Al-Sawi divides prophetic teachings into two categories based on their level of widespreadness. Find where he states the ruling for each category.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the example given: raising hands in bowing and prostration. Why does not denying this not count as disbelief? What does this teach us about takfir?
This shows that not every disputed matter leads to excommunication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) كذا يجب الإيمان بكلِّ ما جاء أي: روي ونقل عن النبي البشير أي : المبشِّر لمن أوفى بالعهود، بأنه محمود العاقبة من كل حكم بيان لكل ما جاء صار في الاشتهار بين الخاصة والعامة كالأمر الضروري الذي لا يخفى على أحد. قوله : ( في الاشتهار) بيان لوجه الشبه؛ أي: إنَّ الأحكام التي أتى بها النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم واشتهرت حتَّى صارت كالأمور الضرورية يجب الإيمان بها، وكلُّ من أنكر شيئاً منها فقد كفر ، وأمَّا الأحكام التي لم تبلغ في الاشتهار هذا الحد؛ فلا يكفّر منكرها ؛ كالرَّفع من الركوع والسجود، ونحو ذلك.
(And) likewise it is obligatory to believe in everything that came, i.e., that was narrated and transmitted from the Prophet, the giver of glad tidings, i.e., the one who gives glad tidings to whoever fulfills the covenants, that he is praiseworthy in outcome, of every ruling, an explanation for everything that came, it became widespread among the elite and the common people like the necessary matter that is hidden from no one. His statement: (in the widespreadness) is an explanation of the point of similarity; i.e., the rulings that the Prophet, peace be upon him, brought and that became widespread until they became like necessary matters, it is obligatory to believe in them, and whoever denies any of them has disbelieved. As for rulings that did not reach this level of widespreadness, the one who denies them is not declared a disbeliever, such as the raising of the hands in bowing and prostration, and the like.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling. Al-Sawi begins by stating the obligation to believe in everything transmitted from the Prophet that has become widespread ('ishtahar') among the general public and the elite, to the point that it is like necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī). He then explains the point of similarity: such rulings are so well-known that denying them indicates rejection of the Prophet's message, thus it is kufr. However, he makes an important distinction: rulings that have not reached this level of widespreadness are not such that their denier becomes a disbeliever. He gives examples: the raising of hands during bowing and prostration, and similar secondary matters. This guards against takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever) for denying matters that are not universally known.

ضروري
كفر
إنكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying any teaching from the Prophet is kufr.
Correction: Al-Sawi specifies that only teachings that have become widespread like necessary knowledge cause disbelief when denied. Lesser-known teachings do not incur takfir.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Denying a widely transmitted prophetic teaching is kufr; denying a lesser-known one is not.

The Mi'raj and Grave Questioning: Denial and Kufr
Al-Sawi distinguishes between Isra and Mi'raj and rules on denial; introduces grave questioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the night journey (Isra) and the ascension (Mi'raj), the ruling on denying each, and the obligation to believe in the questioning of the grave.

Core Idea
Denying the Isra is disbelief; denying solely the Mi'raj details is sin.

Al-Sawi distinguishes two events from the Prophet's journey: the night journey (Isra) and the ascension (Mi'raj). He clarifies that denying the widely-known Isra constitutes disbelief, while denying specific details of the Mi'raj only makes one a sinner. The passage also affirms that belief in the questioning of the grave by two angels is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the flow. Note the two main topics: the Mi'raj description and the grave questioning.
2
Identify the distinction between Isra and Mi'raj
Look for where the author explains that 'Mi'raj includes the Isra.' Understand that the Mi'raj is the ascent after the Isra, and the Isra is the night journey from Mecca to Jerusalem.
The passage uses the term 'Mi'raj' broadly to include the Isra in the context of the objection.
3
Understand the ruling on denial
Find the part where the author addresses the objection: someone who denies the Mi'raj is a sinner, but how can you rule disbelief? The answer distinguishes: denying the Isra is kufr, denying only the Mi'raj details is fisq.
The key sentence: 'fammnkir al-isra kafir, wamunikir al-mi'raj fasiq'.
4
Note the grave questioning
The passage ends with the mention of the questioning of the two angels in the grave. It is an obligation to believe in it, though the text is incomplete.
This section is brief; the main point is that it is among the necessary beliefs.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that not all denials of prophetic reports are equal; some matters are so universally known that rejecting them places one outside Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- وكالمعراج بجسده الشريف ، يقظة، وهو العروج إلى السماء مع جبريل بلا براق بعد الإسراء، ليلاً من المسجد الحرام إلى المسجد الأقصى راكباً للبراق، وهو دابَّة أبيض طويل، فوق الحمار، ودون البغل، يضع حافره عند منتهى طرفه، والمراد بالمعراج ما يعم الإسراء، وقصته مشهورة. - وكسؤال المَلَكَين بيان أن سؤال القبر حق قوله : (بلا براق هذا هو المعتمد، وقيل: عرج بالبراق. قوله : (والمراد بالمعراج ما يعمُّ الإسراء : جواب عما يقال : إنَّ منكر المعراج ،فاسق فكيف تحكم عليه بالكفر ؟! فأجاب : بأنَّ المراد بالمعراج ما يشمل الإسراء، فمنكر الإسراء كافر، ومنكر المعراج فاسق . قوله : (وكسؤال الملكين) أي فهو ممَّا يجب الإيمان به، لكنَّ منكره
- And like the ascension (mi'raj) with his noble body, awake, which is ascending to the heaven with Gabriel without a Buraq after the night journey (isra), by night from the Sacred Mosque to the Aqsa Mosque riding the Buraq, which is a white long beast, above a donkey and below a mule, placing its hoof at the farthest extent of its gaze. And what is meant by the ascension (mi'raj) is that which includes the night journey (isra), and its story is well-known. - And like the questioning of the two angels, an explanation that the questioning of the grave is true. His saying: (without a Buraq, this is the relied-upon opinion, and it is said: he ascended with the Buraq. His saying: (And what is meant by the ascension (mi'raj) is that which includes the night journey (isra)) is a response to what is said: Indeed, the denier of the ascension is a sinner, so how can you judge him with disbelief?! So he answered: That what is meant by the ascension is that which includes the night journey, so the denier of the night journey is a disbeliever, and the denier of the ascension is a sinner. His saying: (And like the questioning of the two angels) i.e., it is among the things that must be believed, but the denier of it...
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage provides two more examples of widely known matters from the Prophet: the Mi'raj (ascension) and the questioning in the grave by two angels. First, it describes the Mi'raj as an ascent with the Prophet's body while awake, after the Isra (night journey), which involved riding the Buraq from Mecca to Jerusalem. The author notes that the word 'mi'raj' is used in a broad sense to include the Isra. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that denying the Mi'raj only makes one a sinner (fasiq), not a disbeliever. The author answers by distinguishing: the term 'mi'raj' here includes the Isra, which is unanimously known; so denying the Isra is disbelief because it rejects a necessarily known matter. Denying only the specific details of the ascent (e.g., the Buraq) is a sin, not disbelief. Finally, the passage lists the questioning of the two angels in the grave as another obligatory belief, though the text breaks off mid-sentence. The implication is that denying this questioning also carries a ruling, but the full ruling is not given in the excerpt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the Mi'raj is always disbelief.
Correction: Denying only the specific details of the Mi'raj (like the Buraq) is a sin, not disbelief; only denial of the widely-known Isra is disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of someone who denies the night journey (Isra) as opposed to someone who denies only the specific details of the Mi'raj?
A) Both are disbelievers
B) Both are sinners
C) Denier of Isra is a disbeliever; denier of Mi'raj details is a sinner
D) Denier of Isra is a sinner; denier of Mi'raj details is a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The Isra is so widely known that denying it is kufr, while denying the specific details of the Mi'raj is fisq; belief in grave questioning is obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that denying the widely-known Isra is kufr. Do you truly believe in that night journey with all your heart, or are there any doubts lurking? What will you do today to remove any hesitation and strengthen your certainty in what Allah has revealed?
?
The grave questioning is a reality you must face. Are you living as if you will be asked tomorrow about your deeds? If not, what specific change will you make in your life today to prepare for that moment?
?
Denying the details of the Mi'raj is only a sin, not disbelief. But does that make it acceptable in your sight? Examine your attitude: do you take lightly matters of the unseen that are less known, or do you submit wholeheartedly to all that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought?

The Definition of Iman: Linguistic vs. Legal
Al-Sawi defines Iman linguistically and legally, emphasizing inward submission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of Iman as heartfelt assent combined with submission, and why mere intellectual acknowledgment is insufficient.

Core Idea
Iman is not just believing in your mind; it is submitting with your heart.

Al-Sawi begins by distinguishing between the linguistic and legal meanings of Iman. While linguistically Iman is simply 'assent,' the legal definition goes deeper: it requires not only intellectual agreement but also heartfelt submission to the Prophet's message. This distinction is crucial because it explains why some who recognized the Prophet's truthfulness, like Abu Talib, are not considered believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Sawi first gives the linguistic meaning, then the legal, and then emphasizes the necessity of submission.
The passage has some unclear portions due to OCR; focus on the main argument.
2
Understand 'by necessity'
Pay attention to the explanation of 'by necessity' as matters known without reflection, even if they are theoretical in origin. This clarifies the scope of what must be believed.
3
Grasp the condition of submission
The passage stresses that legal Iman requires idh'an (submission) and acceptance, not just cognitive assent. Reflect on the example of Abu Talib.
This is the key point distinguishing Ash'ari thought.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this definition distinguishes Ash'ari theology from other schools that might define faith as mere belief.

أولاً : تعريف الإيمان : واعلم أنَّ التصديق بما ذُكر هو الإيمان الشَّرعي ؛ لأنَّ الإيمان : لغة : هو مطلق التصديق . وشرعاً : هو تصديق النَّبي الله وبالقلب في جميع ما علم مجيئه به صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدين بالضرورة :أي: فيما اشتهر بين أهل الإسلام وصار العِلْمُ به يشابه العلم الحاصل بالضّرورة، بحيث يعلمه العامَّةُ من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال، وإن كان في أصله نظرياً ؛ كوحدة الصانع جلَّ وعلا، الصَّلاة ونحوها إجمالاً فيما عُلم إجمالاً، وتفصيلاً فيما ووجوب علم كذلك . والمراد من تصديقه عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: الإذعان والقبول لما جاء به؛ بحيث يقع عليه اسم التسليم من غير تكبر وعِناد، لا مجرَّدَ وقوع نِسبة الصدق إليه في القلب من غير إذعان وقبول، حتَّى يلزم إيمان كثير من الكفَّار الذين كانوا عالمين بحقيقة نبوّته عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام وما جاء به ؛ لأنَّهم لم يكونوا أذعنوا لذلك ولا قبلوه بحيث :قوله : (لا مجرَّد وقوع نسبة الصدق . . . إلخ) : أي : كما يقول السعد وسيأتي له توجيهه بتكلفات. قوله : (كثير من الكفَّار أي كأبي طالب ؛ فإنَّه كان يشهد له بالصدق من غير إذعان .
First: Definition of Iman. Know that the tasdiq (assent) mentioned is the legal Iman, because Iman linguistically is absolute tasdiq. Legally, it is tasdiq of the Prophet of Allah with the heart in everything that is known to have been brought by him (peace be upon him) from religion by necessity: i.e., what is widespread among the people of Islam and knowledge of which resembles necessary knowledge, such that the common people know it without needing reflection and reasoning, even if it is theoretical in origin; like the oneness of the Creator (mighty and sublime), prayer and the like, in general what is known in general, and in detail what is known in detail, and similarly the obligation of knowledge. And the intended meaning of tasdiq of him (peace and blessings be upon him) is idh'an (submission) and acceptance of what he brought, such that the name of submission (taslim) applies to it without arrogance and obstinacy, not just the occurrence of the attribution of truthfulness to him in the heart without submission and acceptance, for that would entail the faith of many disbelievers who were knowledgeable of the reality of his prophethood (peace and blessings be upon him) and what he brought, because they did not submit to that nor accept it. His statement: (Not just the occurrence of the attribution of truthfulness ... etc.) meaning: as al-Sa'd says, and his interpretation will come with unnecessary complications. His statement: (Many disbelievers, like Abu Talib, for he used to testify to his truthfulness without submission.)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines Iman (faith) both linguistically and legally. Linguistically, Iman is absolute tasdiq (assent). Legally, it is heartfelt tasdiq of everything known to be brought by the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) from religion by necessity (bi al-darura). The author clarifies 'by necessity' as matters that have become widespread among Muslims and known without need for reflection, though they may be theoretical in origin, such as the oneness of God and the obligation of prayer. The author then emphasizes that the intended meaning of tasdiq is not mere intellectual assent but idh'an (submission) and acceptance (qabul) of what the Prophet brought, accompanied by taslim (submission) without arrogance or obstinacy. Mere recognition of truthfulness in the heart is insufficient; otherwise, many disbelievers like Abu Talib (who acknowledged the Prophet's truthfulness but did not submit) would be considered believers. The commentary notes that this point is also made by al-Sa'd and will be further explained.

Defining Iman: Linguistic and Legal, and the Necessity of Submission
Al-Sawi defines iman linguistically and legally, emphasizing submission over mere acknowledgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that legal iman (al-iman al-shar'i) consists of heartfelt assent that includes submission and acceptance, not mere intellectual acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Iman is not just knowing the truth but surrendering to it from the heart.

Before we can discuss who is a believer and who is not, we must first define what iman actually means. Al-Sawi begins by distinguishing the linguistic sense of 'assent' from the specific legal meaning that includes heartfelt submission. This distinction is crucial to avoid counting those who simply knew the Prophet was truthful—like many disbelievers—as believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic and legal definitions
Start by reading the first few lines where al-Sawi gives the linguistic and legal definitions of iman. Notice that the legal definition is more specific: it requires assenting to everything the Prophet brought, known by necessity.
The phrase 'by necessity' refers to matters that are so widely known that even common people know them without proof.
2
Understand the requirement of submission
Next, al-Sawi explains that the intended assent (tasdiq) means submission (idh'ān) and acceptance (qubūl), not merely attributing truthfulness in the heart. He gives an example: if it were mere acknowledgment, many disbelievers who knew the Prophet's truthfulness would be believers.
This is the key point that separates iman from mere knowledge.
3
Identify the conclusion about inner speech
Al-Sawi then concludes that legal iman is 'inner speech following knowledge' (hadith al-nafs al-tabi' li al-ma'rifa), which is an act of the soul, not a type of knowledge. This sets up the later debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً : تعريف الإيمان : واعلم أنَّ التصديق بما ذُكر هو الإيمان الشَّرعي ؛ لأنَّ الإيمان : لغة : هو مطلق التصديق . وشرعاً : هو تصديق النبي الله وبالقلب في جميع ما علم مجيئه به صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدين بالضرورة :أي: فيما اشتهر بين أهل الإسلام وصار العِلْمُ به يشابه العلم الحاصل بالضّرورة، بحيث يعلمه العامَّةُ من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال، وإن كان في أصله نظرياً ؛ كوحدة الصانع جلَّ وعلا، الصَّلاة ونحوها إجمالاً فيما عُلم إجمالاً، وتفصيلاً فيما ووجوب علم كذلك . والمراد من تصديقه عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام: الإذعان والقبول لما جاء به؛ بحيث يقع عليه اسم التسليم من غير تكبر وعِناد، لا مجرَّدَ وقوع نِسبة الصدق إليه في القلب من غير إذعان وقبول، حتَّى يلزم إيمان كثير من الكفَّار الذين كانوا عالمين بحقيقة نبوّته عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام وما جاء به ؛ لأنَّهم لم يكونوا أذعنوا لذلك ولا قبلوه بحيث يطلق عليه اسم التسليم . وعلى هذا فالإيمان الشَّرعيُّ هو : حديث النَّفْس التابع للمعرفة؛ أي : الإدراك ،الجازم، بناء على الصحيح من أنَّ إيمان المقلّد صحيح . فالإذعانُ والقبول والتصديق والتسليم عبارات عن شيء واحد، وهو : حديث النَّفس المذكور، فيكون الإيمان فعلاً من أفعال النّفس، وليس من قبيل العلوم والمعارف، ويظهر من كلام بعضهم أنَّه الراجح . وذهب المحقق التفتازاني وكثير من المحققين إلى أنَّ التصديق الشَّرعيّ المعبر عنه بالإيمان والإذعان والتسليم هو: نفس الإدراك، فيكون من قبيل العلوم والمعارف، والأصح في الإدراك أنَّه كيف لا فعل ولا انفعال للنّفس، ويكون التكليف به باعتبار أسبابه من الفكر الموصل إليه .
First: Definition of Iman: Know that the assent (al-tasdiq) mentioned is legal iman (al-iman al-shar'i). Because iman, linguistically, is absolute assent. Legally, it is assenting to the Prophet of God, with the heart, in everything known to have been brought by him (peace be upon him) from religion by necessity, i.e., in what is well-known among the people of Islam, and knowledge of it resembles knowledge obtained by necessity, such that the common people know it without needing reflection and inference, even if in origin it is theoretical; like the oneness of the Creator (mighty and sublime), prayer, and the like, in summary for what is known summarily, and in detail for what is known in detail, and the obligation of knowledge likewise. And what is meant by assenting to him (peace be upon him) is submission (al-idh'ān) and acceptance (al-qubūl) of what he brought, such that the name of surrender (al-taslīm) applies to it, without arrogance and stubbornness; not merely the occurrence of attributing truthfulness to him in the heart without submission and acceptance, lest it entail the iman of many disbelievers who knew the reality of his prophethood (peace be upon him) and what he brought, because they did not submit to that nor accept it such that the name of surrender applies to it. And on this basis, legal iman is the inner speech (ḥadīth al-nafs) following knowledge (al-maʿrifa), i.e., definite perception (al-idrāk al-jāzim), based on the sound view that the iman of the muqallid is valid. So submission, acceptance, assent, and surrender are expressions for one thing, namely the mentioned inner speech. Thus iman is an act of the soul (fiʿl min afʿāl al-nafs), not of the category of sciences and cognitions. And it appears from the speech of some that this is the preponderant view. And the accomplished al-Taftazani and many accomplished scholars held that legal assent, expressed as iman, submission, and surrender, is the perception itself (nafs al-idrāk), so it is of the category of sciences and cognitions. And the correct view regarding perception is that it is a quality (kayf) that is neither an act nor a passive state of the soul, and obligation (al-taklīf) pertains to it by consideration of its causes, namely the thought leading to it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of iman (faith) in Islamic law, followed by a theological debate about its nature. The author begins by stating that the 'assent' previously mentioned is what constitutes legal iman (al-iman al-shar'i). He then gives the linguistic definition: 'absolute assent' (muṭlaq al-taṣdīq). The legal definition is more specific: assenting with the heart to everything known to have been brought by the Prophet (peace be upon him) from religion by necessity—i.e., matters so widely known among Muslims that knowledge of them resembles necessary knowledge, such that even the common people know them without needing proof, even if originally they are theoretical (such as God's oneness, prayer, etc.). The author clarifies that the intended assent is not mere intellectual acknowledgment but includes submission (idh'ān) and acceptance (qubūl), such that the term 'surrender' (taslīm) applies—without arrogance or stubbornness. If it were mere acknowledgment, many disbelievers (like Abu Talib) who knew the Prophet's truthfulness but did not submit would be considered believers, which is false. On this basis, legal iman is defined as 'inner speech following knowledge' (ḥadīth al-nafs al-tābiʿ li al-maʿrifa), i.e., definite perception, based on the sound opinion that the iman of a muqallid (one who follows without proof) is valid. Thus, submission, acceptance, assent, and surrender are synonyms for this inner speech, making iman an act of the soul, not a type of knowledge. The author then notes that some scholars consider this the preponderant view. He then presents the alternative view of al-Taftazani and many other accomplished scholars: legal assent (taṣdīq sharʿī) is the perception itself, placing it in the category of sciences and cognitions. On this view, perception is a quality (kayf) that is neither an act nor a passive state of the soul, and the obligation (taklīf) to it applies by considering its causes (i.e., the thought leading to it). This addresses the objection that one cannot be obligated to have a quality that is not an action.

التسليم
حديث النفس

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage states that iman requires not just knowing but submitting and accepting (idh'ān wa qubūl), such that the name 'surrender' applies.

If iman is an act of the soul, not just a set of beliefs, what are you doing actively to train your soul in submission? Name one small habit you can start today—such as immediate obedience to a sunnah or accepting a difficult decree—that will move you from passive knowledge to active faith.

The Nature of Iman: Act of the Soul or Knowledge?
Al-Sawi presents two views on the nature of iman: as an act of the soul vs. as knowledge, with elaboration on obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on whether iman is an act of the soul (the preponderant view) or a type of knowledge (the view of al-Taftazani), and how obligation (taklif) is understood in each.

Core Idea
Is iman something you do with your soul, or is it what you know? Scholars disagreed.

After defining iman as inner speech and an act of the soul, al-Sawi reports a significant debate. Some scholars, including al-Taftazani, argue that the assent in iman is the perception itself, placing it in the category of knowledge. This raises the question: how can one be obligated to have a perception that is not an action? The passage explores these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the preponderant view
Begin with the first view: after concluding that iman is inner speech, al-Sawi notes that this implies iman is an act of the soul, not knowledge. He adds that this appears to be the preponderant (rajih) view based on some scholars.
The term 'rajih' indicates that this is the stronger opinion according to some.
2
Contrast with al-Taftazani's view
Then read the opposing view: al-Taftazani and many accomplished scholars said that legal assent is the perception itself (nafs al-idrak), making it a type of knowledge or cognition.
Al-Taftazani was a major scholar of kalam and logic.
3
Understand the explanation of perception and obligation
Finally, al-Sawi clarifies that on this second view, perception is a quality (kayf) that is neither an act nor a passive state of the soul. Obligation applies not to the perception itself but to its causes, i.e., the thought leading to it.
This resolves the issue of how one can be commanded to have a perception.

This passage is a definition and explanation of iman (faith) in Islamic law, followed by a theological debate about its nature. The author begins by stating that the 'assent' previously mentioned is what constitutes legal iman (al-iman al-shar'i). He then gives the linguistic definition: 'absolute assent' (muṭlaq al-taṣdīq). The legal definition is more specific: assenting with the heart to everything known to have been brought by the Prophet (peace be upon him) from religion by necessity—i.e., matters so widely known among Muslims that knowledge of them resembles necessary knowledge, such that even the common people know them without needing proof, even if originally they are theoretical (such as God's oneness, prayer, etc.). The author clarifies that the intended assent is not mere intellectual acknowledgment but includes submission (idh'ān) and acceptance (qubūl), such that the term 'surrender' (taslīm) applies—without arrogance or stubbornness. If it were mere acknowledgment, many disbelievers (like Abu Talib) who knew the Prophet's truthfulness but did not submit would be considered believers, which is false. On this basis, legal iman is defined as 'inner speech following knowledge' (ḥadīth al-nafs al-tābiʿ li al-maʿrifa), i.e., definite perception, based on the sound opinion that the iman of a muqallid (one who follows without proof) is valid. Thus, submission, acceptance, assent, and surrender are synonyms for this inner speech, making iman an act of the soul, not a type of knowledge. The author then notes that some scholars consider this the preponderant view. He then presents the alternative view of al-Taftazani and many other accomplished scholars: legal assent (taṣdīq sharʿī) is the perception itself, placing it in the category of sciences and cognitions. On this view, perception is a quality (kayf) that is neither an act nor a passive state of the soul, and the obligation (taklīf) to it applies by considering its causes (i.e., the thought leading to it). This addresses the objection that one cannot be obligated to have a quality that is not an action.

Key Terms
الإدراك
التكليف
الفعل النفساني
المحقق التفتازاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman must be a voluntary action; if it is a perception, we cannot be commanded to have it.
Correction: The passage explains that on the second view, perception is a quality (not an action). Obligation applies to its causes (thought), thus preserving moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's view, how is iman understood?
A) As an act of the soul, called inner speech
B) As the perception itself, which is a quality
C) As a passive state of the soul
D) As a combination of action and knowledge

Key Takeaway
There is a scholarly disagreement on whether iman is an act of the soul or a perception; the latter view handles obligation by focusing on the causes of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman is a perception that arises from reflection, what specific thoughts are you actively cultivating to bring about that perception in your own heart?
?
The scholars linked obligation to the causes of iman, not the iman itself. Are you attending to the causes—seeking knowledge, pondering—or are you waiting for iman to descend without effort?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that perception is neither an act nor a passive state. This means your iman is not something you can fake or force. Does this humble you, or does it drive you to take the means more seriously?

Faith as Inner Speech: The Ash'ari Definition
Al-Sawi defines iman al-shar'i as hadith al-nafs and states the preponderant view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman (faith) in Islamic theology is defined as inner speech (hadith al-nafs) resulting from knowledge, and that this is the preponderant view among Ash'ari theologians.

Core Idea
Faith is an act of the heart, not mere knowledge.

In this lesson, we examine a key definition of iman from the Ash'ari tradition. Al-Sawi presents the view that faith is inner speech (hadith al-nafs) that follows from knowledge. This distinguishes faith from mere intellectual assent and grounds it as an act of the soul. The passage also notes that this is the preponderant view held by major scholars.

1

The passage is concise but dense.

Note that it is based on the correct view that a blind follower's faith is valid.

This adds authority to the definition.

يُطلَق عليه اسم التّسليم . وعلى هذا فالإيمانُ الشَّرعيُّ هو : حديث النَّفْس التابع للمعرفة؛ أي : الإدراك ،الجازم، بناء على الصحيح من أنَّ إيمان المقلّد صحيح . فالإذعانُ والقبول والتصديق والتسليم عبارات عن شيء واحد، وهو : حديث النَّفْس المذكور، فيكون الإيمان فعلاً من أفعال النّفس، وليس من قبيل العلوم والمعارف، ويظـهـر مـن كـلام بـعـضـهـم الراجح . قوله : ( ويظهر من كلام بعضهم أنَّه الرَّاجح ) : أي : لأنَّه قول الأشعري وأبي بكر الباقلاني وأبي إسحاق الإسفرايني وجمهور المتكلمين.
It is called by the name 'submission'. And upon this, legal faith (al-iman al-sharʿi) is: inner speech (hadith al-nafs) resulting from knowledge (maʿrifa), i.e., decisive perception (al-idrak al-jazim), based on the correct view that the faith of the blind follower (muqallid) is valid. His saying (upon his saying "and it appears from the speech of some of them that it is the preponderant"): that is, because it is the statement of al-Ashʿari, Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, and the majority of the theologians (mutakallimun).

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of legal faith (al-iman al-sharʿi) and clarifies its nature. The author first states that faith is called 'submission' (taslim). Then he defines it as inner speech (hadith al-nafs) that follows from knowledge (maʿrifa), meaning decisive perception (al-idrak al-jazim). This definition is based on the correct position that the faith of a blind follower (muqallid) who merely accepts without independent reasoning is valid. The author then equates terms: compliance (idhʿan), acceptance (qabul), assent (tasdiq), and submission (taslim) all refer to the same inner speech. Therefore, faith is an act of the soul, not a kind of knowledge or science. The passage then notes that this view is considered preponderant by some scholars, and the commentary clarifies that these scholars include al-Ashʿari, al-Baqillani, al-Isfara'ini, and the majority of theologians.

Taftazani's View of Tasdiq as Perception and the Sign Objection
Al-Sawi expounds al-Taftazani's view of tasdiq as idrak and resolves an objection with an external sign example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's position that tasdiq is identical to perception (idrak), and how he defends it against the objection that internal perception alone would make all disbelievers believers.

Core Idea
External acts designated by revelation can override internal assent.

Al-Taftazani presents an alternative view on the nature of tasdiq (faith), equating it with mere perception (idrak). This raises a critical objection: if tasdiq is simply knowledge, then anyone who perceives the truth should be a believer, even if they outwardly reject it. In this passage, al-Taftazani resolves the objection by pointing to external signs that the Prophet designated as markers of disbelief, overriding any internal compliance. This discussion refines the relationship between inner assent and outward acts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow from Taftazani's view to the resolution.
Notice the shift from Taftazani's claim to the objection and response.
2
Identify Taftazani's view
Find where Taftazani states that tasdiq is perception (idrak) and that taklif is in consideration of its causes.
He considers tasdiq as a kind of knowledge.
3
Understand the objection
The objection is that if tasdiq is perception, then many disbelievers would be believers because they perceive the truth.
This objection is raised in the commentary.
4
Analyze the resolution
Taftazani responds by giving the example of someone who internally affirms but ties a zunar or prostrates to an idol; the Prophet made those acts signs of denial.
External signs override internal assent.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
Note the commentator's judgment that Taftazani's solution is forced (takalluf) and the correct view is the first.
This shows the ongoing debate in kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب المحقق التفتازاني وكثير من المحققين إلى أنَّ التصديق الشَّرعيّ المعبر عنه بالإيمان والإذعان والتسليم هو: نفس الإدراك، فيكون من قبيل العلوم والمعارف، والأصح في الإدراك أنَّه كيف لا فعل ولا انفعال للنّفس، ويكون التكليف به باعتبار أسبابه من الفكر الموصل إليه . قوله : (وذهب المحقق التفتازاني ...إلخ) : ردَّ ذلك بما تقدَّم في قوله : حتّى يلزم إيمان كثير من الكفار). قوله : (ويكون التكليف به ... إلخ) جواب عما يقال : الكيف وصف قائم بالنفس لا تكليف به وإنَّما التكليف بالأفعال الاختيارية. قال : (وهو معنى التَّصديق المقابل للتَّصوُّر في علم الميزان، حيث يقال : العلم إمَّا تصوُّر وإمَّا تصديق؛ أي فيكون التصديق عند هو الإذعان، بحيث يُطلق عليه اسم التّسليم) (۱) . المناطقة قال: (فلو حصل هذا المعنى للكفَّار كان إطلاق اسم الكافر عليه من جهة أنَّ عليه شيئاً من أمارات التكذيب والإنكار، كما لو فرضنا أنَّ أحداً صدق ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأقرَّ به وعمل ومع ذلك شدَّ بجميع الزنار بالاختيار، أو سجد للصَّنم بالاختيار نجعله كافراً لما أنَّ النبيَّ جعل ذلك علامة التكذيب والإنكار، وتحقيق هذا المقام على ما ذكرتُ يسهل لك الطريق إلى حلّ كثير من الإشكالات الموردة في مسألة الإيمان انتهى كلامه (۲) . قوله : (قال) : أي : السَّعد دافعاً ما يرد عليه من الإشكال، وهو إن قلت : إنَّه الإدراك، يلزم عليه أنه يكفي وإن لم يكن عنده إذعان، فأجاب بقوله : فلو حصل . . . إلخ ، فتدبَّر . قوله: (وتحقيق هذا المقام ... إلخ): قد علمت أنَّ مذهبه تكلُّف، فالحقُ الأول.
And al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani and many investigators went to that legal assent (al-tasdiq al-sharʿi) expressed by faith, compliance, and submission is: the very perception (idrak), so it is of the category of sciences and cognitions. And the correct regarding perception is that it is a howness (kayf) that is neither an action nor a passion of the soul, and obligation (taklif) with it is by consideration of its causes from thought that leads to it. His saying ("and al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani went... etc."): he refuted that by what preceded in his saying: "until it would necessitate the faith of many disbelievers". His saying ("and obligation with it... etc."): it is a response to what is said: the howness is an attribute subsisting in the soul, there is no obligation for it; obligation is only for voluntary acts. He said: "It is the meaning of tasdiq opposed to tasawwur in the science of logic (mizan), where it is said: knowledge is either tasawwur or tasdiq; that is, tasdiq according to the logicians is compliance, such that it is called by the name of submission." He said: "If this meaning occurred for the disbelievers, the application of the name 'disbeliever' to him would be from the aspect that there is upon him some signs of denial and rejection, as if we suppose that someone affirmed what the Prophet came with and acknowledged it and acted, yet he deliberately tied the zunar (Zoroastrian belt) or prostrated to an idol deliberately, we would judge him a disbeliever because the Prophet made that a sign of denial and rejection. And the verification of this station, as I have mentioned, makes easy for you the way to solving many of the problems raised in the issue of faith." His saying ("he said"): i.e., al-Saʿd, repelling the objection that arises against him, which is: if you say that it is perception, it would entail that it suffices even if he does not have compliance, so he answered with his saying: "if this meaning occurred... etc.", so reflect. His saying ("and the verification of this station... etc."): you have known that his view is forced, so the correct view is the first.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents al-Taftazani's alternative view on the nature of tasdiq and then resolves a key objection. The passage first states al-Taftazani's position: legal assent (tasdiq) is identical to perception (idrak), placing it in the category of knowledge and sciences. It clarifies that perception is a 'howness' (kayf) that is neither an act nor a passion of the soul, and that taklif (obligation) regarding it is based on its causes—the thought that leads to it. The commentary then notes that al-Taftazani's view is refuted by an earlier argument that it would entail the faith of many disbelievers. Next, a quote from al-Saʿd (al-Taftazani) explains that the logicians' tasdiq is compliance (idhʿan), distinct from tasawwur. This leads to an objection: if tasdiq is perception, then someone could have perception without compliance and still be called a believer. Al-Taftazani's answer is that even if the meaning of tasdiq (compliance) occurs for disbelievers, they are still called disbelievers because of external signs (amarat) of denial, such as wearing a zunar or prostrating to an idol. The Prophet made these acts signs of denial, overriding any internal compliance. The passage concludes with the commentator's judgment that al-Taftazani's solution is forced (takalluf), and the correct view is the first (that tasdiq is inner speech, not mere perception).

Correction: The passage shows that external acts designated by revelation can override internal assent; internal assent does not automatically make one a believer if outward signs of denial are present.

Definition of Tasdiq as Inner Conviction
Defines tasdiq as inner conviction and submission in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of tasdiq (assent) in Islamic logic and theology as inner conviction and submission, distinct from mere conceptualization.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is not just knowing; it is the heart's surrender.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a key term: tasdiq. In logic, knowledge is divided into conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). But what exactly is tasdiq? It is not mere cognition; it is inner conviction and submission. This is the first building block for understanding faith and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines tasdiq in relation to tasawwur.
The passage is a quotation from a commentary on al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
The passage states that knowledge is either conceptualization (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). Understand the distinction: tasawwur is forming a concept, while tasdiq is affirming or denying something.
This is a standard division in Islamic logic (mantiq).
3
Focus on the definition of tasdiq
The key definition is that tasdiq is idh'an (inner conviction) and taslim (submission). This is more than just mental agreement; it involves the heart's acceptance.
This definition is crucial for later discussions on faith (iman).

He said: (It is the meaning of tasdiq (assent) which is opposed to tasawwur (conceptualization) in the science of logic (mizan), where it is said: knowledge is either conceptualization or assent; i.e., tasdiq with them is inner conviction (idh'an), such that the name of submission (taslim) is applied to it.) (1)
The logicians (al-mantaqiqa)
He said: (If this meaning were to occur to the disbelievers, the name of disbeliever would be applied to them because of the presence upon them of some signs of denial and rejection, just as if we suppose that someone believed what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought and affirmed it and acted upon it, and yet with that he girded himself with the zunnar (Zoroastrian belt) deliberately, or prostrated to an idol deliberately, we would judge him a disbeliever because the Prophet made that a sign of denial and rejection. His words: (He said): i.e., al-Sa'd, repelling what is objected to him, which is: If you say that it (faith) is perception (idrak), then it would be sufficient even if he does not have inner conviction. So he answered with his words: "For if this meaning..." etc. So ponder.
His words: (And the verification of this station... etc.): You have known that his position is forced (takalluf), so the truth is the first.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a quotation from a commentary on al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya and the hashia author's commentary. The passage first defines tasdiq as inner conviction (idh'an) and submission (taslim), distinguishing it from mere conceptualization. Then it presents a defense against an objection: if faith is merely inner conviction, then a person who inwardly believes but outwardly performs acts of disbelief should be considered a believer, but the Shari'a still judges him a disbeliever. The response is that such acts are signs (amarat) of denial, so the person is legally a disbeliever even if his inner state is belief. The hashia author notes that this position is forced (takalluf), implying the first definition is correct.

Outward Signs of Disbelief and the Legal Judgment
Addresses an objection about outward acts overriding inner belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic Law can judge a person as a disbeliever based on outward acts even if he inwardly believes, and why this does not contradict the definition of faith as inner conviction.

Core Idea
Outward acts of disbelief are signs that override inner belief in legal judgment.

The passage raises a sharp objection: if tasdiq is inner conviction, then someone who inwardly believes but performs acts of disbelief seems to be a believer. But Islamic Law judges them as disbeliever. How is this resolved? The solution lies in understanding that such acts are established signs (amarat) of denial, making the person legally a disbeliever. This lesson examines that argument and the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the hypothetical example: a person who believes in the Prophet yet deliberately wears the zunnar or prostrates to an idol.
The example is extreme to make the point clear.
2
Understand the concept of 'signs of denial'
The response explains that such acts are a sign of denial and rejection (amarat al-takdhib wa al-inkar). Because the Prophet legislated them as signs, the person is legally a disbeliever even if his inner state is different.
This is a key principle: Shari'a rulings can be based on outward manifestations.
3
Evaluate the hashia author's critique
The hashia author notes that this defence is forced (takalluf) and that the truth is the first view (that inner conviction alone is the criterion for belief). This shows differing opinions among scholars.
Some scholars hold that outward acts can never override inner belief, but the majority consider such acts as forfeiting the legal status of a believer.

He said: (It is the meaning of tasdiq (assent) which is opposed to tasawwur (conceptualization) in the science of logic (mizan), where it is said: knowledge is either conceptualization or assent; i.e., tasdiq with them is inner conviction (idh'an), such that the name of submission (taslim) is applied to it.) (1)
The logicians (al-mantaqiqa)
He said: (If this meaning were to occur to the disbelievers, the name of disbeliever would be applied to them because of the presence upon them of some signs of denial and rejection, just as if we suppose that someone believed what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought and affirmed it and acted upon it, and yet with that he girded himself with the zunnar (Zoroastrian belt) deliberately, or prostrated to an idol deliberately, we would judge him a disbeliever because the Prophet made that a sign of denial and rejection. And the verification of this station, according to what I have mentioned, will ease for you the way to solving many of the objections raised in the issue of faith.) End of his words (2).
His words: (He said): i.e., al-Sa'd, repelling what is objected to him, which is: If you say that it (faith) is perception (idrak), then it would be sufficient even if he does not have inner conviction. So he answered with his words: "For if this meaning..." etc. So ponder.
His words: (And the verification of this station... etc.): You have known that his position is forced (takalluf), so the truth is the first.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a quotation from a commentary on al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya and the hashia author's commentary. The passage first defines tasdiq as inner conviction (idh'an) and submission (taslim), distinguishing it from mere conceptualization. Then it presents a defense against an objection: if faith is merely inner conviction, then a person who inwardly believes but outwardly performs acts of disbelief should be considered a believer, but the Shari'a still judges him a disbeliever. The response is that such acts are signs (amarat) of denial, so the person is legally a disbeliever even if his inner state is belief. The hashia author notes that this position is forced (takalluf), implying the first definition is correct.

The Simplicity of Faith and the Role of Pronouncement
Explains the simplicity of faith and the ruling on pronouncement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith (iman) is simple according to the Ash'ari view, and that pronouncement of the shahada is a condition of perfection, not a condition of validity or an essential part.

Core Idea
Faith is simple; pronouncement perfects it.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a foundational point about the nature of faith. Drawing on the definition of iman as inner conviction, he explains that the verbal declaration is not what makes one a believer, but rather what completes and manifests that belief. This has important implications for understanding who is considered a believer in the sight of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that faith is simple
The passage begins by stating that faith is 'basit' (simple), meaning it is not composed of multiple parts. This sets up the discussion about the role of pronouncement.
This is based on the definition of iman as inner speech or knowledge.
2
Examine the case of a believer who does not utter
Read the scenario of someone who truly believes in his heart but does not pronounce the shahada with his tongue. Note the conditions: not due to an excuse or refusal, and he would utter if asked.
This person is a believer and saved from eternal punishment.
3
Distinguish between conditions of perfection and validity
The passage clarifies that pronouncement is a condition of perfection (shart kamal), like other acts of worship, not a condition of validity (shart sihha) nor a part of the essence of faith.
The exception 'yes, it is a condition' indicates it is still required in some sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: النّطق بالشهادتين والخلاف فيه
وعلى ما ذكرنا فالإيمان بسيط، وعليه فمن صدَّق بقلبه، ولم يُقرَّ بلسانه لا لعذر مَنَعه ولا لاباء، بل كان بحيث لو طلب منه النُّطق لأجاب؛ فهو مؤمن عند الله تعالى، ناج من الخلود في النار .
فالنُّطقُ : إنَّما هو شرط كمال فيه كبقية الأعمال من صلاة وصوم وزكاة وحجّ، لا شرط صحة، ولا جزءاً من حقيقته، نعم هو شرط
قوله: (وعلى ما ذكرنا ) : أي : على كلِّ من التّعريفين اللذين هما حديث النَّفس التابع للمعرفة أو هو المعرفة
قوله :لا لعذر :أي: وأمَّا المعذور فمتّفق على قبول الإيمان منه ولو على القول بأنَّه مركب.
قوله : (ولا) لإباء) : أي : لأن الآبي كافر بالإجماع.
قوله : (نعم هو شرط) : استدراك على قوله : (إنَّما هو شرط كمال فيه) ويؤيده قوله تعالى : أُولَبِكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَنَ) (۱) ، وقوله عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : «اللَّهمَّ؛ ثبت قلبي على دينك»(۲)
قال شيخنا الأمير : سمعنا من المشايخ كثيراً : أنَّ المدار عند المالكية على أي لفظ يفيد الوحدانية والرِّسالة، ونقله اللقاني في «شرحه» عن
Secondly: The pronouncement of the two shahadas and the disagreement concerning it.
And according to what we have mentioned, faith is simple (basit). Accordingly, whoever believes in his heart and does not utter with his tongue, not due to an excuse preventing him nor due to refusal (iba'), but rather such that if he were asked to utter he would respond, then he is a believer in the sight of Allah, saved from eternity in the Fire.
So pronouncement is only a condition of perfection (shart kamal) in it, like the rest of the deeds: prayer, fasting, zakat, and hajj. It is not a condition of validity (shart sihha) nor a part of its essence. Yes, it is a condition...
His words: (And according to what we have mentioned): i.e., according to each of the two definitions: that it is inner speech (hadith al-nafs) following knowledge, or that it is knowledge itself.
His words: (not due to an excuse): i.e., as for the excused one, it is agreed upon that faith is accepted from him, even on the view that it (faith) is composite.
His words: (and not due to refusal): i.e., because the one who refuses is a disbeliever by consensus.
His words: (Yes, it is a condition): an exception to his statement: 'It is only a condition of perfection in it,' and it is supported by His statement: 'Those – He has written faith in their hearts' (Qur'an 58:22) and his (peace be upon him) saying: 'O Allah, establish my heart upon Your religion' (hadith).
Our teacher al-Amir said: we heard from the shaykhs many times that the criterion according to the Malikis is any wording that conveys tawhid and risala, and al-Laqqani transmitted it in his Sharh from...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of faith (iman) and the role of the verbal pronouncement of the shahada. It begins by stating that faith is simple (basit), meaning it is not composite of multiple parts. Then it addresses the case of a person who believes in his heart but does not utter with his tongue. If the lack of utterance is due to an excuse (like coercion or inability) or not out of refusal, and he would utter if asked, then he is still a believer in Allah's sight and saved from eternal punishment. The passage then clarifies that pronouncement is a condition of perfection (shart kamal) in faith, similar to other acts of worship, not a condition of validity (shart sihha) nor an essential part. However, it adds that it is a condition (in some sense) – the commentary notes that the phrase 'Yes, it is a condition' is an exception to the previous statement, indicating that pronouncement is still a condition in terms of legal ruling. The commentary then explains the reference to the two definitions of faith: inner speech following knowledge, or knowledge itself. It also distinguishes the excused person (accepted) from the one who refuses (disbeliever). Finally, it notes a Maliki view that the criterion is any wording indicating tawhid and risala.

Correction: The passage clarifies that pronouncement is a condition of perfection, not a condition of validity or an essential part. A person who believes in his heart but does not utter due to an excuse is still a believer.

Key Takeaway
Faith is inner conviction; pronouncement perfects it but is not its essence.

The Opinion that Faith is Composite of Attestation and Utterance
Al-Sawi mentions the alternative view that iman is composed of attestation and utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative view that iman is composed of inner attestation and verbal utterance, and how it differs from the simple definition.

Core Idea
Faith as a composite of heart and tongue: a debated addition.

In this lesson, we encounter a competing definition of iman. While the author's position favors a simple definition (mere attestation), al-Sawi mentions a view that adds the utterance of the shahadahs. This brief passage alerts us to a key disagreement among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic phrase 'وقيل : إِنَّه مركَّب من التصديق والنُّطق بالشهادتين' and its translation. Notice the structure: 'it is said' introduces an alternative view.
2
Identify the two components
The composite view says iman has two parts: inner attestation (tasdiq) and verbal utterance (nutaq) of the two shahadahs. Recognize that this adds a verbal element to the simple definition.
3
Understand the disagreement
Al-Sawi then comments that this is 'the disagreement' (al-khilaf). This signals that scholars differ on whether utterance is a necessary component of faith.
4
Reflect on the author's stance
Think about why the author might simply report this view without elaboration. It suggests the author's own position leans toward the simple definition, but the composite view is acknowledged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إِنَّه مركَّب من التصديق والنُّطق بالشهادتين 
 قوله : (وقيل : إنَّه مركَّب من التصديق والنُّطق . . . إلخ) : هذا الخلاف
And it is said: It is composed of attestation and utterance of the two shahadahs. His saying: (And it is said: It is composed of attestation and utterance ... etc.): This is the disagreement.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents a competing definition of iman. After having established the simple view, al-Sawi notes that 'it is said' (qila) that iman is composite (murakkab), consisting of both inner attestation (tasdiq) and verbal utterance (nutaq) of the two shahadahs. He then adds his own commentary on this statement, indicating that this is a point of disagreement (khilaf). The brevity suggests that al-Sawi is simply acknowledging the existence of this view without elaborating, as the position of the author (likely al-Kharidat) favors the simple definition. This serves to alert the reader to the differing opinions among scholars.

Correction: The text explicitly states that this is a point of disagreement (al-khilaf). The author's context favors the simple definition, so the composite view is only one of several positions.

Is Verbal Utterance Part of Iman or a Condition?
Commentary presenting three views on the composition of iman and clarifying the part-condition distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views on whether verbal utterance (nutq) is an integral part of iman or merely a condition for its validity, and the metaphysical distinction between 'part' and 'condition.'

Core Idea
The debate over nutq is about whether utterance is inside or outside the essence of faith.

Faith (iman) is defined as tasdiq (heartfelt affirmation), but scholars debated whether verbal utterance of the shahada is a necessary component or an external requirement. This lesson explores the key views from Abu Hanifa, the Ash'aris, and the Mu'tazila, and clarifies the technical difference between a part (juz') and a condition (shart) of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the full translation. Notice the commentator's structure: he introduces a view, explains it, and then adds a third view.
The passage is a commentary on a line from the matn.
2
Identify the three views
The commentator lists three views on the composition of iman: (1) nutq as a part, (2) nutq as a condition, (3) nutq + action as parts. Note how the first two reduce to one in meaning.
Focus on the distinction between part (juz') and condition (shart).
3
Understand the part-condition distinction
The commentator explains that a part is internal to the essence (dakhil fi al-mahiyya), while a condition is external (kharij 'anha). This is not a mere verbal dispute but a real metaphysical difference.
Reflect on why this distinction matters for the definition of iman.
4
Connect to the Mu'tazili view
The third view (with action) leads to serious consequences: abandoning obligations or committing sins makes one a disbeliever. See how this differs from the other views.
This shows how definitions of iman affect rulings on apostasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إنَّه مركَّب من التصديق والنُّطق بالشهادتين. قوله : (وقيل : إنَّه مركَّب من التصديق والنُّطق . . . إلخ) : هذا الخلاف فالنطق جزء من حقيقته إلّا أن التصديق جزء لا يحتمل السقوط والإقرار قد يحتمله كما في المعذور من خرس أو إكراه. وقيل : بل النُّطق شرط صحة له، ولا فرق بينه وبين القول بالجزئية، إلا باعتبار أنّ الجزء داخل الماهيَّة، والشَّرط خارج عنها . قوله : ( فالنُّطق جزء من حقيقته هذا القول لأبي حنيفة وجماعة من الأشاعرة، فالإيمان عندهم اسم لعملي القلب واللسان جميعاً. قوله : (وقيل: بل النُّطق شرط صحة ... إلخ) : تحصّل أنَّ الأقوال ثلاثة، لكنَّها ترجع إلى قولين لأنَّ من قال : إنَّه شرط صحة؛ فقد وافق القائل في المعنى بأنَّه شطر، وبقي قول ثالث: وهو أنَّ الإيمان مركَّب من تصديق، ونطق، وعمل وهو للمعتزلة، وعليه فمن ترك واجباً كالصَّلاة، أو فعل محرَّماً كالزنا ؛ فهو كافر. قوله : (إلّا باعتبار ... إلخ) : أي : لأنَّه على القول بالشَّطريَّة يكون الإيمان مركباً، وعلى القول بالشَّرطيَّة يكون بسيطاً ، فتدبَّر .
And it is said: It (iman) is composed of tasdiq (affirmation) and nutq (utterance) of the two shahadahs. His saying: (And it is said: it is composed of tasdiq and nutq... etc.): This is the disagreement. So nutq is a part of its reality, except that tasdiq is a part that cannot be dropped, while iqrar (acknowledgment) may be dropped as in the case of the excused person due to muteness or coercion. And it is said: Rather, nutq is a condition for its validity, and there is no difference between it and the view of it being a part, except by consideration that the part is inside the essence, while the condition is outside it. His saying: (So nutq is a part of its reality) – this is the view of Abu Hanifa and a group of the Ash'aris, so iman according to them is a name for both the heart's action and the tongue's action. His saying: (And it is said: rather, nutq is a condition of validity... etc.): It results that the views are three, but they reduce to two views because whoever said it is a condition of validity has agreed with the one who said it is a part in meaning, and a third view remains: that iman is composed of tasdiq, nutq, and action, and that is for the Mu'tazila, and accordingly, whoever abandons an obligation like prayer or commits a prohibition like adultery is an unbeliever. His saying: (except by consideration... etc.): i.e., because on the view of part-ness, iman is composite, and on the view of condition-ness, it is simple, so reflect.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on different views regarding the composition of iman (faith). The commentator begins by quoting the matn statement: 'And it is said: It is composed of tasdiq (affirmation) and nutq (utterance) of the two shahadahs.' He then explains that this is a point of disagreement. The first view holds that nutq is a part (juz') of the essence of iman, with the qualification that tasdiq is an inseparable part while iqrar (which is equivalent to nutq) may be excused in cases of duress or physical inability. The second view holds that nutq is a condition (shart) for the validity of iman, not part of its essence. The commentator clarifies that the difference is not merely verbal: a part is internal to the essence (dakhil fi al-mahiyya) while a condition is external (kharij 'anha). He then attributes the first view (nutq as part) to Abu Hanifa and a group of Ash'aris, for whom iman is the combined action of heart and tongue. He then summarizes the three views on iman's composition: (1) tasdiq and nutq (part view), (2) tasdiq with nutq as a condition (condition view), and (3) tasdiq, nutq, and action (the Mu'tazili view), which deems any major sin as apostasy. He notes that the first two views reduce to one meaning regarding the necessity of utterance, differing only in technical classification. The commentator concludes by urging reflection on the conceptual difference between a composite essence (murakkab) and a simple one (basit).

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
تصديق (tasdiq)
نطق (nutq)
إقرار (iqrar)
جزء (juz')
شرط (shart)
ماهية (mahiyya)
شطر (shatr)
مركب (murakkab)
بسيط (basit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between saying nutq is a part of iman versus a condition is merely a verbal dispute with no real consequence.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that a part is inside the essence, while a condition is outside. This metaphysical distinction affects whether iman is considered composite (murakkab) or simple (basit).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how does the view that nutq is a condition differ from the view that it is a part?
A) The condition view holds that nutq can be dropped; the part view does not.
B) A part is inside the essence, while a condition is outside it.
C) The part view requires both tasdiq and nutq; the condition view only requires tasdiq.
D) There is no real difference; it is only a verbal dispute.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether nutq is a part or condition of iman hinges on the metaphysical distinction between an internal component and an external requirement—a distinction that shapes the very definition of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that some scholars consider verbal utterance a part of iman, while others see it as a condition. As you go about your day, have you ever considered your own utterance of the shahada as more than just words? Do you recite it with the awareness that it is either part of your faith's essence or a necessary condition of its validity? How does this distinction affect the seriousness with which you pronounce it?
?
The passage notes that on the condition view, iman is simple (basit), while on the part view, it is composite (murakkab). Reflect on your own understanding of your iman: Do you think of it as a single, undivided conviction, or as a combination of inner belief and outer expression? Which view challenges you more to live out your faith consistently?
?
The Mu'tazili view, by including action in the definition of iman, labels the one who abandons prayer or commits a major sin as a disbeliever. While you may not hold that view, it forces a question: Is your own faith accompanied by actions that prove its reality? If your deeds were to be weighed against your words today, would they testify to the same iman?

Increase and Decrease of Iman
The commentator affirms that iman increases and decreases with actions, with crucial distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preponderant view that iman increases with deeds, its restriction to the original disbeliever, and the ruling for children of Muslims who do not verbally profess.

Core Idea
Iman increases with deeds, but only for the original disbeliever.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a classical theological debate: Can iman increase or decrease? He states the stronger opinion that it does, linked to actions, but then adds an important qualification—this applies specifically to someone who originally disbelieved. For children born into Muslim families, their iman is affirmed regardless of verbal profession, though they have a duty to utter the shahada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central debate
Read the opening line: 'Thirdly: The disagreement concerning the increase and decrease of iman.' Recognize that theologians differ on this issue.
The commentator presents the preponderant view immediately after.
2
Understand the qualification
Note that the increase and decrease of iman is tied to deeds and is restricted to the original disbeliever (al-kafir al-asli). This limits the scope of the statement.
3
Differentiate the case of children of Muslims
Read the ruling: children of Muslims are judged believers even if they never utter the shahada, but they have violated a subsidiary obligation.
This distinguishes two categories: original disbeliever vs. Muslim-born.
4
Note the cross-reference
The last line is a grammatical note: 'with increase of deeds' refers back to 'it increases'.
This clarifies the relationship between iman and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً : الخلاف في زيادة الإيمان ونقصانه. ثمَّ الرَّاجح أنّ الإيمان يزيد وينقص بزيادة الأعمال. مقيَّد بالكافر الأصلي، وأمَّا أولاد المسلمين ; فمحكوم بإيمانهم عندنا وعند الله ولو لم ينطقوا طول عمرهم غير أنّهم خالفوا الواجب الفرعي. قوله : ( بزيادة الأعمال : راجع لقوله : (يزيد).

It is restricted to the original disbeliever. As for the children of Muslims, they are judged to have iman with us and with Allah even if they do not utter (the shahada) throughout their lives, except that they have violated the subsidiary obligation. His saying: (with increase of deeds) refers to his saying: (it increases).
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological debate over whether iman (faith) can increase and decrease. The commentator states that the preponderant (rajih) view is that iman does increase and decrease with the increase and decrease of deeds (a'mal). He then qualifies this: this statement applies specifically to the original disbeliever (al-kafir al-asli) who enters Islam; for such a person, iman can increase through good deeds. However, for children born to Muslim parents, they are considered believers by Islamic judgment both in this world and in God's knowledge, even if they never verbally profess the shahada throughout their lives, though they have failed to fulfill a subsidiary obligation (al-wajib al-far'i) by not uttering it. The commentator ends with a cross-reference note: 'His saying: (with increase of deeds) refers to his saying: (it increases).' This clarifies that the phrase 'increase of deeds' explains the manner of increase.
