Is Utterance a Part or Condition of Faith?
Al-Sawi presents and compares the 'part' and 'condition' views on utterance in faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the view that utterance is a part of faith's essence and the view that it is a condition for its validity, and the technical distinction between them.

Core Idea
A part is inside the essence; a condition is outside. This distinction shapes the definition of faith.

The role of verbal utterance in defining faith is a key debate in Islamic theology. In this passage, al-Sawi contrasts two views: one that considers utterance as an integral part of faith's reality, and another that sees it as an external condition. While they seem similar in effect, the technical difference lies in whether utterance is inside or outside the essence of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage presents two views and then comments on their similarity.
2
Identify the two views
Identify the first view: utterance is a part of faith. The second view: utterance is a condition. Note the attributions to Abu Hanifa and Ash'aris.
3
Understand the technical distinction
Focus on the phrase 'the part is inside the essence, while the condition is outside it'. This is the key difference.
This distinction is conceptual, not practical.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how each view affects the definition of faith. Does it make faith composite or simple?
The passage later addresses this in the next section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنطق جزء من حقيقته إلّا أن التصديق جزء لا يحتمل السقوط والإقرار قد يحتمله كما في المعذور من خرس أو إكراه. وقيل : بل النُّطق شرط صحة له، ولا فرق بينه وبين القول بالجزئية، إلا باعتبار أنّ الجزء داخل الماهيَّة، والشَّرط خارج عنها . قوله : ( فالنُّطق جزء من حقيقته هذا القول لأبي حنيفة وجماعة من الأشاعرة، فالإيمان عندهم اسم لعملي القلب واللسان جميعاً. قوله : (وقيل: بل النُّطق شرط صحة ... إلخ) : تحصّل أنَّ الأقوال ثلاثة، لكنَّها ترجع إلى قولين لأنَّ من قال : إنَّه شرط صحة؛ فقد وافق القائل في المعنى بأنَّه شطر، وبقي قول ثالث: وهو أنَّ الإيمان مركَّب من تصديق، ونطق، وعمل وهو للمعتزلة، وعليه فمن ترك واجباً كالصَّلاة، أو فعل محرَّماً كالزنا ؛ فهو كافر. قوله : (إلّا باعتبار ... إلخ) : أي : لأنَّه على القول بالشَّطريَّة يكون الإيمان مركباً، وعلى القول بالشَّرطيَّة يكون بسيطاً ، فتدبَّر .
The utterance is part of its reality, except that the assent is a part that cannot fall away, while the acknowledgment may possibly fall away, as in the case of one excused by dumbness or coercion. And it was said: Rather, utterance is a condition for its validity, and there is no difference between this and the statement of it being a part, except in consideration that the part is inside the essence, while the condition is outside it. His statement: ('The utterance is part of its reality.') This statement is for Abu Hanifa and a group of Ash'aris, for faith according to them is a name for the actions of the heart and the tongue together. His statement: ('And it was said: Rather, utterance is a condition of validity... etc.') So it is understood that the opinions are three, but they reduce to two opinions, because whoever said that it is a condition of validity has agreed with the one who said it is a part in meaning, and a third opinion remains: that faith is composed of assent, utterance, and action, and this is for the Mu'tazila, and accordingly, whoever leaves an obligation such as prayer, or commits a forbidden act such as adultery, is a disbeliever. His statement: ('Except in consideration... etc.') That is, because on the statement of it being a part, faith is composite; on the statement of it being a condition, it is simple. So reflect.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage explains the different scholarly positions on the role of verbal utterance (al-nutq) in the reality of faith (al-iman). The author first states that, according to one view (attributed to Abu Hanifa and a group of Ash'aris), utterance is a part (juz') of the essence of faith, though the essential part that cannot fall is assent (al-tasdiq), while acknowledgment (al-iqrar) may be dropped in cases of excuse like dumbness or coercion. Then he mentions a second view: that utterance is merely a condition (shart) for the validity of faith, not part of its essence. He notes that in meaning, there is no real difference between saying it is a part and saying it is a condition, except in technical consideration: a part is internal to the essence, while a condition is external. Then he summarizes that the opinions are actually three: the two mentioned (part and condition) reduce to one in meaning, and a third opinion is that of the Mu'tazila, who hold that faith is composed of three parts: assent, utterance, and action, so that neglecting an obligation or committing a grave sin entails disbelief (kufr). The author then elaborates on the distinction: if utterance is a part, faith is composite; if it is a condition, faith is simple. The passage concludes with an exhortation to reflect (tadabbar). The argument follows a comparative structure, contrasting the views without preferring one explicitly here.

Key Terms
الإيمان
النطق
التصديق
الإقرار
جزء
شرط
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'part' and 'condition' are synonymous or that they have the exact same implication.
Correction: While they agree in requiring utterance for faith, the technical distinction lies in whether utterance is internal (part) or external (condition) to the essence of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between considering utterance a part of faith versus a condition for its validity?
A) A part is necessary at all times; a condition is only sometimes required.
B) A part is inside the essence, while a condition is outside it.
C) A part can be dropped, but a condition cannot.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
The part-view and condition-view agree in requiring utterance for faith, but differ in the technical location: part is internal, condition is external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If faith requires utterance, but you remain silent about your beliefs in front of others, what does that say about the state of your faith? Will you commit to speaking one sentence of shahada today to someone who doesn't know?
?
The passage shows that even the part-view allows for utterance to fall away in cases of coercion. Have you ever chosen silence out of fear when no coercion existed? How will you respond next time?
?
Both views agree that utterance is required, but you might rationalise keeping your faith private. What is holding you back from declaring your faith openly, and what step will you take this week to change that?

The Three Opinions on Utterance and Faith
Al-Sawi summarizes the three opinions on utterance and reduces them to two, highlighting the Mu'tazila view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the role of utterance in faith and how they reduce to two distinct views, including the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
The three opinions reduce to two: faith requires utterance (part or condition) vs faith requires utterance and action (Mu'tazila).

After explaining the part and condition views, al-Sawi introduces a third opinion from the Mu'tazila, who require action as part of faith. He then notes that the first two opinions effectively merge, leaving two major positions. This lesson examines that reduction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the summary of opinions and the reduction.
This passage builds on the previous lesson.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions: (1) utterance is part, (2) utterance is condition, (3) faith is assent+utterance+action (Mu'tazila).
3
Understand the reduction
Notice that the condition-view is considered equivalent in meaning to the part-view, so they reduce to one group. The Mu'tazila view remains distinct.
The reduction is based on practical outcome, not technical definition.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila implication
Consider the consequence: if faith includes action, then neglecting obligations or committing sins can make one a disbeliever. This was a major point of contention.

Proofs for the Increase and Decrease of Faith
Al-Sawi presents rational and transmitted proofs for the fluctuation of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and revealed proofs that faith increases and decreases with actions, and the nuance that prophets are exceptions.

Core Idea
Faith is not static; it rises with good deeds and falls with sins.

In this passage, al-Sawi presents the case for the preponderant Ash'ari view that faith (iman) is dynamic. He grounds this in rational certainty, a Quranic verse, and a prophetic hadith, explaining how deeds affect the heart's light. This lesson unpacks each proof and clarifies the scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a statement followed by proofs.
The passage uses a very systematic style.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the rational proof (certainty of difference), the Quranic proof (verse 8:2), and the hadith proof (Ibn Umar).
The rational proof comes first, then the transmitted.
3
Understand the mechanism
Read how inner and outer actions increase the radiance of faith in the heart. Reflect on the saying 'Report is not like eyewitnessing.'
4
Note the exceptions and attribution
Recognize that prophets' faith cannot decrease, and faith can increase by Allah's grace. Notice how al-Sawi attributes this view to major scholars.
This shows the consensus behind the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ الرَّاجح أنّ الإيمان يزيد وينقص بزيادة الأعمال ونقصها؛ للقطع بأنَّ إيمان الفُسَّاق لا يساوي إيمان الصِّدِّيقين والأنبياء والمرسلين، ولقوله تعالى : (وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ ءَايَتُهُ، زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا) (١) ، وغير ذلك من الآيات، ولقوله لابن عمر لها حين سأله : الإيمان يزيد صلى الله عليه وسلم وينقص؟ نعم، يزيد حتى يدخل صاحبه الجنَّة، وينقص حتى يُدخل صاحبه النار» (٢). وبالجملة فزيادة الأعمال الباطنيّة والظَّاهريَّة تُوجب زيادة إشراقه وضيائه في القلب، وقلتُها توجب ضعفه، وظاهر أنَّ التصديق قد يقوى بقوَّة الأسباب، ولذا يقال : ليس الخبر كالعيان . وقوله : (ونقصها): راجع لقوله : ( وينقص) فهو لفّ ونشر مرتّب، وزايادته بالأعمال على حسب الغالب، وإلا فقد يزيد بفضل الله . قوله : (للقطع ... إلخ): علَّة للأرجحيَّة، ومحصل ما ذكره أدلة عقلية ونقلية، صدر بالعقلي، ثمَّ ثني بالنقلي. قوله : ((زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا ) : أي : وما قبل الزّيادة يقبل النَّقص إلَّا لعارض؛ كعصمة الأنبياء، فإنَّ إيمانهم يستحيل عليه النقص، وما ذكره الشارح من التّرجيح قول جمهور الأشاعرة والماتريدية ومالك والشَّافعي وأحمد .
Then the preponderant view is that faith increases and decreases with the increase and decrease of actions, because of the certainty that the faith of sinners is not equal to the faith of the truthful and the prophets and messengers, and because of His statement, the Exalted: (And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith) [Surah al-Anfal: 2], and other verses, and because of his statement, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, to Ibn 'Umar when he asked him: Does faith increase and decrease? Yes, it increases until it enters its possessor into Paradise, and it decreases until it enters its possessor into the Fire. In sum, the increase of inner and outer actions necessitates an increase of its radiance and light in the heart, and their fewness necessitates its weakness, and it is apparent that assent may strengthen through the strength of causes, hence it is said: Report is not like eyewitnessing. And his statement: ('and its decrease') refers back to his saying ('decreases'), so it is an orderly intertwining and spreading, and its increase by actions is according to the general rule, otherwise it may increase by the grace of Allah. His statement: ('because of the certainty... etc.') is a cause for the preponderance, and the gist of what he mentioned is rational and transmitted proofs, beginning with the rational, then followed by the transmitted. His statement: ('They increase them in faith') means that what precedes increase is susceptible to decrease except for an obstacle, like the sinlessness of the prophets, for their faith is impossible to decrease, and what the commentator mentioned of preponderance is the saying of the majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage provides the case for the preponderant view (al-rajih) among Sunni scholars that faith (al-iman) increases and decreases. The author first states the position: faith increases and decreases with the increase and decrease of actions. He then gives the rationale: rational certainty (al-qat') that the faith of sinners is not equal to the faith of the truthful, prophets, and messengers. Then he cites transmitted evidence: Quranic verses (starting with Surah al-Anfal: 2) and a prophetic hadith to Ibn 'Umar affirming increase and decrease. He explains that increase in inner and outer deeds causes increase in the radiance and light of faith in the heart, while decreased deeds cause weakness. He notes that assent itself can strengthen or weaken depending on the strength of causes, using the adage 'Report is not like eyewitnessing.' He clarifies that the phrase 'and its decrease' in the commentary refers back to 'decreases,' forming an ordered intertwining (laff wa nashr). He also notes that the increase through deeds is the general pattern, but sometimes faith increases purely by Allah's grace. The author then reveals that the rational and transmitted proofs are structured starting with rational, then transmitted. Finally, he interprets the verse: what is subject to increase is also subject to decrease unless an obstacle like the sinlessness of prophets prevents it, and he attributes this preponderant view to the majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and the four imams mentioned. The argument moves from stating the view, grounding it in certainty, then supporting it with revelation and hadith, then elaborating the mechanism, and finally attributing it to authoritative scholars.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الزيادة
النقصان
الأعمال
التصديق
الصدّيقين
الآيات
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is a fixed quantity that does not change.
Correction: Reason and revelation show faith increases with good deeds and decreases with sins, except for prophets whose faith is infallible.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a proof given in the passage for the increase and decrease of faith?
A) Rational certainty that the faith of sinners differs from that of prophets
B) The verse 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith'
C) The hadith of Ibn 'Umar on increase and decrease
D) The consensus of the companions on the issue

Key Takeaway
Faith increases and decreases with actions, but prophets' faith is exempt from decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you sense a real decrease in the light of faith in your heart? What specific action will you take today to restore what was lost?
?
You learned that faith can increase purely by Allah's grace. Have you ever humbly begged for that grace? What prevents you from doing so right now, with full awareness of your need?
?
The adage 'report is not like eyewitnessing' shows that stronger evidence strengthens conviction. Are you content with the level of certainty you have about the truths of faith, or will you seek deeper knowledge to see them as if with your own eyes?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Evidence for Faith's Increase and Decrease
Sanusi provides rational and scriptural proofs for the increase and decrease of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural evidence for the majority view that faith increases and decreases, and how the increase in actions strengthens faith.

Core Idea
Faith is not static; it grows through actions and strengthens with evidence.

Sanusi begins by presenting a strong case for the increase and decrease of faith, using both reason and revelation. The rational proof is that no one would equate the faith of a sinner with that of a prophet. The scriptural proofs include the Quranic verse 'they increase them in faith' and a hadith where the Prophet explicitly affirms increase and decrease. This lesson unpacks these arguments and explains how faith's radiance grows with good deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Notice the logical argument: the faith of sinners is clearly less than that of prophets, indicating variation.
2
Examine the Quranic and hadith evidence
Read the verse and hadith. The verse speaks of increase; the hadith confirms both increase and decrease.
The hadith mentions increase leading to Paradise and decrease leading to Hell.
3
Understand the mechanism of increase
Note that inward and outward actions increase faith's radiance, and the lack of actions weakens it. Affirmation can strengthen with stronger causes.
The saying 'News is not like direct vision' illustrates this.
4
Note the exception for prophets
Sanusi clarifies that prophetic faith cannot decrease due to their inerrancy, but this does not negate the general rule.

And its decrease: because it is certain that the faith of the wicked is not equal to the faith of the truthful (siddiqin) and the prophets and the messengers, and by His statement, the Exalted: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' (1), and other such verses, and by his statement, peace be upon him, to Ibn Umar when he asked him: Does faith increase and decrease? Yes, it increases until its possessor enters Paradise, and decreases until its possessor enters the Fire (2). In sum, the increase of inward and outward actions necessitates an increase of its radiance and light in the heart, and their paucity necessitates its weakness, and it is apparent that affirmation (tasdiq) may strengthen by the strength of causes, hence it is said: News is not like direct vision (al-ʿiyan). And his statement: 'and its decrease' refers to his statement 'and decreases' so it is orderly luff wa nashr, and its increase is by works according to the usual, otherwise it may increase by the grace of God. His statement: 'because it is certain...' etc.: a cause for the preponderance, and the sum of what he mentioned are rational (ʿaqliyya) and transmitted (naqliyya) proofs, beginning with the rational, then following with the transmitted. His statement: 'They increase them in faith': meaning, and what precedes the increase accepts decrease except for an impediment; like the inerrancy (ʿisma) of the prophets, for their faith is impossible to decrease, and what the commentator mentioned of preponderance is the saying of the majority of Ashʿaris and Maturidis, Malik, al-Shafiʿi, and Ahmad. And it is said: It neither increases nor decreases, because affirmation reaching the level of certainty. His statement: 'And it is said: it neither increases nor decreases': it is the saying of a group, among them Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions, and they interpreted the proofs of the first group by saying that the verse 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' means the object of faith (al-muʾmanu bihi); for the Companions had the Quran and rulings revealed to them gradually, so whenever the rulings increased, their practice of them increased. And they interpret the hadith by saying that increase and decrease refer to actions, not to affirmation (tasdiq). And among what also refutes his saying is what Ibn al-ʿArabi said: The divisions of faith are five: faith of taqlid (imitation) – which is taking beliefs from a shaykh and affirming them without knowledge of proof; faith of knowledge (ʿilm) – which is knowing beliefs with their proofs; faith of direct vision (ʿiyan) – which is knowing God by watchfulness of the heart as if seeing Him; faith of truth (ḥaqq) – which is seeing God with one's heart, the station of witnessing (mushahada); and faith of reality (ḥaqiqa) – which is annihilation (fanaʾ) in God from other than Him. Each one is greater than what precedes it, and the locus of disagreement is other than the faith of prophets and angels; for it increases and does not decrease, and it is said that the faith of angels neither increases nor decreases (2). If you say: His statement, the Exalted, regarding the Friend (Ibrahim): 'Do you not believe?' (3) gives the impression that the faith of prophets decreases. The answer: The meaning is: Is your perfect faith not sufficient for you? He said: Yes, but:
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage defends the position that faith (iman) can increase and decrease, and refutes the view that it does not. The author first presents the majority view (Ashʿaris, Maturidis, and the Imams Malik, Shafiʿi, Ahmad) that faith increases and decreases. The evidence is both rational (the faith of sinners is clearly not equal to that of prophets) and scriptural (Quran 8:2 and a hadith where the Prophet confirms increase and decrease). The author explains that increase in inward and outward actions causes the light of faith to increase in the heart, and vice versa. He also notes affirmation can strengthen due to stronger causes (e.g., direct vision vs. hearsay). Then he presents the opposing view of Abu Hanifa and his followers: faith does not increase or decrease because affirmation (tasdiq) at the level of certainty is singular. They interpret the verse to mean the object of faith (revelations) increases, and the hadith to refer to actions, not the essence of faith. The author then refutes this by citing Ibn al-ʿArabi's five divisions of faith: taqlid (imitation), ʿilm (knowledge), ʿiyan (direct vision), ḥaqq (truth), and ḥaqiqa (reality). Each level is greater and more complete, demonstrating that faith indeed admits of degrees. The disagreement is excepted for prophets and angels, whose faith does not decrease. Finally, an objection from the story of Prophet Ibrahim (Quran 2:260) is raised: does it imply his faith was incomplete? The answer begins: the meaning is 'Is your perfect faith not sufficient?' — implying he already had certainty, but wanted more. The argument is cut off.

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
زيادة (increase)
نقصان (decrease)
تصديق (tasdiq)
عصمة (infallibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is a monolithic entity that cannot change once attained.
Correction: The majority view, supported by reason and revelation, holds that faith increases with deeds and decreases with sin, as the light of faith in the heart waxes and wanes.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what happens when faith decreases?
A) It leads to Hell
B) It remains the same
C) It disappears
D) It is restored

Key Takeaway
Faith increases with good deeds and decreases with sins, as proven by reason, Quran, and hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own faith, are you actively cultivating its increase through deeds and seeking Allah's grace, or have you become complacent, assuming it is sufficient? What specific action can you take today to move it forward?
?
If your faith were measured by its radiance in your heart, would it be growing or shrinking? Which inward or outward habit is most responsible for its current direction, and what will you change?
?
The Prophet warned that faith can decrease until it leads to the Fire. Is there a sin or neglect in your life that you have normalized, knowing it diminishes your faith? Will you repent and restore the light before it is too late?

The Hanafi View and the Levels of Faith
Sanusi presents the Hanafi view and refutes it with Ibn al-Arabi's five divisions of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi position that faith does not increase or decrease, their interpretation of the texts, and the refutation using Ibn al-Arabi's five levels of faith.

Core Idea
Faith has degrees: from imitation to vision to annihilation, refuting the claim that it is unchangeable.

After presenting the majority view, Sanusi turns to the opposing position of Imam Abu Hanifa and his followers, who argue that faith as affirmation does not vary. They reinterpret the Quran and hadith to refer to actions or the object of faith. Sanusi then refutes this by citing Ibn al-Arabi's classification of faith into five ascending levels, from blind imitation to mystical annihilation. This demonstrates that faith itself admits of degrees, not just its fruits.

Guided Path
1
Learn the Hanafi view
Read the passage stating that faith does not increase or decrease. Note that they argue affirmation (tasdiq) is singular and cannot vary.
2
Understand their interpretation of the evidence
They reinterpret the verse to mean the object of faith increases, and the hadith to refer to actions, not faith itself.
The Companions received revelations gradually, so their practice increased.
3
Study the five levels of faith by Ibn al-Arabi
Examine the five categories: taqlid, ʿilm, ʿiyan, ḥaqq, ḥaqiqa. Each is higher than the previous, showing faith has degrees.
This classification directly refutes the Hanafi claim.
4
Note the exception for prophets and angels
The disagreement applies to others; prophetic faith only increases. There is a difference of opinion about angels.
5
Consider the objection from Ibrahim's story
Read the objection: the verse 'Do you not believe?' might suggest decrease. The answer is that it asks whether his perfect faith is sufficient.
The answer here is incomplete.

Refutation of the View that Iman Does Not Increase or Decrease
Al-Sawi presents the opposing view and refutes it using Ibn Arabi's fivefold classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether iman increases or decreases, the arguments of those who deny increase, and the refutation using Ibn Arabi's five categories.

Core Idea
Iman can increase through deeper knowledge and spiritual states—it is not static.

This lesson addresses a key theological dispute: does faith (iman) increase or decrease? Some scholars, like Abu Hanifa, argued that iman is fixed. Al-Sawi presents their case and then refutes it with Ibn Arabi's classification of five ascending levels of iman, showing that genuine faith deepens.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
Read the passage and identify the group that claims iman does not increase or decrease. Note that it includes Imam Abu Hanifa and his followers.
This is a minority position; most Ash'aris hold that iman increases and decreases.
2
Examine their interpretation
Notice how they reinterpret the verse '...increases them in faith' to refer to the object of faith (new revelations) and actions, not the inner conviction (tasdiq).
They also interpret the hadith similarly.
3
Study Ibn Arabi's five categories
Read the five categories: iman of taqlid, knowledge, vision, truth, and reality. Understand that each is higher than the last, demonstrating that iman can increase.
This is a persuasive enumeration that shows iman has levels.
4
Consider the exception for prophets and angels
Note that the disagreement applies to ordinary believers. Prophets' iman increases but never decreases; angels' iman is said to be fixed by some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقيل: لا يزيد ولا ينقص) : هو قول جماعة؛ منهم الإمام أبو حنيفة وأصحابه، وتأوّلوا أدلّة الأوّلين بأنَّ آية (وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ ءَايَتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا) المراد : المؤمَن به؛ فإنَّ الصَّحابة كان يتجدد عليهم القرآن والأحكام شيئاً فشيئاً، فكلما زادت الأحكام زاد عملهم بها. ويؤوّل الحديث : بأنَّ الزِّيادة والنقص ترجع إلى الأعمال لا التصديق. وممَّا يردُّ قوله أيضاً ما قاله ابن العربي: أقسام الإيمان خمسة : إيمان تقليد؛ وهو من أخذ العقائد عن شيخ وجزم بها من غير معرفة دليل، وإيمان علم؛ وهو معرفة العقائد بأدلتها، وإيمان عيان؛ وهو معرفة الله بمراقبة القلب كأنَّه يراه، وإيمان حقٌّ؛ وهو رؤية الله بقلبه وهو مقام المشاهدة، وإيمان حقيقة؛ وهو الفناء بالله عما سواه، فكل واحد أزيد ممَّا قبله، ومحل الخلاف في غير إيمان الأنبياء والملائكة؛ فإنَّه يزيد ولا ينقص، وقيل : إنَّ إيمان الملائكة لا يزيد ولا ينقص).
His statement: 'And it is said: it does not increase nor decrease' is the statement of a group, among them Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions. They interpreted the proofs of the first group that the verse 'And when His verses are recited to them, it increases them in faith' (al-Anfal 2) means the object of faith (al-mu'man bihi), because the Companions were receiving the Quran and its rulings gradually, so whenever rulings increased, their practice of them increased. And they interpret the hadith that increase and decrease refer to actions, not to tasdiq. Also, what refutes his statement further is what Ibn Arabi said: The categories of iman are five: 1) iman of taqlid – that is, taking beliefs from a shaykh and being certain of them without knowing the proof; 2) iman of knowledge – that is, knowing beliefs with their proofs; 3) iman of vision – that is, knowing God by watchfulness of the heart as if seeing Him; 4) iman of truth – that is, seeing God with one's heart, which is the station of witnessing; 5) iman of reality – that is, extinction in God from what is other than Him. And each one is greater than the one before it. And the locus of disagreement is other than the faith of the prophets and angels, for it increases and does not decrease, and it is said that the faith of angels does not increase nor decrease.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that iman does not increase or decrease. The author begins by identifying the proponents as Abu Hanifa and his followers, who interpret verses that seem to indicate an increase in iman as referring to the object of faith (the Quran and rulings) and to actions, not to the inner conviction (tasdiq). Then al-Sawi presents a strong refutation from Ibn Arabi, who divides iman into five ascending categories: taqlid (uncritical acceptance), knowledge (based on proofs), vision (heartfelt awareness), truth (witnessing), and reality (annihilation in God). Since each category is greater than the previous one, iman clearly can increase. The author notes that the disagreement does not apply to prophets and angels, whose iman is perfect and can only increase but never decrease—according to some, angels' iman is fixed. The passage thus defends the Ash'ari position that iman increases and decreases.

Key Terms
يزيد وينقص
التصديق
إيمان تقليد
إيمان علم
إيمان عيان
إيمان حق
إيمان حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse 'increases them in faith' refers only to outward actions, not the essence of iman.
Correction: Ibn Arabi's classification shows that iman itself can deepen internally through different levels.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Arabi, which of the following is the highest category of iman?
A) Iman of taqlid
B) Iman of knowledge
C) Iman of reality (al-fana')
D) Iman of truth (mushahada)

Key Takeaway
Iman can increase through deepening knowledge and spiritual realization, as demonstrated by Ibn Arabi's five ascending categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the five categories best describes your current iman? Are you content to stay at that level, or do you feel the urgency to ascend?
?
Your faith likely began as taqlid—accepted from parents or teachers. Have you ever taken a step beyond that to seek knowledge with proof? If not, what holds you back?
?
Imagine you reach the station of haqiqa (annihilation in God). Would your daily worries and attachments still matter? How can you begin to detach from them starting today?

Prophet Ibrahim's Faith: Objection and Answer
Al-Sawi presents an objection and its answer about Prophet Ibrahim's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse about Prophet Ibrahim (a.s.) does not imply any deficiency in his faith, and how the standard theological answer affirms his perfect faith.

Core Idea
The verse is rhetorical: it questions how perfect faith could be more perfect.

Al-Sawi addresses a common objection about the faith of prophets. Some might think that Prophet Ibrahim's request to see resurrection implies doubt. But the verse is rhetorical, and the answer clarifies that his request was for a higher level of certainty for the sake of his mission, not due to any lack of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to see the structure: 'If you say... the answer is...'. Then read the translation.
Notice the verse citation and the immediate answer.
2
Understand the rhetorical question
The answer explains that the question 'Did you not believe?' is rhetorical, meaning 'Is your perfect faith not enough?' This removes any hint of doubt.
In Arabic, 'أَوَلَمْ تُؤْمِنَ' is a rhetorical question expecting a positive answer.
3
Identify the purpose of Ibrahim's request
Ibrahim says 'Yes, but...' and explains he wants his heart to be at ease by seeing the miracle, so he can establish a proof for his people.
This shows his faith was complete, but he sought a higher level of certainty for da'wah.

Source Text

إن قلت : إنَّ قوله تعالى في حقّ الخليل : أَوَلَمْ تُؤْمِنَ ) يوهم أنَّ إيمان الأنبياء ينقص أجيب بأنَّ المعنى : أولم يكفك إيمانك الكامل؟ قال: بلى، ولكن : ليطمئن قلبي برؤية المعجزة الباهرة؛ لتقوم له الحجة على قومه .

He said: 'Yes, but only so that my heart may be at ease by seeing the brilliant miracle, so that the proof might be established against his people.'

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector cites the verse where God asks Prophet Ibrahim 'Did you not believe?' after Ibrahim's request to see how God brings the dead to life. The objector claims that this suggests prophets' faith can decrease, because Ibrahim seems to ask for reassurance. The author provides the standard answer: the question is rhetorical, meaning 'Is not your complete faith enough for you?' Ibrahim's response 'Yes, but' shows that his request was not for himself but to witness a miracle in order to have a stronger proof to present to his people. Thus, the verse does not indicate any lack of faith.

No Increase or Decrease in Certain Tasdiq
Al-Sawi explains that true tasdiq already at maximum certainty cannot change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why essential iman (tasdiq) is considered fixed and cannot increase or decrease in its core certainty.

Core Idea
Certain faith, being absolute, cannot become more or less.

Al-Sawi tackles a subtle point about the nature of faith: if true belief is already at the peak of certainty, can it change? This short but powerful passage shows why the answer is no, using a logical argument that guards against misinterpreting the stability of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then read the translation. Notice the key terms: tasdiq, jazm, zann.
The passage is only one sentence but dense in meaning.
2
Identify the two hypothetical cases
The author considers what would happen if tasdiq decreased (it becomes zann, which is kufr) or increased (meaningless, as it's already at the maximum). Trace this logic in the commentary.
3
Connect to the broader theology of iman
Reflect on how this argument distinguishes the essence of iman (tasdiq) from its external manifestations (actions, knowledge), which can increase. The passage focuses only on the core affirmation.
This helps prevent the misconception that faith cannot be strengthened through deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يتصوّر فيه زيادة ولا نقصان : أي : لأنَّه التصديق البالغ حدَّ الجزم، فلو قلنا بنقصه؛ لكان ظنّاً وهو كفر، ولو قلنا بزيادته؛ لكان لا معنى له؛ لأنَّه في غاية الجزم، وهو منتهى الزيادة.
His statement: 'No increase or decrease is conceivable in it' means: because it is tasdiq that has reached the limit of certainty. So if we said it decreases, it would become mere conjecture, which is unbelief; and if we said it increases, it would be meaningless, because it is at the utmost certainty, which is the limit of increase.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains why some scholars maintain that iman does not increase or decrease. The author clarifies that if iman is defined as tasdiq that has reached the highest level of certainty (jazzm), it cannot decrease because that would turn it into mere probability (zann), which is not valid iman and constitutes kufr. Similarly, it cannot increase because it has already reached the maximum degree of certainty. The argument is logical: certainty is an absolute quality; any change would entail loss of certainty or an impossible further increase. Thus, for the basic essence of iman (tasdiq), increase and decrease are not conceivable.

Key Terms
التصديق
الجزم
الظن
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this teaching means faith cannot change at all, including actions and knowledge.
Correction: The passage only refers to the core tasdiq (heartfelt certainty). Actions, knowledge, and other aspects of faith can increase or decrease; the stability of the essence does not contradict spiritual growth.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that tasdiq cannot increase?
A) Because it is already in the category of zann
B) Because it has reached the utmost limit of certainty
C) Because prophets do not need increase
D) Because actions are separate

Key Takeaway
The essence of true faith, being absolute certainty, logically excludes both increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith has 'decreased'? According to this lesson, if your core tasdiq is genuine certainty, it cannot truly decrease. So what might those feelings actually be—neglect of action, or a test? This week, when doubt creeps in, repeat to yourself: 'Certainty cannot become conjecture without destroying itself.' Then ask: What action can I take to strengthen my awareness of that certainty?
?
Is there an area of your life where you act as if you only have 'conjecture' about Allah's promises or warnings? The passage says conjecture in matters of faith is unbelief—this is a severe warning. Identify one specific belief where you are not fully certain. Commit to seeking knowledge until that area becomes certainty, not just hope.

Third Opinion, Verbal Dispute, and Critique
Al-Sawi presents an alternative opinion and evaluates a claim of verbal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion on faith, the claim of verbal dispute, and why the author considers the disagreement substantive.

Core Idea
The debate about faith is real, not just verbal.

This passage introduces a third position on the nature of iman, attributing it to al-Khattabi. It then presents a claim that the entire controversy is merely verbal, before offering a critique that reaffirms the dispute as genuine. Understanding this section clarifies why theological disagreements sometimes hinge on definitions, but here the author insists on a real difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the full English translation. Let the flow of al-Sawi's argument settle in your mind.
2
Identify the three parts
The passage has three clear parts: (1) al-Khattabi's opinion, (2) the claim of verbal dispute, (3) al-Sawi's critique. Locate each.
The author's critique begins with 'قوله : ( وفيه نظر)'.
3
Understand al-Khattabi's view
Al-Khattabi divides iman into three components: speech (fixed), action (variable), and belief (variable). Note the condition that if belief decreases to nothing, iman disappears.
This is a nuanced position not found in the earlier two opinions.
4
Analyze the claim of verbal dispute
Fakhr al-Razi said the dispute between the two main views is verbal, meaning both sides agree on the reality but differ in terminology. Identify why someone might think this.
The author rejects this, saying the disagreement is about the foundation of iman (tasdiq).
5
Grasp the author's critique
Al-Sawi says ' فيه نظر' (there is consideration), meaning he finds the verbal dispute claim problematic. The real issue is about the essence of faith (tasdiq), making the dispute substantive. The final point reaffirms the earlier preponderance.
This critique ties back to the main discussion on increase and decrease of iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقي قول ثالث للخطابي : وهو أنَّ الإيمان قول، وهو لا يزيد ولا ينقص، وعمل، وهو يزيد وينقص واعتقاد، وهو يزيد وينقص، فإذا نقص ذهب . قوله : (وقيل: الخلف لفظي : هذا القول للفخر الرازي جامعاً بين القولين . قوله : ( وفيه نظر) أي: لأنَّ الخلاف إنَّما هو في أصل الإيمان؛ وهو التصديق، فهو حقيقي لا لفظي، والمعوّل عليه الترجيح المتقدم.
And there remains a third saying of al-Khattabi: that faith is speech (qawl) which does not increase nor decrease, and action (amal) which increases and decreases, and belief (itiqad) which increases and decreases. So when it decreases, it disappears. His statement: 'And it is said: the dispute is verbal' – this is the statement of Fakhr al-Razi, combining both views. His statement: 'And in it there is consideration' means that the disagreement is concerning the foundation of faith, which is tasdiq, so it is real (haqiqi), not verbal. And the relied upon is the earlier preponderance.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents three more doctrinal points. First, a third opinion from al-Khattabi distinguishes three components of iman: speech (qawl) which is fixed, and action and belief which can vary, with the caveat that if belief decreases to the point of vanishing, iman ceases. Second, al-Sawi mentions the claim that the whole controversy is merely verbal (khilaf lafzi), attributed to Fakhr al-Razi, who attempted to reconcile the two main positions by dividing the subject. Third, the author critiques this verbal dispute claim: the real disagreement is about the essence of iman (tasdiq), not a matter of terminology. Therefore, the dispute is substantive. The author concludes by stating that the correct and relied-upon position is the earlier preponderance (presumably the view that iman can increase and decrease concerning its essence, except for the basic tasdiq which is fixed).

Key Terms
قول
عمل
اعتقاد
الخلف لفظي
الترجيح المتقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the whole controversy about iman increasing/decreasing is just a matter of terminology, not substance.
Correction: Al-Sawi insists the disagreement concerns the very foundation of faith (tasdiq), so it is a real, substantive dispute.

Checkpoint

A) He accepts it
B) He considers it the strongest opinion
C) He rejects it
D) He attributes it to al-Khattabi

Key Takeaway
The dispute about the nature of faith is substantive, centered on the role of tasdiq, not merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the foundation of faith is tasdiq (inner attestation). When you examine your own heart, does your tasdiq feel like it can increase or decrease? When was the last time you caught it weakening? What specific action will you take today to strengthen your inner conviction?
?
Al-Khattabi warned that if belief (itiqad) decreases enough, it can disappear entirely. Have you ever experienced a season where your faith felt dangerously low? How did you respond? What is one practical step you can commit to this week to guard against that loss?
?
The passage insists the dispute about iman is not just academic—it concerns the very reality of your faith. Does that challenge you to take your own understanding of iman more seriously? What will you change today about how you pursue or protect your faith based on this lesson?

Faith and Disbelief

5 min
Faith Does Not Increase or Decrease
Al-Sawi argues that faith is fixed and critiques the verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that faith (iman) as inner affirmation (tasdiq) is definitive certainty and cannot increase or decrease, and you will be able to evaluate the verbal dispute and the third opinion of al-Khattabi.

Core Idea
Faith is certain affirmation; if it changed, it would become doubt which is disbelief.

In this passage, al-Sawi argues that faith (iman) is not subject to increase or decrease because it is the reality of affirmation (tasdiq) that reaches the utmost certainty (jazzm). He explains that any decrease would turn it into conjecture (zann), which is disbelief, and increase is meaningless since it is already at the peak. He then addresses a verbal dispute attributed to al-Razi and presents a third opinion by al-Khattabi, before affirming that the core disagreement is substantive, not verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation to get the overall flow. Notice that the passage begins with a strong assertion, then explains it, then discusses alternative views.
The passage includes a stray Quranic phrase (ليطمئن قلبي...) which may be a marginal note; it does not affect the main argument.
2
Identify the main claim and its justification
The author states that faith cannot increase or decrease because it is the reality of affirmation that is fixed. The justification is that faith is definitive certainty; if it decreased it would become conjecture (disbelief), and if it increased it would be meaningless since it is already at the ultimate level.
Notice the logical structure: if faith decreased, it becomes conjecture (zann), which is a type of disbelief.
3
Examine the verbal dispute and al-Khattabi's opinion
Some claimed the dispute is verbal, interpreting texts about increase/decrease as referring to complete faith (including actions) and those about no change as referring to the foundation (tasdiq). Al-Sawi rejects this, saying the disagreement is real. Then al-Khattabi divided faith into speech (invariable), action (variable), and belief (variable).
Al-Razi is attributed with the verbal dispute resolution; al-Sawi criticizes it.
4
Understand al-Sawi's final position
Al-Sawi concludes that the relied-upon view is the earlier one: faith (as affirmation) does not change. The disagreement is about the essence of faith, not just words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُتصوَّر فيه زيادة ولا نقصان حتَّى إِنَّ مَن حصل له حقيقة التصديق، فسواء أتى بالطاعات أو ارتكب المخالفات فتصديقه باقٍ على حاله من غير تغير فيه أصلاً . وقيل : الخُلْف لفظيٌّ ؛ لأنّ ما يدلُّ على أنَّ الإيمان يزيد وينقص فمحمول على الإيمان الكامل المركَّب من تصديق وعمل، فالزّيادة والنقصان مصروفان إلى ما به الكمال من الأعمال، وما يدل على عدم الزّيادة والنقص فمحمول على أصل الإيمان، وهو التصديق، وفيه نظر . ليطمئن قلبي برؤية المعجزة الباهرة؛ لتقوم له الحجة على قومه . قوله : (لا يتصوّر فيه زيادة ولا نقصان : أي : لأنَّه التصديق البالغ حدَّ الجزم، فلو قلنا بنقصه؛ لكان ظنّاً وهو كفر، ولو قلنا بزيادته ؛ لكان لا معنى له؛ لأنَّه في غاية الجزم، وهو منتهى الزيادة. وبقي قول ثالث للخطابي : وهو أنَّ الإيمان قول، وهو لا يزيد ولا ينقص، وعمل، وهو يزيد وينقص واعتقاد، وهو يزيد وينقص، فإذا نقص ذهب . قوله : (وقيل: الخلف لفظي : هذا القول للفخر الرازي جامعاً بين القولين . قوله : ( وفيه نظر) أي: لأنَّ الخلاف إنَّما هو في أصل الإيمان؛ وهو التصديق، فهو حقيقي لا لفظي، والمعوّل عليه الترجيح المتقدم قطعاً .
It cannot be conceived to have increase or decrease, such that whoever attains the reality of affirmation, whether he performs acts of obedience or commits violations, his affirmation remains as it is without any change in it at all. And it was said: the dispute is verbal; because what indicates that faith increases and decreases is carried upon the complete faith composed of affirmation and action, so the increase and decrease are directed to what perfects it from actions, and what indicates the absence of increase and decrease is carried upon the foundation of faith, which is affirmation. And there is consideration in it. That my heart may be assured by seeing the clear miracle, so that the proof may be established for his people. His saying: (It cannot be conceived to have increase or decrease) meaning because it is affirmation that reaches the ultimate level of certainty. If we said it decreases, it would be conjecture, which is disbelief; and if we said it increases, it would have no meaning because it is at the utmost certainty, which is the limit of increase. And there remains a third opinion of al-Khattabi: that faith is speech, which does not increase or decrease, and action, which increases and decreases, and belief, which increases and decreases; so if it decreases, it disappears. His saying: (And it was said: the dispute is verbal) This is the opinion of al-Fakhr al-Razi, reconciling between the two opinions. His saying: (And there is consideration in it) meaning because the disagreement is about the foundation of faith, which is affirmation, so it is real, not verbal, and the relied-upon is the earlier preponderance absolutely.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an argument and discussion about whether faith (iman) can increase or decrease. The author, al-Sawi, begins by stating that such change cannot be conceived in faith, because faith is the reality of affirmation (tasdiq) that is fixed and unchanged regardless of actions. Then he presents a view that the dispute is verbal: passages suggesting increase/decrease refer to complete faith (including works), while passages denying change refer to the core affirmation. He adds 'and there is consideration in it' (fihi nazar), indicating doubt. The Quranic phrase 'that my heart may be assured' seems inserted here, possibly as an example from the story of Ibrahim. Next, al-Sawi explains the earlier statement: faith cannot increase or decrease because it is definitive certainty (jazzm); any decrease would turn it into conjecture (zann), which is disbelief, and increase is meaningless since it is already at its peak. He then records a third opinion from al-Khattabi that divides faith into speech (non-varying), action (varying), and belief (varying). Finally, al-Sawi notes that the claim of a verbal dispute belongs to al-Razi, but corrects it: the disagreement is real, not verbal, because it concerns the very essence of faith (tasdiq). The correct view is the earlier one that faith does not change.

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
تصديق (tasdiq)
جزم (jazzm)
خلف لفظي (verbal dispute)
ظن (conjecture)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over whether faith increases or decreases is merely verbal; one can reconcile all texts without disagreement.
Correction: Al-Sawi argues that the disagreement is real because it concerns the very essence of faith (tasdiq). Those who claim it is verbal are mistaken.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sawi argue that faith cannot increase or decrease?
A) Because faith is composed of speech, action, and belief, each of which varies.
B) Because faith is definitive certainty; any decrease would turn it into conjecture, which is disbelief.
C) Because the texts are contradictory and must be reconciled verbally.
D) Because al-Razi said so.

Key Takeaway
Faith (iman) as inner affirmation (tasdiq) is definitive certainty and does not increase or decrease; any change would negate its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Sawi says if faith could decrease it would become doubt. Look honestly at your heart: is there any area where you are not fully certain about Allah? What would it take to move from that doubt to the certainty described in this passage?
?
You have now read that faith is at the peak of certainty and cannot increase. Does your heart find rest in that, or does it resist? If you feel it should increase, what does that reveal about your understanding of faith's nature? Will you submit to this truth and let it transform how you view your relationship with your Lord?

Islam: Linguistic and Legal Meaning, and Its Relationship to Faith
Al-Sawi defines Islam linguistically and legally, presenting two opinions on its relation to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definitions of Islam, the two scholarly opinions on its relationship with faith, and the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Islam and faith: synonymous or distinct? The answer shapes how we understand submission and belief.

In this passage, al-Sawi introduces the concept of Islam, first by its linguistic meaning of submission and compliance, then by its legal definitions. Scholars have two main views on whether Islam is identical to faith or distinct from it. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping core theological concepts in Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic definition
Read how Islam is defined as submission and compliance in language, noting it differs from faith linguistically.
2
Examine the first scholarly opinion
Identify the view that Islam and faith are synonymous legally, held by most Maturidis and some Ash'aris.
3
Examine the second opinion
See how the majority of Ash'aris distinguish Islam from faith, defining Islam as submission and faith as affirmation of the heart.
4
Identify the relied-upon position
Note that al-Sawi indicates the second opinion is the relied-upon one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً : بيان معنى الإسلام وأمّا الإسلام فهو لغة الخضوع والانقياد، فهو غير الإيمان لغة وأمّا شرعاً : فقد اختلف فيهما : - فذهب أكثر الماتريديَّة وبعض محققي الأشاعرة إلى أنَّه الخضوع والانقياد للأوامر والنواهي، بمعنى قبول ذلك والإذعان له، وعليه فهو عين الإيمان، فالإيمان والإسلام مترادفان شرعاً، وقال النسفي في «العقائد : (والإيمان والإسلام واحد)(۱). - والأكثر من الأشاعرة مع كثير من الماتريدية إلى تغايرهما مفهوماً كتغايرهما لغة، إذ مفهوم الإيمان: تصديقُ القلب بكلِّ ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مما علم من الدِّين ضرورة؛ أي: الإذعان لذلك، ومفهوم قوله: (الخضوع والانقياد :أي فيقال أسلمت الدابة واستسلمت؛ أي : انقادت . قوله : ( والأكثر من الأشاعرة . . . إلخ) : مقابل للقول الأول، وهو المعتمد . : ... قوله : (إذ مفهوم الإيمان أي مدلوله (۱)
Fourth: Explanation of the meaning of Islam. As for Islam, it is linguistically submission and compliance, so it is different from faith linguistically. As for legally, they differed concerning them: The majority of the Maturidis and some of the verifying scholars of the Ash'aris held that it is submission and compliance to commands and prohibitions, meaning acceptance and acquiescence to that, and accordingly it is the very same as faith, so faith and Islam are synonymous legally. And al-Nasafi said in al-‘Aqa’id: (Faith and Islam are one). And the majority of the Ash'aris along with many of the Maturidis held that they are different conceptually just as they are different linguistically, for the concept of faith is the heart’s affirmation of everything brought by the Prophet ﷺ that is known from religion by necessity, i.e., acquiescence to that, and the concept of Islam is submission and compliance. That is, one says: the animal submitted and surrendered, i.e., it complied. His saying: (And the majority of the Ash'aris... etc.) is in contrast to the first opinion, and it is the relied-upon. ... His saying: (For the concept of faith i.e., its denotation) (1).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the meanings of Islam (islam) and its relationship to faith (iman). The author, al-Sawi, organizes it under a fourth heading: 'Explanation of the meaning of Islam.' He first gives the linguistic definition: Islam is 'submission and compliance' (al-khudu‘ wa al-inqiyad), noting that it differs from faith linguistically. Then he presents the legal (shar‘i) definitions, stating that scholars have differed: The first opinion, held by the majority of Maturidis and some verifying Ash'aris, is that Islam is submission to commands and prohibitions, i.e., acceptance and acquiescence, and thus it is identical to faith; faith and Islam are synonyms in the sacred law. He cites al-Nasafi’s creed declaring them one. The second opinion, held by the majority of Ash'aris and many Maturidis, is that they are conceptually distinct, just as they are linguistically distinct. The concept of faith is the heart’s affirmation (tasdiq) of everything brought by the Prophet ﷺ that is necessarily known from religion, i.e., acquiescence. The concept of Islam is submission and compliance. The commentator then clarifies the linguistic point: one says 'the animal submitted' (aslamat al-dabba) meaning it complied. Finally, he notes that this second opinion is the relied-upon (al-mu‘tamad) position, contrasting with the first. The passage ends with a grammatical note linking 'concept' (mafhūm) to 'denotation' (madlūl).

إيمان (faith)
خضوع (submission)
انقياد (compliance)
ماتريدية (Maturidis)
أشاعرة (Ash'aris)
تصديق (affirmation)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Islam and faith are always synonymous in Islamic theology.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows there is significant scholarly disagreement: the majority of Ash'aris and many Maturidis hold that they are distinct, and this is the relied-upon position.

Key Takeaway
Islam and faith are related but not always synonymous; the relied-upon view among Ash'aris distinguishes them as submission vs. heart affirmation.

Definitions of Islam and Its Relation to Faith
Al-Sawi defines Islam and presents two opinions on its relation to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of Islam and the two major theological opinions on whether Islam is synonymous with faith or distinct.

Core Idea
Islam and faith: same or different? The answer shapes how we understand submission and belief.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary on al-Kharidat lays the groundwork for one of the most important debates in Islamic theology: the relationship between Islam (submission) and iman (faith). Before we can discuss who is a believer or what constitutes apostasy, we must clarify these terms. Al-Sawi presents both the linguistic meaning and the two technical opinions held by Maturidis and Ash'aris.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definition
Note that linguistically, Islam is submission (khudu') and yielding (inqiyad), and it differs from the linguistic meaning of faith (tasdiq).
The linguistic distinction already hints at the debate to come.
2
Examine the first opinion: synonymy
Most Maturidis and some Ash'aris hold that technically Islam is identical to faith, making the two terms synonymous. Al-Nasafi's statement from al-'Aqa'id is cited as support.
This opinion emphasizes the inner state of acceptance and acquiescence.
3
Examine the second opinion: distinction
Most Ash'aris and many Maturidis distinguish the concepts: faith is heart's affirmation, Islam is compliance with commands based on that affirmation. They are inseparable in law but different in meaning.
This is the relied-upon view according to al-Sawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الإيمان والإسلام
رابعاً : بيان معنى الإسلام
وأمّا الإسلام فهو لغة الخضوع والانقياد، فهو غير الإيمان لغة
وأمّا شرعاً : فقد اختلف فيهما :
- فذهب أكثر الماتريديَّة وبعض محققي الأشاعرة إلى أنَّه الخضوع والانقياد للأوامر والنواهي، بمعنى قبول ذلك والإذعان له، وعليه فهو عين الإيمان، فالإيمان والإسلام مترادفان شرعاً، وقال النسفي في «العقائد : (والإيمان والإسلام واحد)(١).
- والأكثر من الأشاعرة مع كثير من الماتريدية إلى تغايرهما مفهوماً كتغايرهما لغة، إذ مفهوم الإيمان: تصديقُ القلب بكلِّ ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مما علم من الدِّين ضرورة؛ أي: الإذعان لذلك، ومفهوم الإسلام: امتثال الأوامر والنواهي ببناء العمل على ذلك الإذعان، فهما مختلفان وإن تلازما شرعاً، بحيث لا يوجد مسلم ليس بمؤمن، ولا العكس، إذ يلزم من الإذعان الامتثال المذكور، ومن الامتثال الإذعان، فليتأمل.
قوله: (الخضوع والانقياد :أي فيقال أسلمت الدابة واستسلمت؛ أي : انقادت .
قوله : ( والأكثر من الأشاعرة . . . إلخ) : مقابل للقول الأول، وهو المعتمد .
قوله : (إذ مفهوم الإيمان أي مدلوله
Topics of Faith and Islam
Fourth: Explanation of the meaning of Islam
As for Islam, it is linguistically submission and yielding, so it is different from faith linguistically.
As for technically: there is disagreement about them:
- The majority of the Maturidis and some of the verifying Ash'aris held that it is submission and yielding to commands and prohibitions, meaning acceptance and acquiescence to them, and accordingly it is identical to faith, so faith and Islam are synonymous technically, and Al-Nasafi said in Al-'Aqa'id: 'Faith and Islam are one.'
- The majority of the Ash'aris along with many of the Maturidis hold that they differ in concept as they differ linguistically, because the concept of faith is the heart's affirmation of everything brought by the Prophet (peace be upon him) that is known necessarily from religion, i.e., acquiescence to that, and the concept of Islam is compliance with commands and prohibitions by building action upon that acquiescence; so they are different even though they are necessarily concomitant technically, such that there is no Muslim who is not a believer, nor vice versa, for acquiescence necessarily entails the mentioned compliance, and compliance entails acquiescence, so reflect.
His saying: (submission and yielding): i.e., one says 'the animal submitted' and 'it surrendered,' i.e., it yielded.
His saying: (and the majority of the Ash'aris... etc.): it is in contrast to the first opinion, and it is the relied-upon.
His saying: (since the concept of faith) i.e., its signification.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term Islam, followed by the two major theological opinions on its relationship to faith (iman). The author begins with the linguistic meaning: submission and yielding, noting that this differs from the linguistic meaning of faith (which is affirmation). He then turns to the technical (legal) definition, stating that scholars disagree. The first opinion, held by most Maturidis and some Ash'aris, takes Islam to be submission and yielding to divine commands and prohibitions, i.e., acceptance and acquiescence; on this view Islam is identical to faith, so the two terms are synonymous in the law. Al-Nasafi's statement that 'faith and Islam are one' is cited in support. The second opinion, held by most Ash'aris and many Maturidis, distinguishes the two concepts: faith is the heart's affirmation (tasdiq) of everything necessarily known from religion, while Islam is compliance (imtithal) with commands and prohibitions by building action on that affirmation. Despite being different in concept, they are mutually entailing (mutalazim) in the law: no Muslim is without faith and no believer is without Islam, because acquiescence necessitates compliance and compliance necessitates acquiescence. The hashiah comments clarify the linguistic usage of 'submission' and note that the second opinion is the relied-upon view. The passage thus provides the foundational terminology and sets up the debate about iman and islam.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows that while some scholars equate them, the majority distinguish between them in concept while affirming their inseparability in law.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Islam and faith are technically distinct in concept yet inseparable in law according to the majority of Ash'aris and many Maturidis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says Islam is 'compliance with commands by building action upon the heart's acquiescence.' Is your worship and daily obedience truly built on deep inner conviction, or are you just going through the motions? Identify one action you do routinely. Before you do it tomorrow, pause and actively reaffirm your faith in your heart. Then note whether it changes your engagement.
?
The relied-upon view holds that faith and Islam are distinct yet inseparable—inner belief must manifest in outward action. If your actions have been inconsistent with your claimed belief, are you really a believer according to this definition? What is the one area where your compliance is weakest, and what specific step will you take this week to align your action with your affirmation?

Some scholars equate Islam entirely with faith, making no distinction. Could it be that you prefer that opinion because it asks less of you in terms of visible commitment? Be honest: does your internal state match the external demands of Islam? If not, what are you afraid of changing?

Defending the Concomitance of Islam and Faith
Al-Sawi refutes two objections against the distinctiveness and concomitance of Islam and faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi refutes two objections against the necessary link between Islam and faith in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
True Islam and faith are inseparable; hypocrisy only shows external submission.

Al-Sawi now faces two reasoned objections that challenge the relationship between Islam and faith established earlier. The first uses the Quranic verse about hypocrites; the second cites a Prophetic hadith defining Islam by actions. How can the necessary concomitance hold if hypocrisy exists, and if Islam is defined by actions alone? Al-Sawi's answers reveal the depth of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how the objections are framed and answered.
The second objection is incomplete in the source; it is cut off after mentioning the hadith.
2
Identify the two objections
Notice that the first objection claims Islam can exist without faith (hypocrites). The second claims Islam is equated with actions (hadith). Find where each is introduced.
The hashiah labels each objection's target: first against concomitance, second against synonymy.
3
Examine Al-Sawi's first answer
Al-Sawi distinguishes between legally accepted Islam (which saves from hell) and mere external submission. Hypocrites only have the latter.
This preserves the concomitance for true Islam.
4
Reflect on the second answer and concluding clarification
Though the answer to the second objection is missing, the hashiah clarifies that the verse listing Muslims and believers separately (33:35) does not break concomitance, as conceptual difference suffices for conjunction.
The lesson thus defends both distinctness and necessary connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: إنَّ الإسلام قد ينفرد عن الإيمان في المنافق كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى : قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ امَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا ) . قلتُ : كلامنا في الإسلام المعتبر شرعاً، المنجي من خلود النَّار، وأما ما في الآية فالمراد به الانقياد الظاهري فقط .
فإن قلت : قد فَسَّر النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم الإسلام بنفس العمل، حيث قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : «الإسلام أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله، وتقيمَ الصَّلاة، وتؤتي الزَّكاة، وتصوم رمضان، وتحجّ

قوله : (فإن قلت : قد فسَّر النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم ... إلخ) : هذا السؤال وارد على القول بترادفهما .
If you say: Islam can be separate from faith in the hypocrite, as indicated by His statement: The Bedouins say, 'We have believed.' Say, 'You have not believed; but say, "We have submitted" ' (Quran 49:14). I say: Our discussion concerns the Islam that is considered legally, which saves from eternal punishment. As for what is in the verse, it refers only to external submission.
If you say: The Prophet (peace be upon him) interpreted Islam as action itself, where he said: 'Islam is that you testify that there is no deity except Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establish prayer, give zakat, fast Ramadan, and perform Hajj...
His saying: (and they are necessarily concomitant legally): i.e., and it is not contradicted by His statement: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, and the believing men and believing women' (Quran 33:35) because the difference in concept between Muslim and believer is sufficient for conjunction, so it does not necessitate a difference in the essence of the believer from the essence of the Muslim.
His saying: (If you say: Islam can be separate from faith... etc.): This objection is raised against the establishment of concomitance between them.
His saying: (If you say: The Prophet... etc.): This objection is raised against the view that they are synonymous.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The author presents two objections against the relationship between Islam and faith as described in the previous lesson. The first objection comes from the case of hypocrites: they outwardly submit (islam) but lack true faith (iman), as indicated by Quran 49:14. The answer distinguishes between two kinds of Islam: the legally valid Islam that saves from hell (which is concomitant with faith) and mere external submission. The hypocrite's Islam is only the latter, so it does not break the necessary concomitance of true Islam with faith. The second objection cites the Prophet's (peace be upon him) definition of Islam as the five pillars (testimony, prayer, zakah, fasting, Hajj), which are actions, not internal affirmation. This objection is incomplete in the text, but the answer is implied: the hadith is explaining the external manifestations of Islam, not its essence. The hashiah comments then clarify that the verse mentioning Muslims and believers separately (Quran 33:35) does not contradict the concomitance, because the difference in concept allows for conjunction without requiring a difference in essence. Finally, the hashiah labels the two objections: the first attacks the concomitance, the second attacks the synonymy view. The lesson thus defends the Ash'ari position that iman and islam are distinct yet inseparable.

The Relationship Between Iman and Islam: Distinct Yet Inseparable
Al-Sawi defines islam, states its relation to iman, and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi defines iman and islam as distinct concepts that are legally inseparable, and how he responds to key objections about hypocrisy and prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Iman and Islam are distinct concepts but legally inseparable.

Al-Sawi begins by clarifying the relationship between inward faith (iman) and outward submission (islam). While they are often used interchangeably, he argues they have distinct meanings yet are mutually necessary in Islamic law. This passage carefully defines both terms, addresses key objections from the Qur'an and Hadith, and reconciles the positions of the Maturidis and Ash'aris.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage where Al-Sawi defines Islam as compliance built upon inward assent. Note his claim that iman and islam are different yet legally inseparable.
The key phrase: 'فهما مختلفان وإن تلازما شرعاً'
2
Understand the first objection
Examine the objection about hypocrites who appear Muslim without faith. Al-Sawi responds by distinguishing legally valid islam from mere external compliance.
The verse about Bedouins (49:14) is cited.
3
Analyze the second objection
Now look at the objection based on the Prophet's hadith listing specific actions as Islam. Al-Sawi explains that the Prophet meant those as signs, not the essence.
Compare with the hadith defining iman by similar actions.
4
Follow the reconciliation
Read the attempted reconciliation between Maturidis and Ash'aris. Note the author's own conclusion: different in concept, identical in referent, legally inseparable.
The author dismisses the reconciliation as unnecessary.
