قوله : (وإن تلازما شرعاً ) : أي : ولا يبعده قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَتِ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتِ (۲)؛ لأنَّ تغاير مفهوم المسلم والمؤمن كاف في العطف، فلا يلزم منه مغايرة ذات المؤمن لذات المسلم .
فإن قلت: إنَّ الإسلام قد ينفرد عن الإيمان في المنافق كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى : قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ امَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا . قلتُ : كلامنا في الإسلام المعتبر شرعاً، المنجي من خلود النَّار، وأما ما في الآية فالمراد به الانقياد الظاهري فقط .
قوله : (فإن قلت : إنَّ الإسلام قد ينفرد... إلخ): هذا السُّؤال وارد على ثبوت التلازم بينهما .
فإن قلت : قد فَسَّر النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم الإسلام بنفس العمل، حيث قال عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : «الإسلام أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله، وتقيمَ الصَّلاة، وتؤتي الزَّكاة، وتصوم رمضان، وتحج البيت إن استطعت إليه سبيلاً» فالجواب : أنَّ مراده عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام بالإسلام علاماته الدَّالَّة عليه، كما قال عليه الصّلاة والسَّلام لوفد قدموا عليه: «أتدرون ما الإيمان بالله تعالى وحده؟»، فقالوا : الله ورسوله أعلم، فقال: «شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله ، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة، وصيام رمضان، وأن تُعطوا من المَغْنم الخمس»، فقد فَسَّر الإيمان بعلاماته لظهور أنَّ الإيمان ليس ما ذُكر بل التصديق والإذعان، قاله التفتازاني.
قوله : (فإن قلت : قد فسَّر النَّبيُّ... إلخ) : هذا السؤال وارد على القول بترادفهما .
وقد جمع الله بين قولي الماتريدية والأشاعرة بالتّرادف وعدمه بأنَّهما خلاف في حال، فإن مفهوم الإسلام: - إن فُسِّر بالانقياد الظاهري، بمعنى امتثال الأوامر والنواهي والعمل بمقتضى تلك الأحكام من غير ملاحظة الإذعان والتسليم القلبي كان مخالفاً لمفهوم الإيمان.
وبيان ذلك : أنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلّم فسَّر الإسلام بالعمل، ومن المعلوم أنَّ العمل غير التصديق، فكيف يقال بترادفهما؟
والحقُّ : أنَّهما مختلفان ،مفهوماً، متّحدان ما صدقا، متلازمان شرعاً، فقوله : (وقد جمع الله ... إلخ تكلف لا داعي إليه .
Islam: compliance with commands and prohibitions by building action upon that inward assent; so the two are different even though they are legally inseparable, such that there is no Muslim who is not a believer, nor the opposite, since inward assent necessitates the aforementioned compliance, and compliance necessitates inward assent, so let it be pondered. 
His statement: (and they are legally inseparable): meaning that the verse ﴿Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women and the believing men and believing women﴾ does not contradict this; because the difference in meaning between 'Muslim' and 'believer' suffices for the conjunction, so the verse does not imply that the essence of the believer is different from the essence of the Muslim. 
If you say: Indeed, Islam may be separate from faith in the hypocrite, as indicated by His statement: 'The Bedouins say, "We have believed." Say, "You have not [yet] believed; but say, "We have submitted.""' I say: Our discussion concerns Islam that is legally valid, which saves from eternal damnation; as for what is in the verse, it means only external compliance. 
His statement: (If you say: Indeed, Islam may be separate... etc.): This objection is directed at the establishment of the mutual necessity between them. 
If you say: The Prophet (peace be upon him) interpreted Islam as actions themselves, where he said: 'Islam is that you testify that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establish prayer, give zakat, fast Ramadan, and perform pilgrimage to the House if you are able to find a way.' The answer: His intention (peace be upon him) by 'Islam' was its indicative signs, just as he said (peace be upon him) to a delegation that came to him: 'Do you know what faith in Allah alone is?' They said: Allah and His Messenger know best. He said: 'Testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establishment of prayer, giving zakat, fasting Ramadan, and that you give from the spoils one-fifth.' So he interpreted faith by its signs, because it is evident that faith is not what was mentioned but rather belief and inward assent, as al-Taftazani said. 
His statement: (If you say: The Prophet interpreted... etc.): This objection is directed at the opinion that they are synonymous. 
And Allah has reconciled between the statements of the Maturidis and the Ash'aris regarding synonymy and non-synonymy: that they differ in [particular] situation. For the meaning of Islam: if it is interpreted as external compliance, meaning compliance with commands and prohibitions and acting according to those rulings without regard to inward assent and submission of the heart, then it would be different from the meaning of faith. 
The explanation of that: The Prophet (peace be upon him) interpreted Islam as action, and it is known that action is different from belief, so how can it be said that they are synonymous? 
The truth: they are different in concept, identical in referent, and mutually necessary in law. So his statement: (And Allah has reconciled... etc.) is an affectation that is uncalled for.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about the relationship between iman (faith) and islam (submission). The author begins by defining islam as compliance with commands and prohibitions built upon inward assent (idh'an). He states that the two are different in concept but legally inseparable: every Muslim is a believer and vice versa, because inward assent necessarily leads to compliance and vice versa. 

He then anticipates objections. The first objection cites the hypocrite who externally submits without faith, as in the verse about the Bedouins. The author answers that the discussion concerns legally valid islam that saves from hellfire, whereas the verse refers only to external compliance. A marginal note clarifies that this objection challenges the claim of mutual necessity.

The second objection points to the Prophet's hadith defining islam as specific actions (testimony, prayer, zakat, etc.), which seems to equate islam with action, not inward assent. The author answers that the Prophet meant those actions as signs (symptoms) of islam, just as he defined iman in another hadith by listing actions, even though iman is truly belief and inward assent. This is supported by a quote from al-Taftazani. Another marginal note says this objection challenges the view that the two are synonymous.

Finally, the author presents the reconciliation between Maturidis and Ash'aris: if islam is interpreted narrowly as external compliance without inward assent, then it differs from iman. He then argues that the truth is that they are different in concept (māfhūman), identical in referent (muṣaddaqan), and mutually necessary in law (mutalāzimān sharʿan). He dismisses the attempted reconciliation by others as an unnecessary affectation, implying that the correct view is clear without needing to force harmony between contradictory statements.

Key Terms
إيمان
إسلام
إذعان
امتثال
انقياد ظاهري
تلازم
ترادف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman and islam are either completely separate (e.g., hypocrite) or completely synonymous (based on hadith listing actions).
Correction: Al-Sawi shows they are distinct in concept but legally inseparable: every legally valid Muslim is a believer and vice versa, but the meanings differ.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the relationship between iman and islam in terms of concept and legal reality?
A) They are synonymous both in concept and referent.
B) They are identical in referent but differ in concept and are legally inseparable.
C) They are completely separate and independent of each other.
D) They differ in referent but are identical in concept.

Key Takeaway
Iman and islam are distinct in meaning but legally inseparable: every true Muslim has both, and each implies the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that iman and islam are legally inseparable. If someone performs all the outward acts of Islam but harbors doubt or hypocrisy in their heart, are they truly a Muslim in the sight of Allah according to this passage? Examine your own life: is your outward compliance always built upon genuine inward assent, or do you sometimes act without the heart? What would it take for you to ensure that your islam flows from your iman?
?
Al-Sawi says that the Prophet's listing of actions for Islam are signs, not the essence. The essence of Islam is compliance built on inward assent. When you perform the five pillars, do you treat them as mere signs or as expressions of your inner submission? What would change if you performed each action with conscious awareness that it must stem from your heart's surrender?
?
The author dismisses the attempted reconciliation between Maturidis and Ash'aris as 'an affectation that is uncalled for.' Why do you think he was so firm? Could it be that forcing harmony where none is needed obscures the truth? In your own religious understanding, are you sometimes too quick to reconcile contradictory views instead of accepting the clear distinction? How might accepting distinctions strengthen rather than weaken your faith?

The Signs That Indicate Islam
Sanusi answers an objection about the hadith on Islam's signs via al-Taftazani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi, following al-Taftazani, resolves the apparent tension between outward actions and inner faith by interpreting the Prophet's words as referring to signs of Islam.

Core Idea
Outward actions are signs of Islam, not its reality.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to a subtle theological objection. If Islam is defined by inward submission (idh'aan), how can a hadith list outward actions as its components? The answer lies in distinguishing between a thing and its signs. Sanusi, drawing on al-Taftazani, shows that the Prophet was pointing to the markers of Islam, not its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the answer begins with 'So the answer:' — it directly addresses an unstated objection about the hadith.
The objection is not fully quoted but inferred from the context.
2
Identify the key distinction
The core move is distinguishing between Islam itself and its signs (علامات). The Prophet meant the signs that indicate Islam, not the inner reality.
This align with the technical definition of Islam as idh'aan (submission).
3
Examine the supporting hadith
Sanusi cites another hadith where the Prophet interprets 'iman' by listing the five pillars. This shows the same pattern: explaining a concept by its outward indicators.
The hadith emphasizes that faith is tasdiq (affirmation) and idh'aan (submission), not just actions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Prophet's words are consistent with the true meaning of Islam. Sanusi attributes this interpretation to al-Taftazani, grounding it in scholarly authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواب : أنَّ مراده عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام بالإسلام علاماته الدَّالَّة عليه، كما قال عليه الصّلاة والسّلام لوفد قدموا عليه: «أتدرون ما الإيمان بالله تعالى وحده؟»، فقالوا : الله ورسوله أعلم، فقال: «شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله ، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة، وصيام رمضان، وأن تُعطوا من المَغْنم الخمس» (۲)، فقد فَسَّر الإيمان بعلاماته لظهور أنَّ الإيمان ليس ما ذُكر بل التصديق والإذعان، قاله التفتازاني (۳).
So the answer: the Prophet, peace be upon him, meant by Islam its signs that indicate it, as the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to a delegation that came to him: 'Do you know what faith in Allah alone is?' They said: Allah and His Messenger know. He said: 'The testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, establishing prayer, giving zakat, fasting Ramadan, and giving one-fifth of the spoils.' So he interpreted faith by its signs because it is evident that faith is not what was mentioned but rather affirmation and submission. Al-Taftazani said this.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objection likely questioned a hadith where Islam was defined by outward actions. The answer explains that the Prophet meant the signs (علامات) that indicate Islam, not Islam itself. The author then cites a hadith where the Prophet defined iman (faith) by the five pillars, but notes that this is an explanation by signs, because the reality of iman is inward tasdiq (affirmation) and idh'aan (submission). This interpretation is attributed to al-Taftazani.

Key Terms

إيمان
علامات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Islam is merely the outward actions listed in the hadith, such as prayer and zakat.
Correction: The Prophet meant those actions as signs (علامات) that indicate the inner reality of Islam, which is submission (إذعان) to Allah. The true essence of faith is affirmation (تصديق) and submission, not just the external deeds.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what did the Prophet mean by 'Islam' in the hadith?
A) The outward actions themselves
B) The signs that indicate Islam
C) The inner reality of Islam
D) Both actions and belief

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's mention of outward actions in defining Islam refers to signs that indicate inner submission, not the essence of faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
This lesson teaches that faith is affirmation and submission, not just actions. Have you ever deceived yourself into thinking that your Islamic practices are sufficient while your heart remains indifferent? What specific step will you take today to nurture true affirmation and submission in your heart?

Reconciling Maturidi and Ash'ari on Iman and Islam
The author reconciles Maturidi and Ash'ari views on iman and islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced relationship between iman and islam as presented in the reconciliation of Maturidi and Ash'ari views.

Core Idea
Iman and Islam are distinct in concept but inseparable in referent and law.

This passage addresses a classic theological debate: Are iman (faith) and islam (submission) synonymous? The author presents a reconciliation between Maturidi and Ash'ari positions, explaining that the disagreement is about the definition of islam. Understanding this nuance is crucial for grasping Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the argument's flow.
The passage uses a compressed style; take your time.
2
Identify the two definitions of islam
The author presents two interpretations of islam: outward compliance (inqiyad zahiri) vs inner submission (idhan). Note how each relates to iman.
These two definitions are key to the reconciliation.
3
Grasp the proof from the Prophet's statement
The author argues that the Prophet interpreted islam as action (amal), which differs from affirmation (tasdiq). So if islam is action, it cannot be synonymous with iman.
This proof is from hadith; you may recall it.
4
Understand the final verdict
The correct view: iman and islam are different in concept, identical in referent (maṣdaq), and legally inseparable. This is the balance between full synonymy and full separation.
The terms 'different in concept' and 'identical in referent' are technical.
5
Reflect on the criticism of the reconciliation
The author criticizes the earlier attempt at reconciliation as unnecessary contrivance (takalluf). Why does he say that? What does this teach about theological methodology?
This shows the author's preference for a simpler, more direct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جمع الله بين قولي الماتريدية والأشاعرة بالتّرادف وعدمه بأنَّهما خلاف في حال، فإن مفهوم الإسلام: - إن فُسِّر بالانقياد الظاهري، بمعنى امتثال الأوامر والنواهي والعمل بمقتضى تلك الأحكام من غير ملاحظة الإذعان والتسليم القلبي كان مخالفاً لمفهوم الإيمان. وبيان ذلك : أنَّ النَّبِيَّ الله فسَّر الإسلام بالعمل، ومن المعلوم أنَّ العمل غير التصديق، فكيف يقال بترادفهما؟ والحقُّ : أنَّهما مختلفان ،مفهوماً، متّحدان ما صدقا، متلازمان شرعاً، فقوله : (وقد جمع الله ... إلخ تكلف لا داعي إليه . انتهى . وقوله : (من غير ملاحظة (الإذعان يعني في مفهومه، فلا ينافي أنَّه لا بدَّ من ملاحظة البناء عليه ليتأتّى التّلازم.
And Allah has reconciled between the statements of the Maturidis and the Ash'aris regarding synonymy and non-synonymy, in that they are a difference in circumstance. For the concept of islam: if interpreted as outward compliance, meaning fulfilling commands and prohibitions and acting according to those rulings without regard to inner submission and acceptance, it would be different from the concept of iman. Explanation of that: the Prophet interpreted islam as action, and it is known that action is different from affirmation, so how can they be said to be synonymous? The truth is that they are different in concept, identical in referent, and legally inseparable. So his statement: 'Allah has reconciled... etc.' is a contrivance that is not needed. End. Also his statement: 'without observing submission' means in its concept, so it does not contradict that one must consider building upon it for the legal inseparability to be achieved.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal reconciliation and correction. It addresses the Maturidi and Ash'ari difference about whether iman and islam are synonymous. The author explains that the difference is actually about the concept: if islam is defined as outward compliance (inqiyad zahiri) without inner submission, it is different from iman. He supports this by noting that the Prophet interpreted islam as action (amal), which is distinct from affirmation (tasdiq). The correct view is that they are conceptually different (mukhtalifan mafhuman) but identical in referent (muttaḥidan maṣdaqan) and legally inseparable (mutalaziman shar'an). The author criticizes the attempt at reconciliation as unnecessary contrivance (takalluf). Then a follow-up comment clarifies that the phrase 'without observing submission' refers to the concept, not that submission is absent from the person.

Key Terms

أشاعرة

مفهوم
انقياد
إذعان
تلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That iman and islam are either fully synonymous or completely separate with no connection.
Correction: The correct view is that they are different in concept (one is inner affirmation, the other is outward compliance), identical in referent (both point to the same person), and legally inseparable (one cannot exist without the other in practice).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if islam is interpreted as outward compliance, how does it relate to iman?
A) It is the same
B) It is different
C) It is contradictory
D) It is legally inseparable

Key Takeaway
Iman and Islam are distinct in definition but unified in reality and inseparable in law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own practice, do you see a disconnect between your outward actions and your inner conviction? Which one do you prioritize, and how does this lesson push you to integrate both more deeply?

?
The author calls a prior reconciliation attempt 'a contrivance not needed.' Are there areas in your faith where you overcomplicate things instead of accepting the straightforward truth? What would it mean for you to embrace the simple truth of this lesson in your daily life?

The Meaning of the Word of Islam
Sanusi defines the 'word of Islam' and analyzes its grammatical construction and meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the two testimonies (shahada) are called the 'word of Islam' and the linguistic relationship between the sign and the signified.

Core Idea
The shahada is the sign that points to Islam, and it is called a word because it conveys a single meaning.

Sanusi begins by defining the key term 'word of Islam,' explaining that it refers to the shahada. He then delves into the linguistic nature of this phrase, analyzing whether the addition (idafa) is one of sign to signified, cause to effect, or part to whole. This passage clarifies that the shahada is not just a set of words but a single meaningful unit that signifies the essence of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the passage and locate what is included in the meaning of 'word of Islam'. Note that it is the two testimonies.
The Arabic phrase كلمة الإسلام is the focus.
2
Analyze the idafa
The author discusses three possible interpretations of the grammatical addition (idafa): sign-signified, cause-effect, or part-whole. Consider each and why the first is preferred.
The text says the sign-signified interpretation is 'not determined,' meaning other possibilities exist.
3
Understand why it is called a 'word'
The passage explains that it is called a word because it indicates one meaning (Islam). Reflect on the distinction between word (kalima) and speech (kalam).
The Quranic verse and Ibn Malik's line support that a word can refer to speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينطوي أي : يندرج في معنى كلمة الإسلام أي: الدَّالَّة على الإسلام، وهي لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله فإضافتها للإسلام من إضافة الدّالّ للمدلول، سُمِّيت كلمة لدلالتها على معنى واحد، وهو الإسلام. قوله : (من إضافة الدّالّ للمدلول) : غير متعيّن، بل يصحُ أن يكون من إضافة السبب للمسبب، أو من إضافة الجزء للكلِّ، بناء على تكلُّف أنَّ الإسلام اسم للعمل . قوله : (لدلالتها على معنى واحد) : أي : فسميت باسم مدلولها، وإلَّا فهي كلام، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهَا كَلِمَةٌ هُوَ قَائِلُهَا﴾ (١) ، قال ابن مالك(۲) : وكلمةٌ بها كلام قد يُقَمْ
And it includes, i.e., is subsumed under the meaning of the word of Islam, i.e., that which indicates Islam, and it is 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' So its being added to Islam is an addition of the sign to the signified. It is called a word because it indicates one meaning, which is Islam. His statement: '(From the addition of the sign to the signified)' is not determined; rather it is permissible that it be from the addition of the cause to the effect, or from the addition of the part to the whole, based on the contrivance that Islam is a name for action. His statement: '(Because it indicates one meaning)' means: so it is named by the name of its signified; otherwise it is speech. Among such is His statement: 'Nay, it is a word that he speaks' (23:100). Ibn Malik said: 'And a word by which speech may be intended.'
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the 'word of Islam' (kalimat al-islam). It states that the two testimonies (لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله) are subsumed under its meaning. The phrase 'word of Islam' is an idafa of the sign (dal) to the signified (madlul). The author then notes alternative interpretations: it could be cause-effect or part-whole, if one adopts the view that islam is a name for action. A further comment explains why it is called a 'word' (kalima): because it indicates a single meaning (Islam), so it is named after its signified. Otherwise it would be considered speech (kalam). A Quranic verse and a line from Ibn Malik's Alfiyya are cited to support the usage of 'word' for speech.

Key Terms
كلمة الإسلام
شهادة
دال
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the shahada is merely a word, like any other utterance, without special significance.
Correction: The shahada is called a word because it signifies the single meaning of Islam, not because it is just speech. It is named after its signified.

Checkpoint
What single meaning does the word of Islam (kalima) indicate?
A) Tawhid
B) Islam
C) Iman
D) The Prophet

Key Takeaway
The shahada is called the word of Islam because it is the sign that points directly to the single meaning of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you consciously reflect that it is a sign pointing to the entire meaning of Islam, or do you treat it as a routine phrase? How can you change that starting today?
?
If the shahada is called a word because it indicates one single meaning (Islam), what does that imply about your own life's focus? Are you giving your full allegiance to that one meaning, or are you distracted by other loyalties?
?
The author suggests multiple interpretations of the grammatical relationship between 'word' and 'Islam'. Does your understanding of the shahada allow for this richness, or have you limited it to just one perspective? How might embracing these other interpretations deepen your faith and practice?

The Meaning of the Word of Islam and Its Components
Al-Sawi defines the Word of Islam and its inclusion of the two testimonies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the 'Word of Islam' is and how it includes the two testimonies.

Core Idea
The Word of Islam is the two testimonies, which indicate the entire religion.

Al-Sawi begins by clarifying the term 'Word of Islam'. This phrase refers to the two testimonies of faith, which are the foundation of Islam. In this passage, he explains how the two testimonies are subsumed within this word and how the attribution works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Focus on the definition of 'Word of Islam'.
The passage uses a concise style common in scholarly commentaries.
2
Identify the components
Note that the Word of Islam is explicitly stated as 'La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah'. This is the complete declaration of faith.
3
Understand the inclusion
The phrase 'yinṭawī' means the two testimonies are included in the meaning of the Word of Islam. They are not separate but part of it.
4
Grasp the attribution
The attribution (idafa) of the word to Islam is described as 'the attribution of the indicator to the indicated'. This means the word is the sign that points to Islam.
This is the primary interpretation; alternatives will be discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الشهادتين
(وينطوي ) أي : يندرج (في) معنى (كِلْمَة الإسلام) أي: الدَّالَّة على الإسلام، وهي (لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله) فإضافتها للإسلام من إضافة الدّالّ للمدلول، سُمِّيت كلمة لدلالتها على معنى واحد، وهو الإسلام.

قوله : (من إضافة الدَّالّ للمدلول) : غير متعيّن، بل يصحُ أن يكون من إضافة السبب للمسبب، أو من إضافة الجزء للكلِّ، بناء على تكلُّف أنَّ الإسلام اسم للعمل .

قوله : (لدلالتها على معنى (واحد) : أي : فسميت باسم مدلولها، وإلَّا فهي كلام، ومنه قوله تعالى: (كَلَّا إِنَّهَا كَلِمَةٌ هُوَ قَابِلُها) (١) ،

قال ابن مالك(۲) :
(١) سورة المؤمنون: (۱۰۰).
(٢) ألفية ابن مالك ( ص ) .
Explanation of the meaning of the two testimonies: (It is included) i.e., it is subsumed in the meaning of the Word of Islam, i.e., that which indicates Islam, and it is (La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah). So its attribution to Islam is the attribution of the indicator to the indicated. It is called a word because it indicates one meaning, and that is Islam. His saying: (the attribution of the indicator to the indicated) is not certain; rather it is valid that it be the attribution of cause to effect, or the attribution of part to whole, based on the strained assumption that Islam is a name for action. His saying: (because it indicates one meaning) i.e., so it was named by the name of its indicated meaning; otherwise it is speech, and from that is the saying of the Most High: (Nay! It is a word that he utters) (1). Ibn Malik said: (2) (Quran 23:100; Alfiyyah Ibn Malik).
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines the 'word of Islam' (kalimat al-Islam) and explains its components. The author begins by stating that the two testimonies (al-shahadatayn) are subsumed in the meaning of the word of Islam. This word is specifically 'La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah', which indicates Islam itself. The grammatical relation (idafa) between the word and Islam is discussed: it can be the relation of indicant to indicated (dal to madlul), or alternatively cause to effect, or part to whole if Islam is considered a name for action. Then the reason it is called a 'word' (kalima) is given: because it signifies a single meaning, which is Islam. The author supports this by quoting the Quranic verse 'Nay! It is a word that he utters' (23:100) and referencing Ibn Malik's Alfiyyah. The passage thus establishes the foundational definition of the two testimonies as the core of Islamic belief.

Key Terms
كلمة الإسلام
الشهادتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'Word of Islam' refers only to the verbal utterance without understanding its comprehensive meaning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Word of Islam is a single word indicating the entire meaning of Islam, encompassing both belief and action.

Checkpoint
What is the 'Word of Islam' (kalimat al-Islam) according to this passage?
A) Only 'La ilaha illallah'
B) 'La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah'
C) The Quran
D) The act of prayer

Key Takeaway
The Word of Islam is the two testimonies, and its attribution to Islam is that of an indicator to the indicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the two testimonies are one word indicating the entire religion of Islam, then your entire life must be a single, unified response to that word. Are there areas of your life where you act as if you can accept one testimony without the other — where your deeds contradict your declaration? What would it take to bring every action under that one meaning?
?
The passage says the Word of Islam is called a 'word' because it indicates one meaning: Islam. But you know that your own understanding and practice of Islam is often fragmented — different parts of your life seem to follow different logics. How can you reconnect the scattered pieces of your religious life so that your whole being becomes that single 'indicated' meaning?
?
You have heard the two testimonies countless times. But have you truly seen them as one compact word that demands your entire submission? If the Word of Islam is the indicator of everything in the religion, then it leaves no room for selective obedience. What is one thing you have been avoiding in your commitment that this passage now exposes as a contradiction?

The Grammatical Relation and Why It Is Called a Word
Al-Sawi examines the idafa of 'Word of Islam' and the reason for calling it a word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the possible grammatical interpretations of the phrase 'Word of Islam' and the reason it is called a 'word'.

Core Idea
The attribution of 'word' to 'Islam' can be understood as indicator-indicated, cause-effect, or part-whole.

Al-Sawi continues by examining the grammatical relation (idafa) in 'Word of Islam' more deeply. He notes that the interpretation is not fixed and offers three possibilities. He also explains why the phrase is called a 'word' (kalima), citing Quran and Ibn Malik.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation, focusing on the different possibilities for the idafa.
The passage begins with a critique of the previous interpretation.
2
Notice the flexibility
Al-Sawi states that the attribution is 'not certain' (ghayr muta'ayyin), meaning it can be understood in multiple ways.
This shows scholarly precision and awareness of linguistic ambiguity.
3
List the three interpretations
The three possibilities are: (1) indicator to indicated, (2) cause to effect, (3) part to whole (if Islam is a name for action).
The third interpretation is based on a 'takalluf' (strained) assumption.
4
Understand why it is called a word
It is called a 'word' because it indicates a single meaning (Islam). Otherwise, it would be speech (kalam). The Quranic verse supports this usage.
The reference to Ibn Malik indicates this is a standard linguistic principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الشهادتين
(وينطوي ) أي : يندرج (في) معنى (كِلْمَة الإسلام) أي: الدَّالَّة على الإسلام، وهي (لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله) فإضافتها للإسلام من إضافة الدّالّ للمدلول، سُمِّيت كلمة لدلالتها على معنى واحد، وهو الإسلام.

قوله : (من إضافة الدَّالّ للمدلول) : غير متعيّن، بل يصحُ أن يكون من إضافة السبب للمسبب، أو من إضافة الجزء للكلِّ، بناء على تكلُّف أنَّ الإسلام اسم للعمل .

قوله : (لدلالتها على معنى (واحد) : أي : فسميت باسم مدلولها، وإلَّا فهي كلام، ومنه قوله تعالى: (كَلَّا إِنَّهَا كَلِمَةٌ هُوَ قَابِلُها) (١) ،

قال ابن مالك(۲) :
(١) سورة المؤمنون: (۱۰۰).
(٢) ألفية ابن مالك ( ص ) .
Explanation of the meaning of the two testimonies: (It is included) i.e., it is subsumed in the meaning of the Word of Islam, i.e., that which indicates Islam, and it is (La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah). So its attribution to Islam is the attribution of the indicator to the indicated. It is called a word because it indicates one meaning, and that is Islam. His saying: (the attribution of the indicator to the indicated) is not certain; rather it is valid that it be the attribution of cause to effect, or the attribution of part to whole, based on the strained assumption that Islam is a name for action. His saying: (because it indicates one meaning) i.e., so it was named by the name of its indicated meaning; otherwise it is speech, and from that is the saying of the Most High: (Nay! It is a word that he utters) (1). Ibn Malik said: (2) (Quran 23:100; Alfiyyah Ibn Malik).

This word is specifically 'La ilaha illallah Muhammad Rasul Allah', which indicates Islam itself. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that 'Word of Islam' can be understood as cause to effect or part to whole. Which interpretation best describes your own experience? Is your declaration of faith the cause that brought you into Islam, or is it a part of a larger whole of actions? How does your answer challenge you to live more consistently?
?
If a phrase is called a 'word' because it indicates one single meaning, what is the one meaning that your life—your words and actions—collectively indicate? Are all parts of your life aligned with that one meaning, or are there contradictions?
?
The verse quoted (Quran 23:100) calls the dying man's utterance a 'kalima'. Your own last utterance might be a 'kalima' that defines your end. Are you practicing saying the true 'Word of Islam' now, so that it becomes natural at the moment of death?
